   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter25</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XXV - CONCLUSION</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>'WELL, Agnes, you must not take such long walks again before



<P>breakfast,' said my mother, observing that I drank an extra cup of



<P>coffee and ate nothing - pleading the heat of the weather, and the



<P>fatigue of my long walk as an excuse.&nbsp; I certainly did feel



<P>feverish and tired too.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You always do things by extremes:&nbsp; now, if you had taken a SHORT



<P>walk every morning, and would continue to do so, it would do you



<P>good.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, mamma, I will.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But this is worse than lying in bed or bending over your books:



<P>you have quite put yourself into a fever.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I won't do it again,' said I.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I was racking my brains with thinking how to tell her about Mr.



<P>Weston, for she must know he was coming to-morrow.&nbsp; However, I



<P>waited till the breakfast things were removed, and I was more calm



<P>and cool; and then, having sat down to my drawing, I began - 'I met



<P>an old friend on the sands to-day, mamma.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'An old friend!&nbsp; Who could it be?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Two old friends, indeed.&nbsp; One was a dog;' and then I reminded

her



<P>of Snap, whose history I had recounted before, and related the



<P>incident of his sudden appearance and remarkable recognition; 'and



<P>the other,' continued I, 'was Mr. Weston, the curate of Horton.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Mr. Weston!&nbsp; I never heard of him before.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, you have:&nbsp; I've mentioned him several times, I believe:&nbsp;

but



<P>you don't remember.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I've heard you speak of Mr. Hatfield.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Mr. Hatfield was the rector, and Mr. Weston the curate:&nbsp; I used

to



<P>mention him sometimes in contradistinction to Mr. Hatfield, as



<P>being a more efficient clergyman.&nbsp; However, he was on the sands



<P>this morning with the dog - he had bought it, I suppose, from the



<P>rat-catcher; and he knew me as well as it did - probably through



<P>its means:&nbsp; and I had a little conversation with him, in the course



<P>of which, as he asked about our school, I was led to say something



<P>about you, and your good management; and he said he should like to



<P>know you, and asked if I would introduce him to you, if he should



<P>take the liberty of calling to-morrow; so I said I would.&nbsp; Was

I



<P>right?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Of course.&nbsp; What kind of a man is he?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'A very RESPECTABLE man, I think:&nbsp; but you will see him to-morrow.



<P>He is the new vicar of F-, and as he has only been there a few



<P>weeks, I suppose he has made no friends yet, and wants a little



<P>society.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The morrow came.&nbsp; What a fever of anxiety and expectation I was

in



<P>from breakfast till noon - at which time he made his appearance!



<P>Having introduced him to my mother, I took my work to the window,



<P>and sat down to await the result of the interview.&nbsp; They got on



<P>extremely well together - greatly to my satisfaction, for I had



<P>felt very anxious about what my mother would think of him.&nbsp; He

did



<P>not stay long that time:&nbsp; but when he rose to take leave, she said



<P>she should be happy to see him, whenever he might find it



<P>convenient to call again; and when he was gone, I was gratified by



<P>hearing her say, - 'Well!&nbsp; I think he's a very sensible man.&nbsp;

But



<P>why did you sit back there, Agnes,' she added, 'and talk so



<P>little?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Because you talked so well, mamma, I thought you required no



<P>assistance from me:&nbsp; and, besides, he was your visitor, not mine.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After that, he often called upon us - several times in the course



<P>of a week.&nbsp; He generally addressed most of his conversation to

my



<P>mother:&nbsp; and no wonder, for she could converse.&nbsp; I almost

envied



<P>the unfettered, vigorous fluency of her discourse, and the strong



<P>sense evinced by everything she said - and yet, I did not; for,



<P>though I occasionally regretted my own deficiencies for his sake,



<P>it gave me very great pleasure to sit and hear the two beings I



<P>loved and honoured above every one else in the world, discoursing



<P>together so amicably, so wisely, and so well.&nbsp; I was not always



<P>silent, however; nor was I at all neglected.&nbsp; I was quite as much



<P>noticed as I would wish to be:&nbsp; there was no lack of kind words

and



<P>kinder looks, no end of delicate attentions, too fine and subtle to



<P>be grasped by words, and therefore indescribable - but deeply felt



<P>at heart.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Ceremony was quickly dropped between us:&nbsp; Mr. Weston came as an



<P>expected guest, welcome at all times, and never deranging the



<P>economy of our household affairs.&nbsp; He even called me 'Agnes:' the



<P>name had been timidly spoken at first, but, finding it gave no



<P>offence in any quarter, he seemed greatly to prefer that



<P>appellation to 'Miss Grey;' and so did I.&nbsp; How tedious and gloomy



<P>were those days in which he did not come!&nbsp; And yet not miserable;



<P>for I had still the remembrance of the last visit and the hope of



<P>the next to cheer me.&nbsp; But when two or three days passed without

my



<P>seeing him, I certainly felt very anxious - absurdly, unreasonably



<P>so; for, of course, he had his own business and the affairs of his



<P>parish to attend to.&nbsp; And I dreaded the close of the holidays,

when



<P>MY business also would begin, and I should be sometimes unable to



<P>see him, and sometimes - when my mother was in the schoolroom -



<P>obliged to be with him alone:&nbsp; a position I did not at all desire,



<P>in the house; though to meet him out of doors, and walk beside him,



<P>had proved by no means disagreeable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One evening, however, in the last week of the vacation, he arrived



<P>- unexpectedly:&nbsp; for a heavy and protracted thunder-shower during



<P>the afternoon had almost destroyed my hopes of seeing him that day;



<P>but now the storm was over, and the sun was shining brightly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'A beautiful evening, Mrs. Grey!' said he, as he entered.&nbsp; 'Agnes,



<P>I want you to take a walk with me to - ' (he named a certain part



<P>of the coast - a bold hill on the land side, and towards the sea a



<P>steep precipice, from the summit of which a glorious view is to be



<P>had).&nbsp; 'The rain has laid the dust, and cooled and cleared the

air,



<P>and the prospect will be magnificent.&nbsp; Will you come?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Can I go, mamma?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes; to be sure.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I went to get ready, and was down again in a few minutes; though,



<P>of course, I took a little more pains with my attire than if I had



<P>merely been going out on some shopping expedition alone.&nbsp; The



<P>thunder-shower had certainly had a most beneficial effect upon the



<P>weather, and the evening was most delightful.&nbsp; Mr. Weston would



<P>have me to take his arm; he said little during our passage through



<P>the crowded streets, but walked very fast, and appeared grave and



<P>abstracted.&nbsp; I wondered what was the matter, and felt an indefinite



<P>dread that something unpleasant was on his mind; and vague



<P>surmises, concerning what it might be, troubled me not a little,



<P>and made me grave and silent enough.&nbsp; But these fantasies vanished



<P>upon reaching the quiet outskirts of the town; for as soon as we



<P>came within sight of the venerable old church, and the - hill, with



<P>the deep blue beyond it, I found my companion was cheerful enough.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I'm afraid I've been walking too fast for you, Agnes,' said he:



<P>'in my impatience to be rid of the town, I forgot to consult your



<P>convenience; but now we'll walk as slowly as you please.&nbsp; I see,

by



<P>those light clouds in the west, there will be a brilliant sunset,



<P>and we shall be in time to witness its effect upon the sea, at the



<P>most moderate rate of progression.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When we had got about half-way up the hill, we fell into silence



<P>again; which, as usual, he was the first to break.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'My house is desolate yet, Miss Grey,' he smilingly observed, 'and



<P>I am acquainted now with all the ladies in my parish, and several



<P>in this town too; and many others I know by sight and by report;



<P>but not one of them will suit me for a companion; in fact, there is



<P>only one person in the world that will:&nbsp; and that is yourself;

and



<P>I want to know your decision?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Are you in earnest, Mr. Weston?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'In earnest!&nbsp; How could you think I should jest on such a subject?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He laid his hand on mine, that rested on his arm:&nbsp; he must have



<P>felt it tremble - but it was no great matter now.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I hope I have not been too precipitate,' he said, in a serious



<P>tone.&nbsp; 'You must have known that it was not my way to flatter and



<P>talk soft nonsense, or even to speak the admiration that I felt;



<P>and that a single word or glance of mine meant more than the honied



<P>phrases and fervent protestations of most other men.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I said something about not liking to leave my mother, and doing



<P>nothing without her consent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I settled everything with Mrs. Grey, while you were putting on



<P>your bonnet,' replied he.&nbsp; 'She said I might have her consent,

if I



<P>could obtain yours; and I asked her, in case I should be so happy,



<P>to come and live with us - for I was sure you would like it better.



<P>But she refused, saying she could now afford to employ an



<P>assistant, and would continue the school till she could purchase an



<P>annuity sufficient to maintain her in comfortable lodgings; and,



<P>meantime, she would spend her vacations alternately with us and



<P>your sister, and should be quite contented if you were happy.&nbsp;

And



<P>so now I have overruled your objections on her account.&nbsp; Have you



<P>any other?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No - none.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You love me then?' said be, fervently pressing my hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here I pause.&nbsp; My Diary, from which I have compiled these pages,



<P>goes but little further.&nbsp; I could go on for years, but I will



<P>content myself with adding, that I shall never forget that glorious



<P>summer evening, and always remember with delight that steep hill,



<P>and the edge of the precipice where we stood together, watching the



<P>splendid sunset mirrored in the restless world of waters at our



<P>feet - with hearts filled with gratitude to heaven, and happiness,



<P>and love - almost too full for speech.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A few weeks after that, when my mother had supplied herself with an



<P>assistant, I became the wife of Edward Weston; and never have found



<P>cause to repent it, and am certain that I never shall.&nbsp; We have

had



<P>trials, and we know that we must have them again; but we bear them



<P>well together, and endeavour to fortify ourselves and each other



<P>against the final separation - that greatest of all afflictions to



<P>the survivor.&nbsp; But, if we keep in mind the glorious heaven beyond,



<P>where both may meet again, and sin and sorrow are unknown, surely



<P>that too may be borne; and, meantime, we endeavour to live to the



<P>glory of Him who has scattered so many blessings in our path.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Edward, by his strenuous exertions, has worked surprising reforms



<P>in his parish, and is esteemed and loved by its inhabitants - as he



<P>deserves; for whatever his faults may be as a man (and no one is



<P>entirely without), I defy anybody to blame him as a pastor, a



<P>husband, or a father.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Our children, Edward, Agnes, and little Mary, promise well; their



<P>education, for the time being, is chiefly committed to me; and they



<P>shall want no good thing that a mother's care can give.&nbsp; Our modest



<P>income is amply sufficient for our requirements:&nbsp; and by practising



<P>the economy we learnt in harder times, and never attempting to



<P>imitate our richer neighbours, we manage not only to enjoy comfort



<P>and contentment ourselves, but to have every year something to lay



<P>by for our children, and something to give to those who need it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And now I think I have said sufficient.

<BR>&nbsp;

<UL>

<UL>

<UL>

<UL><FONT SIZE=+1>The End</FONT></UL>

</UL>

</UL>

</UL>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XIX - THE LETTER</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>MY father's mortal remains had been consigned to the tomb; and we,



<P>with sad faces and sombre garments, sat lingering over the frugal



<P>breakfast-table, revolving plans for our future life.&nbsp; My mother's



<P>strong mind had not given way beneath even this affliction:&nbsp; her



<P>spirit, though crushed, was not broken.&nbsp; Mary's wish was that I



<P>should go back to Horton Lodge, and that our mother should come and



<P>live with her and Mr. Richardson at the vicarage:&nbsp; she affirmed



<P>that he wished it no less than herself, and that such an



<P>arrangement could not fail to benefit all parties; for my mother's



<P>society and experience would be of inestimable value to them, and



<P>they would do all they could to make her happy.&nbsp; But no arguments



<P>or entreaties could prevail:&nbsp; my mother was determined not to go.



<P>Not that she questioned, for a moment, the kind wishes and



<P>intentions of her daughter; but she affirmed that so long as God



<P>spared her health and strength, she would make use of them to earn



<P>her own livelihood, and be chargeable to no one; whether her



<P>dependence would be felt as a burden or not.&nbsp; If she could afford



<P>to reside as a lodger in - vicarage, she would choose that house



<P>before all others as the place of her abode; but not being so



<P>circumstanced, she would never come under its roof, except as an



<P>occasional visitor:&nbsp; unless sickness or calamity should render

her



<P>assistance really needful, or until age or infirmity made her



<P>incapable of maintaining herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, Mary,' said she, 'if Richardson and you have anything to



<P>spare, you must lay it aside for your family; and Agnes and I must



<P>gather honey for ourselves.&nbsp; Thanks to my having had daughters

to



<P>educate, I have not forgotten my accomplishments.&nbsp; God willing,

I



<P>will check this vain repining,' she said, while the tears coursed



<P>one another down her cheeks in spite of her efforts; but she wiped



<P>them away, and resolutely shaking back her head, continued, 'I will



<P>exert myself, and look out for a small house, commodiously situated



<P>in some populous but healthy district, where we will take a few



<P>young ladies to board and educate - if we can get them - and as



<P>many day pupils as will come, or as we can manage to instruct.



<P>Your father's relations and old friends will be able to send us



<P>some pupils, or to assist us with their recommendations, no doubt:



<P>I shall not apply to my own.&nbsp; What say you to it, Agnes? will you



<P>be willing to leave your present situation and try?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Quite willing, mamma; and the money I have saved will do to



<P>furnish the house.&nbsp; It shall be taken from the bank directly.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'When it is wanted:&nbsp; we must get the house, and settle on



<P>preliminaries first.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mary offered to lend the little she possessed; but my mother



<P>declined it, saying that we must begin on an economical plan; and



<P>she hoped that the whole or part of mine, added to what we could



<P>get by the sale of the furniture, and what little our dear papa had



<P>contrived to lay aside for her since the debts were paid, would be



<P>sufficient to last us till Christmas; when, it was hoped, something



<P>would accrue from our united labours.&nbsp; It was finally settled that



<P>this should be our plan; and that inquiries and preparations should



<P>immediately be set on foot; and while my mother busied herself with



<P>these, I should return to Horton Lodge at the close of my four



<P>weeks' vacation, and give notice for my final departure when things



<P>were in train for the speedy commencement of our school.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>We were discussing these affairs on the morning I have mentioned,



<P>about a fortnight after my father's death, when a letter was



<P>brought in for my mother, on beholding which the colour mounted to



<P>her face - lately pale enough with anxious watchings and excessive



<P>sorrow.&nbsp; 'From my father!' murmured she, as she hastily tore off



<P>the cover.&nbsp; It was many years since she had heard from any of her



<P>own relations before.&nbsp; Naturally wondering what the letter might



<P>contain, I watched her countenance while she read it, and was



<P>somewhat surprised to see her bite her lip and knit her brows as if



<P>in anger.&nbsp; When she had done, she somewhat irreverently cast it

on



<P>the table, saying with a scornful smile, - 'Your grandpapa has been



<P>so kind as to write to me.&nbsp; He says he has no doubt I have long



<P>repented of my "unfortunate marriage," and if I will only



<P>acknowledge this, and confess I was wrong in neglecting his advice,



<P>and that I have justly suffered for it, he will make a lady of me



<P>once again - if that be possible after my long degradation - and



<P>remember my girls in his will.&nbsp; Get my desk, Agnes, and send these



<P>things away:&nbsp; I will answer the letter directly.&nbsp; But first,

as I



<P>may be depriving you both of a legacy, it is just that I should



<P>tell you what I mean to say.&nbsp; I shall say that he is mistaken in



<P>supposing that I can regret the birth of my daughters (who have



<P>been the pride of my life, and are likely to be the comfort of my



<P>old age), or the thirty years I have passed in the company of my



<P>best and dearest friend; - that, had our misfortunes been three



<P>times as great as they were (unless they had been of my bringing



<P>on), I should still the more rejoice to have shared them with your



<P>father, and administered what consolation I was able; and, had his



<P>sufferings in illness been ten times what they wore, I could not



<P>regret having watched over and laboured to relieve them; - that, if



<P>he had married a richer wife, misfortunes and trials would no doubt



<P>have come upon him still; while I am egotist enough to imagine that



<P>no other woman could have cheered him through them so well:&nbsp; not



<P>that I am superior to the rest, but I was made for him, and he for



<P>me; and I can no more repent the hours, days, years of happiness we



<P>have spent together, and which neither could have had without the



<P>other, than I can the privilege of having been his nurse in



<P>sickness, and his comfort in affliction.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Will this do, children? - or shall I say we are all very sorry for



<P>what has happened during the last thirty years, and my daughters



<P>wish they had never been born; but since they have had that



<P>misfortune, they will be thankful for any trifle their grandpapa



<P>will be kind enough to bestow?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Of course, we both applauded our mother's resolution; Mary cleared



<P>away the breakfast things; I brought the desk; the letter was



<P>quickly written and despatched; and, from that day, we heard no



<P>more of our grandfather, till we saw his death announced in the



<P>newspaper a considerable time after - all his worldly possessions,



<P>of course, being left to our wealthy unknown cousins.



<P>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XVIII - MIRTH AND MOURNING</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE 1st of June arrived at last:&nbsp; and Rosalie Murray was transmuted



<P>into Lady Ashby.&nbsp; Most splendidly beautiful she looked in her



<P>bridal costume.&nbsp; Upon her return from church, after the ceremony,



<P>she came flying into the schoolroom, flushed with excitement, and



<P>laughing, half in mirth, and half in reckless desperation, as it



<P>seemed to me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Now, Miss Grey, I'm Lady Ashby!' she exclaimed.&nbsp; 'It's done, my



<P>fate is sealed:&nbsp; there's no drawing back now.&nbsp; I'm come to

receive



<P>your congratulations and bid you good-by; and then I'm off for



<P>Paris, Rome, Naples, Switzerland, London - oh, dear! what a deal I



<P>shall see and hear before I come back again.&nbsp; But don't forget

me:



<P>I shan't forget you, though I've been a naughty girl.&nbsp; Come, why



<P>don't you congratulate me?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I cannot congratulate you,' I replied, 'till I know whether this



<P>change is really for the better:&nbsp; but I sincerely hope it is; and

I



<P>wish you true happiness and the best of blessings.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, good-by, the carriage is waiting, and they're calling me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She gave me a hasty kiss, and was hurrying away; but, suddenly



<P>returning, embraced me with more affection than I thought her



<P>capable of evincing, and departed with tears in her eyes.&nbsp; Poor



<P>girl!&nbsp; I really loved her then; and forgave her from my heart all



<P>the injury she had done me - and others also:&nbsp; she had not half



<P>known it, I was sure; and I prayed God to pardon her too.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>During the remainder of that day of festal sadness, I was left to



<P>my own devices.&nbsp; Being too much unhinged for any steady occupation,



<P>I wandered about with a book in my hand for several hours, more



<P>thinking than reading, for I had many things to think about.&nbsp; In



<P>the evening, I made use of my liberty to go and see my old friend



<P>Nancy once again; to apologize for my long absence (which must have



<P>seemed so neglectful and unkind) by telling her how busy I had



<P>been; and to talk, or read, or work for her, whichever might be



<P>most acceptable, and also, of course, to tell her the news of this



<P>important day:&nbsp; and perhaps to obtain a little information from

her



<P>in return, respecting Mr. Weston's expected departure.&nbsp; But of

this



<P>she seemed to know nothing, and I hoped, as she did, that it was



<P>all a false report.&nbsp; She was very glad to see me; but, happily,

her



<P>eyes were now so nearly well that she was almost independent of my



<P>services.&nbsp; She was deeply interested in the wedding; but while

I



<P>amused her with the details of the festive day, the splendours of



<P>the bridal party and of the bride herself, she often sighed and



<P>shook her head, and wished good might come of it; she seemed, like



<P>me, to regard it rather as a theme for sorrow than rejoicing.&nbsp;

I



<P>sat a long time talking to her about that and other things - but no



<P>one came.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Shall I confess that I sometimes looked towards the door with a



<P>half-expectant wish to see it open and give entrance to Mr. Weston,



<P>as had happened once before? and that, returning through the lanes



<P>and fields, I often paused to look round me, and walked more slowly



<P>than was at all necessary - for, though a fine evening, it was not



<P>a hot one - and, finally, felt a sense of emptiness and



<P>disappointment at having reached the house without meeting or even



<P>catching a distant glimpse of any one, except a few labourers



<P>returning from their work?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sunday, however, was approaching:&nbsp; I should see him then:&nbsp;

for now



<P>that Miss Murray was gone, I could have my old corner again.&nbsp; I



<P>should see him, and by look, speech, and manner, I might judge



<P>whether the circumstance of her marriage had very much afflicted



<P>him.&nbsp; Happily I could perceive no shadow of a difference:&nbsp;

he wore



<P>the same aspect as he had worn two months ago - voice, look,



<P>manner, all alike unchanged:&nbsp; there was the same keen-sighted,



<P>unclouded truthfulness in his discourse, the same forcible



<P>clearness in his style, the same earnest simplicity in all he said



<P>and did, that made itself, not marked by the eye and ear, but felt



<P>upon the hearts of his audience.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I walked home with Miss Matilda; but HE DID NOT JOIN US.&nbsp; Matilda



<P>was now sadly at a loss for amusement, and wofully in want of a



<P>companion:&nbsp; her brothers at school, her sister married and gone,



<P>she too young to be admitted into society; for which, from



<P>Rosalie's example, she was in some degree beginning to acquire a



<P>taste - a taste at least for the company of certain classes of



<P>gentlemen; at this dull time of year - no hunting going on, no



<P>shooting even - for, though she might not join in that, it was



<P>SOMETHING to see her father or the gamekeeper go out with the dogs,



<P>and to talk with them on their return, about the different birds



<P>they had bagged.&nbsp; Now, also, she was denied the solace which the



<P>companionship of the coachman, grooms, horses, greyhounds, and



<P>pointers might have afforded; for her mother having,



<P>notwithstanding the disadvantages of a country life, so



<P>satisfactorily disposed of her elder daughter, the pride of her



<P>heart had begun seriously to turn her attention to the younger;



<P>and, being truly alarmed at the roughness of her manners, and



<P>thinking it high time to work a reform, had been roused at length



<P>to exert her authority, and prohibited entirely the yards, stables,



<P>kennels, and coachhouse.&nbsp; Of course, she was not implicitly obeyed;



<P>but, indulgent as she had hitherto been, when once her spirit was



<P>roused, her temper was not so gentle as she required that of her



<P>governesses to be, and her will was not to be thwarted with



<P>impunity.&nbsp; After many a scene of contention between mother and



<P>daughter, many a violent outbreak which I was ashamed to witness,



<P>in which the father's authority was often called in to confirm with



<P>oaths and threats the mother's slighted prohibitions - for even HE



<P>could see that 'Tilly, though she would have made a fine lad, was



<P>not quite what a young lady ought to be' - Matilda at length found



<P>that her easiest plan was to keep clear of the forbidden regions;



<P>unless she could now and then steal a visit without her watchful



<P>mother's knowledge.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Amid all this, let it not be imagined that I escaped without many a



<P>reprimand, and many an implied reproach, that lost none of its



<P>sting from not being openly worded; but rather wounded the more



<P>deeply, because, from that very reason, it seemed to preclude self-



<P>defence.&nbsp; Frequently, I was told to amuse Miss Matilda with other



<P>things, and to remind her of her mother's precepts and



<P>prohibitions.&nbsp; I did so to the best of my power:&nbsp; but she

would not



<P>be amused against her will, and could not against her taste; and



<P>though I went beyond mere reminding, such gentle remonstrances as I



<P>could use were utterly ineffectual.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'DEAR Miss Grey! it is the STRANGEST thing.&nbsp; I suppose you can't



<P>help it, if it's not in your nature - but I WONDER you can't win



<P>the confidence of that girl, and make your society at LEAST as



<P>agreeable to her as that of Robert or Joseph!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'They can talk the best about the things in which she is most



<P>interested,' I replied.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well! that is a strange confession, HOWEVER, to come from her



<P>GOVERNESS!&nbsp; Who is to form a young lady's tastes, I wonder, if

the



<P>governess doesn't do it?&nbsp; I have known governesses who have so



<P>completely identified themselves with the reputation of their young



<P>ladies for elegance and propriety in mind and manners, that they



<P>would blush to speak a word against them; and to hear the slightest



<P>blame imputed to their pupils was worse than to be censured in



<P>their own persons - and I really think it very natural, for my



<P>part.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Do you, ma'am?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, of course:&nbsp; the young lady's proficiency and elegance is

of



<P>more consequence to the governess than her own, as well as to the



<P>world.&nbsp; If she wishes to prosper in her vocation she must devote



<P>all her energies to her business:&nbsp; all her ideas and all her



<P>ambition will tend to the accomplishment of that one object.&nbsp; When



<P>we wish to decide upon the merits of a governess, we naturally look



<P>at the young ladies she professes to have educated, and judge



<P>accordingly.&nbsp; The JUDICIOUS governess knows this:&nbsp; she knows

that,



<P>while she lives in obscurity herself, her pupils' virtues and



<P>defects will be open to every eye; and that, unless she loses sight



<P>of herself in their cultivation, she need not hope for success.



<P>You see, Miss Grey, it is just the same as any other trade or



<P>profession:&nbsp; they that wish to prosper must devote themselves body



<P>and soul to their calling; and if they begin to yield to indolence



<P>or self-indulgence they are speedily distanced by wiser



<P>competitors:&nbsp; there is little to choose between a person that ruins



<P>her pupils by neglect, and one that corrupts them by her example.



<P>You will excuse my dropping these little hints:&nbsp; you know it is

all



<P>for your own good.&nbsp; Many ladies would speak to you much more



<P>strongly; and many would not trouble themselves to speak at all,



<P>but quietly look out for a substitute.&nbsp; That, of course, would

be



<P>the EASIEST plan:&nbsp; but I know the advantages of a place like this



<P>to a person in your situation; and I have no desire to part with



<P>you, as I am sure you would do very well if you will only think of



<P>these things and try to exert yourself a LITTLE more:&nbsp; then, I

am



<P>convinced, you would SOON acquire that delicate tact which alone is



<P>wanting to give you a proper influence over the mind of your



<P>pupil.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I was about to give the lady some idea of the fallacy of her



<P>expectations; but she sailed away as soon as she had concluded her



<P>speech.&nbsp; Having said what she wished, it was no part of her plan

to



<P>await my answer:&nbsp; it was my business to hear, and not to speak.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>However, as I have said, Matilda at length yielded in some degree



<P>to her mother's authority (pity it had not been exerted before);



<P>and being thus deprived of almost every source of amusement, there



<P>was nothing for it but to take long rides with the groom and long



<P>walks with the governess, and to visit the cottages and farmhouses



<P>on her father's estate, to kill time in chatting with the old men



<P>and women that inhabited them.&nbsp; In one of these walks, it was our



<P>chance to meet Mr. Weston.&nbsp; This was what I had long desired; but



<P>now, for a moment, I wished either he or I were away:&nbsp; I felt my



<P>heart throb so violently that I dreaded lest some outward signs of



<P>emotion should appear; but I think he hardly glanced at me, and I



<P>was soon calm enough.&nbsp; After a brief salutation to both, he asked



<P>Matilda if she had lately heard from her sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes,' replied she.&nbsp; 'She was at Paris when she wrote, and very



<P>well, and very happy.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She spoke the last word emphatically, and with a glance



<P>impertinently sly.&nbsp; He did not seem to notice it, but replied,

with



<P>equal emphasis, and very seriously -

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I hope she will continue to be so.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Do you think it likely?' I ventured to inquire:&nbsp; for Matilda had



<P>started off in pursuit of her dog, that was chasing a leveret.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I cannot tell,' replied he.&nbsp; 'Sir Thomas may be a better man than



<P>I suppose; but, from all I have heard and seen, it seems a pity



<P>that one so young and gay, and - and interesting, to express many



<P>things by one word - whose greatest, if not her only fault, appears



<P>to be thoughtlessness - no trifling fault to be sure, since it



<P>renders the possessor liable to almost every other, and exposes him



<P>to so many temptations - but it seems a pity that she should be



<P>thrown away on such a man.&nbsp; It was her mother's wish, I suppose?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes; and her own too, I think, for she always laughed at my



<P>attempts to dissuade her from the step.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You did attempt it?&nbsp; Then, at least, you will have the



<P>satisfaction of knowing that it is no fault of yours, if any harm



<P>should come of it.&nbsp; As for Mrs. Murray, I don't know how she can



<P>justify her conduct:&nbsp; if I had sufficient acquaintance with her,



<P>I'd ask her.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It seems unnatural:&nbsp; but some people think rank and wealth the



<P>chief good; and, if they can secure that for their children, they



<P>think they have done their duty.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'True:&nbsp; but is it not strange that persons of experience, who have



<P>been married themselves, should judge so falsely?'&nbsp; Matilda now



<P>came panting back, with the lacerated body of the young hare in her



<P>hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Was it your intention to kill that hare, or to save it, Miss



<P>Murray?' asked Mr. Weston, apparently puzzled at her gleeful



<P>countenance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I pretended to want to save it,' she answered, honestly enough,



<P>'as it was so glaringly out of season; but I was better pleased to



<P>see it lolled.&nbsp; However, you can both witness that I couldn't help



<P>it:&nbsp; Prince was determined to have her; and he clutched her by

the



<P>back, and killed her in a minute!&nbsp; Wasn't it a noble chase?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Very! for a young lady after a leveret.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a quiet sarcasm in the tone of his reply which was not



<P>lost upon her; she shrugged her shoulders, and, turning away with a



<P>significant 'Humph!' asked me how I had enjoyed the fun.&nbsp; I replied



<P>that I saw no fun in the matter; but admitted that I had not



<P>observed the transaction very narrowly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Didn't you see how it doubled - just like an old hare? and didn't



<P>you hear it scream?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I'm happy to say I did not.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It cried out just like a child.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Poor little thing!&nbsp; What will you do with it?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Come along - I shall leave it in the first house we come to.&nbsp;

I



<P>don't want to take it home, for fear papa should scold me for



<P>letting the dog kill it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr. Weston was now gone, and we too went on our way; but as we



<P>returned, after having deposited the hare in a farm-house, and



<P>demolished some spice-cake and currant-wine in exchange, we met him



<P>returning also from the execution of his mission, whatever it might



<P>be.&nbsp; He carried in his hand a cluster of beautiful bluebells, which



<P>he offered to me; observing, with a smile, that though he had seen



<P>so little of me for the last two months, he had not forgotten that



<P>blue-bells were numbered among my favourite flowers.&nbsp; It was done



<P>as a simple act of goodwill, without compliment or remarkable



<P>courtesy, or any look that could be construed into 'reverential,



<P>tender adoration' (VIDE Rosalie Murray); but still, it was



<P>something to find my unimportant saying so well remembered:&nbsp; it

was



<P>something that he had noticed so accurately the time I had ceased



<P>to be visible.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I was told,' said he, 'that you were a perfect bookworm, Miss



<P>Grey:&nbsp; so completely absorbed in your studies that you were lost

to



<P>every other pleasure.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, and it's quite true!' cried Matilda.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, Mr. Weston:&nbsp; don't believe it:&nbsp; it's a scandalous libel.



<P>These young ladies are too fond of making random assertions at the



<P>expense of their friends; and you ought to be careful how you



<P>listen to them.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I hope THIS assertion is groundless, at any rate.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why?&nbsp; Do you particularly object to ladies studying?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No; but I object to anyone so devoting himself or herself to



<P>study, as to lose sight of everything else.&nbsp; Except under peculiar



<P>circumstances, I consider very close and constant study as a waste



<P>of time, and an injury to the mind as well as the body.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, I have neither the time nor the inclination for such



<P>transgressions.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>We parted again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Well! what is there remarkable in all this?&nbsp; Why have I recorded



<P>it?&nbsp; Because, reader, it was important enough to give me a cheerful



<P>evening, a night of pleasing dreams, and a morning of felicitous



<P>hopes.&nbsp; Shallow-brained cheerfulness, foolish dreams, unfounded



<P>hopes, you would say; and I will not venture to deny it:



<P>suspicions to that effect arose too frequently in my own mind.&nbsp;

But



<P>our wishes are like tinder:&nbsp; the flint and steel of circumstances



<P>are continually striking out sparks, which vanish immediately,



<P>unless they chance to fall upon the tinder of our wishes; then,



<P>they instantly ignite, and the flame of hope is kindled in a



<P>moment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But alas! that very morning, my flickering flame of hope was



<P>dismally quenched by a letter from my mother, which spoke so



<P>seriously of my father's increasing illness, that I feared there



<P>was little or no chance of his recovery; and, close at hand as the



<P>holidays were, I almost trembled lest they should come too late for



<P>me to meet him in this world.&nbsp; Two days after, a letter from Mary



<P>told me his life was despaired of, and his end seemed fast



<P>approaching.&nbsp; Then, immediately, I sought permission to anticipate



<P>the vacation, and go without delay.&nbsp; Mrs. Murray stared, and



<P>wondered at the unwonted energy and boldness with which I urged the



<P>request, and thought there was no occasion to hurry; but finally



<P>gave me leave:&nbsp; stating, however, that there was 'no need to be

in



<P>such agitation about the matter - it might prove a false alarm



<P>after all; and if not - why, it was only in the common course of



<P>nature:&nbsp; we must all die some time; and I was not to suppose myself



<P>the only afflicted person in the world;' and concluding with saying



<P>I might have the phaeton to take me to O-.&nbsp; 'And instead of



<P>REPINING, Miss Grey, be thankful for the PRIVILEGES you enjoy.



<P>There's many a poor clergyman whose family would be plunged into



<P>ruin by the event of his death; but you, you see, have influential



<P>friends ready to continue their patronage, and to show you every



<P>consideration.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I thanked her for her 'consideration,' and flew to my room to make



<P>some hurried preparations for my departure.&nbsp; My bonnet and shawl



<P>being on, and a few things hastily crammed into my largest trunk, I



<P>descended.&nbsp; But I might have done the work more leisurely, for

no



<P>one else was in a hurry; and I had still a considerable time to



<P>wait for the phaeton.&nbsp; At length it came to the door, and I was



<P>off:&nbsp; but, oh, what a dreary journey was that! how utterly



<P>different from my former passages homewards!&nbsp; Being too late for



<P>the last coach to -, I had to hire a cab for ten miles, and then a



<P>car to take me over the rugged hills.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was half-past ten before I reached home.&nbsp; They were not in bed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>My mother and sister both met me in the passage - sad - silent -



<P>pale!&nbsp; I was so much shocked and terror-stricken that I could not



<P>speak, to ask the information I so much longed yet dreaded to



<P>obtain.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Agnes!' said my mother, struggling to repress some strong emotion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, Agnes!' cried Mary, and burst into tears.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'How is he?' I asked, gasping for the answer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Dead!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was the reply I had anticipated:&nbsp; but the shock seemed none

the



<P>less tremendous.



<P>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XXIV - THE SANDS</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>OUR school was not situated in the heart of the town:&nbsp; on entering



<P>A- from the north-west there is a row of respectable-looking



<P>houses, on each side of the broad, white road, with narrow slips of



<P>garden-ground before them, Venetian blinds to the windows, and a



<P>flight of steps leading to each trim, brass-handled door.&nbsp; In one



<P>of the largest of these habitations dwelt my mother and I, with



<P>such young ladies as our friends and the public chose to commit to



<P>our charge.&nbsp; Consequently, we were a considerable distance from

the



<P>sea, and divided from it by a labyrinth of streets and houses.&nbsp;

But



<P>the sea was my delight; and I would often gladly pierce the town to



<P>obtain the pleasure of a walk beside it, whether with the pupils,



<P>or alone with my mother during the vacations.&nbsp; It was delightful

to



<P>me at all times and seasons, but especially in the wild commotion



<P>of a rough sea-breeze, and in the brilliant freshness of a summer



<P>morning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I awoke early on the third morning after my return from Ashby Park



<P>- the sun was shining through the blind, and I thought how pleasant



<P>it would be to pass through the quiet town and take a solitary



<P>ramble on the sands while half the world was in bed.&nbsp; I was not



<P>long in forming the resolution, nor slow to act upon it.&nbsp; Of course



<P>I would not disturb my mother, so I stole noiselessly downstairs,



<P>and quietly unfastened the door.&nbsp; I was dressed and out, when the



<P>church clock struck a quarter to six.&nbsp; There was a feeling of



<P>freshness and vigour in the very streets; and when I got free of



<P>the town, when my foot was on the sands and my face towards the



<P>broad, bright bay, no language can describe the effect of the deep,



<P>clear azure of the sky and ocean, the bright morning sunshine on



<P>the semicircular barrier of craggy cliffs surmounted by green



<P>swelling hills, and on the smooth, wide sands, and the low rocks



<P>out at sea - looking, with their clothing of weeds and moss, like



<P>little grass-grown islands - and above all, on the brilliant,



<P>sparkling waves.&nbsp; And then, the unspeakable purity - and freshness



<P>of the air!&nbsp; There was just enough heat to enhance the value of

the



<P>breeze, and just enough wind to keep the whole sea in motion, to



<P>make the waves come bounding to the shore, foaming and sparkling,



<P>as if wild with glee.&nbsp; Nothing else was stirring - no living



<P>creature was visible besides myself.&nbsp; My footsteps were the first



<P>to press the firm, unbroken sands; - nothing before had trampled



<P>them since last night's flowing tide had obliterated the deepest



<P>marks of yesterday, and left them fair and even, except where the



<P>subsiding water had left behind it the traces of dimpled pools and



<P>little running streams.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Refreshed, delighted, invigorated, I walked along, forgetting all



<P>my cares, feeling as if I had wings to my feet, and could go at



<P>least forty miles without fatigue, and experiencing a sense of



<P>exhilaration to which I had been an entire stranger since the days



<P>of early youth.&nbsp; About half-past six, however, the grooms began

to



<P>come down to air their masters' horses - first one, and then



<P>another, till there were some dozen horses and five or six riders:



<P>but that need not trouble me, for they would not come as far as the



<P>low rocks which I was now approaching.&nbsp; When I had reached these,



<P>and walked over the moist, slippery sea-weed (at the risk of



<P>floundering into one of the numerous pools of clear, salt water



<P>that lay between them), to a little mossy promontory with the sea



<P>splashing round it, I looked back again to see who next was



<P>stirring.&nbsp; Still, there were only the early grooms with their



<P>horses, and one gentleman with a little dark speck of a dog running



<P>before him, and one water-cart coming out of the town to get water



<P>for the baths.&nbsp; In another minute or two, the distant bathing



<P>machines would begin to move, and then the elderly gentlemen of



<P>regular habits and sober quaker ladies would be coming to take



<P>their salutary morning walks.&nbsp; But however interesting such a scene



<P>might be, I could not wait to witness it, for the sun and the sea



<P>so dazzled my eyes in that direction, that I could but afford one



<P>glance; and then I turned again to delight myself with the sight



<P>and the sound of the sea, dashing against my promontory - with no



<P>prodigious force, for the swell was broken by the tangled sea-weed



<P>and the unseen rocks beneath; otherwise I should soon have been



<P>deluged with spray.&nbsp; But the tide was coming in; the water was



<P>rising; the gulfs and lakes were filling; the straits were



<P>widening:&nbsp; it was time to seek some safer footing; so I walked,



<P>skipped, and stumbled back to the smooth, wide sands, and resolved



<P>to proceed to a certain bold projection in the cliffs, and then



<P>return.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently, I heard a snuffling sound behind me and then a dog came



<P>frisking and wriggling to my feet.&nbsp; It was my own Snap - the little



<P>dark, wire-haired terrier!&nbsp; When I spoke his name, he leapt up

in



<P>my face and yelled for joy.&nbsp; Almost as much delighted as himself,

I



<P>caught the little creature in my arms, and kissed him repeatedly.



<P>But how came he to be there?&nbsp; He could not have dropped from the



<P>sky, or come all that way alone:&nbsp; it must be either his master,

the



<P>rat-catcher, or somebody else that had brought him; so, repressing



<P>my extravagant caresses, and endeavouring to repress his likewise,



<P>I looked round, and beheld - Mr. Weston!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Your dog remembers you well, Miss Grey,' said he, warmly grasping



<P>the hand I offered him without clearly knowing what I was about.



<P>'You rise early.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Not often so early as this,' I replied, with amazing composure,



<P>considering all the circumstances of the case.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'How far do you purpose to extend your walk?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I was thinking of returning - it must be almost time, I think.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He consulted his watch - a gold one now - and told me it was only



<P>five minutes past seven.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But, doubtless, you have had a long enough walk,' said he, turning



<P>towards the town, to which I now proceeded leisurely to retrace my



<P>steps; and he walked beside me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'In what part of the town do you live?' asked he.&nbsp; 'I never could



<P>discover.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Never could discover?&nbsp; Had he endeavoured to do so then?&nbsp;

I told



<P>him the place of our abode.&nbsp; He asked how we prospered in our



<P>affairs.&nbsp; I told him we were doing very well - that we had had

a



<P>considerable addition to our pupils after the Christmas vacation,



<P>and expected a still further increase at the close of this.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You must be an accomplished instructor,' he observed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, it is my mother,' I replied; 'she manages things so well, and



<P>is so active, and clever, and kind.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I should like to know your mother.&nbsp; Will you introduce me to her



<P>some time, if I call?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, willingly.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And will you allow me the privilege of an old friend, of looking



<P>in upon you now and then?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, if - I suppose so.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This was a very foolish answer, but the truth was, I considered



<P>that I had no right to invite anyone to my mother's house without



<P>her knowledge; and if I had said, 'Yes, if my mother does not



<P>object,' it would appear as if by his question I understood more



<P>than was expected; so, SUPPOSING she would not, I added, 'I suppose



<P>so:' but of course I should have said something more sensible and



<P>more polite, if I had had my wits about me.&nbsp; We continued our walk



<P>for a minute in silence; which, however, was shortly relieved (no



<P>small relief to me) by Mr. Weston commenting upon the brightness of



<P>the morning and the beauty of the bay, and then upon the advantages



<P>A- possessed over many other fashionable places of resort.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You don't ask what brings me to A- ' said he.&nbsp; 'You can't suppose



<P>I'm rich enough to come for my own pleasure.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I heard you had left Horton.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You didn't hear, then, that I had got the living of F-?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>F- was a village about two miles distant from A-.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No,' said I; 'we live so completely out of the world, even here,



<P>that news seldom reaches me through any quarter; except through the



<P>medium of the - GAZETTE.&nbsp; But I hope you like your new parish;

and



<P>that I may congratulate you on the acquisition?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I expect to like my parish better a year or two hence, when I have



<P>worked certain reforms I have set my heart upon - or, at least,



<P>progressed some steps towards such an achievement.&nbsp; But you may



<P>congratulate me now; for I find it very agreeable to HAVE a parish



<P>all to myself, with nobody to interfere with me - to thwart my



<P>plans or cripple my exertions:&nbsp; and besides, I have a respectable



<P>house in a rather pleasant neighbourhood, and three hundred pounds



<P>a year; and, in fact, I have nothing but solitude to complain of,



<P>and nothing but a companion to wish for.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He looked at me as he concluded:&nbsp; and the flash of his dark eyes



<P>seemed to set my face on fire; greatly to my own discomfiture, for



<P>to evince confusion at such a juncture was intolerable.&nbsp; I made

an



<P>effort, therefore, to remedy the evil, and disclaim all personal



<P>application of the remark by a hasty, ill-expressed reply, to the



<P>effect that, if he waited till he was well known in the



<P>neighbourhood, he might have numerous opportunities for supplying



<P>his want among the residents of F- and its vicinity, or the



<P>visitors of A-, if he required so ample a choice:&nbsp; not considering



<P>the compliment implied by such an assertion, till his answer made



<P>me aware of it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I am not so presumptuous as to believe that,' said he, 'though you



<P>tell it me; but if it were so, I am rather particular in my notions



<P>of a companion for life, and perhaps I might not find one to suit



<P>me among the ladies you mention.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'If you require perfection, you never will.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I do not - I have no right to require it, as being so far from



<P>perfect myself.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here the conversation was interrupted by a water-cart lumbering



<P>past us, for we were now come to the busy part of the sands; and,



<P>for the next eight or ten minutes, between carts and horses, and



<P>asses, and men, there was little room for social intercourse, till



<P>we had turned our backs upon the sea, and begun to ascend the



<P>precipitous road leading into the town.&nbsp; Here my companion offered



<P>me his arm, which I accepted, though not with the intention of



<P>using it as a support.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You don't often come on to the sands, I think,' said he, 'for I



<P>have walked there many times, both morning and evening, since I



<P>came, and never seen you till now; and several times, in passing



<P>through the town, too, I have looked about for your school - but I



<P>did not think of the - Road; and once or twice I made inquiries,



<P>but without obtaining the requisite information.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When we had surmounted the acclivity, I was about to withdraw my



<P>arm from his, but by a slight tightening of the elbow was tacitly



<P>informed that such was not his will, and accordingly desisted.



<P>Discoursing on different subjects, we entered the town, and passed



<P>through several streets.&nbsp; I saw that he was going out of his way

to



<P>accompany me, notwithstanding the long walk that was yet before



<P>him; and, fearing that he might be inconveniencing himself from



<P>motives of politeness, I observed - 'I fear I am taking you out of



<P>your way, Mr. Weston - I believe the road to F- lies quite in



<P>another direction.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I'll leave you at the end of the next street,' said he.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And when will you come to see mamma?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'To-morrow - God willing.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The end of the next street was nearly the conclusion of my journey.



<P>He stopped there, however, bid me good-morning, and called Snap,



<P>who seemed a little doubtful whether to follow his old mistress or



<P>his new master, but trotted away upon being summoned by the latter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I won't offer to restore him to you, Miss Grey,' said Mr. Weston,



<P>smiling, 'because I like him.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, I don't want him,' replied I, 'now that he has a good master;



<P>I'm quite satisfied.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You take it for granted that I am a good one, then?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The man and the dog departed, and I returned home, full of



<P>gratitude to heaven for so much bliss, and praying that my hopes



<P>might not again be crushed.



<P>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER VIII - THE 'COMING OUT'</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>AT eighteen, Miss Murray was to emerge from the quiet obscurity of



<P>the schoolroom into the full blaze of the fashionable world - as



<P>much of it, at least, as could be had out of London; for her papa



<P>could not be persuaded to leave his rural pleasures and pursuits,



<P>even for a few weeks' residence in town.&nbsp; She was to make her debut



<P>on the third of January, at a magnificent ball, which her mamma



<P>proposed to give to all the nobility and choice gentry of O- and



<P>its neighbourhood for twenty miles round.&nbsp; Of course, she looked



<P>forward to it with the wildest impatience, and the most extravagant



<P>anticipations of delight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Miss Grey,' said she, one evening, a month before the all-



<P>important day, as I was perusing a long and extremely interesting



<P>letter of my sister's - which I had just glanced at in the morning



<P>to see that it contained no very bad news, and kept till now,



<P>unable before to find a quiet moment for reading it, - 'Miss Grey,



<P>do put away that dull, stupid letter, and listen to me!&nbsp; I'm sure



<P>my talk must be far more amusing than that.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She seated herself on the low stool at my feet; and I, suppressing



<P>a sigh of vexation, began to fold up the epistle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You should tell the good people at home not to bore you with such



<P>long letters,' said she; 'and, above all, do bid them write on



<P>proper note-paper, and not on those great vulgar sheets.&nbsp; You



<P>should see the charming little lady-like notes mamma writes to her



<P>friends.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'The good people at home,' replied I, 'know very well that the



<P>longer their letters are, the better I like them.&nbsp; I should be

very



<P>sorry to receive a charming little lady-like note from any of them;



<P>and I thought you were too much of a lady yourself, Miss Murray, to



<P>talk about the "vulgarity" of writing on a large sheet of paper.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, I only said it to tease you.&nbsp; But now I want to talk about



<P>the ball; and to tell you that you positively must put off your



<P>holidays till it is over.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why so? - I shall not be present at the ball.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, but you will see the rooms decked out before it begins, and



<P>hear the music, and, above all, see me in my splendid new dress.&nbsp;

I



<P>shall be so charming, you'll be ready to worship me - you really



<P>must stay.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I should like to see you very much; but I shall have many



<P>opportunities of seeing you equally charming, on the occasion of



<P>some of the numberless balls and parties that are to be, and I



<P>cannot disappoint my friends by postponing my return so long.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, never mind your friends!&nbsp; Tell them we won't let you go.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But, to say the truth, it would be a disappointment to myself:&nbsp;

I



<P>long to see them as much as they to see me - perhaps more.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, but it is such a short time.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Nearly a fortnight by my computation; and, besides, I cannot bear



<P>the thoughts of a Christmas spent from home:&nbsp; and, moreover, my



<P>sister is going to be married.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Is she - when?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Not till next month; but I want to be there to assist her in



<P>making preparations, and to make the best of her company while we



<P>have her.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why didn't you tell me before?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I've only got the news in this letter, which you stigmatize as



<P>dull and stupid, and won't let me read.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'To whom is she to be married?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'To Mr. Richardson, the vicar of a neighbouring parish.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Is he rich?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No; only comfortable.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Is he handsome?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No; only decent.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Young?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No; only middling.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, mercy! what a wretch!&nbsp; What sort of a house is it?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'A quiet little vicarage, with an ivy-clad porch, an old-fashioned



<P>garden, and - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, stop! - you'll make me sick.&nbsp; How CAN she bear it?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I expect she'll not only be able to bear it, but to be very happy.



<P>You did not ask me if Mr. Richardson were a good, wise, or amiable



<P>man; I could have answered Yes, to all these questions - at least



<P>so Mary thinks, and I hope she will not find herself mistaken.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But - miserable creature! how can she think of spending her life



<P>there, cooped up with that nasty old man; and no hope of change?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'He is not old:&nbsp; he's only six or seven and thirty; and she herself



<P>is twenty-eight, and as sober as if she were fifty.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh! that's better then - they're well matched; but do they call



<P>him the "worthy vicar"?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I don't know; but if they do, I believe he merits the epithet.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Mercy, how shocking! and will she wear a white apron and make pies



<P>and puddings?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I don't know about the white apron, but I dare say she will make



<P>pies and puddings now and then; but that will be no great hardship,



<P>as she has done it before.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And will she go about in a plain shawl, and a large straw bonnet,



<P>carrying tracts and bone soup to her husband's poor parishioners?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I'm not clear about that; but I dare say she will do her best to



<P>make them comfortable in body and mind, in accordance with our



<P>mother's example.'



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">
