</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XX - THE FAREWELL</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>A HOUSE in A-, the fashionable watering-place, was hired for our



<P>seminary; and a promise of two or three pupils was obtained to



<P>commence with.&nbsp; I returned to Horton Lodge about the middle of



<P>July, leaving my mother to conclude the bargain for the house, to



<P>obtain more pupils, to sell off the furniture of our old abode, and



<P>to fit out the new one.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>We often pity the poor, because they have no leisure to mourn their



<P>departed relatives, and necessity obliges them to labour through



<P>their severest afflictions:&nbsp; but is not active employment the best



<P>remedy for overwhelming sorrow - the surest antidote for despair?



<P>It may be a rough comforter:&nbsp; it may seem hard to be harassed with



<P>the cares of life when we have no relish for its enjoyments; to be



<P>goaded to labour when the heart is ready to break, and the vexed



<P>spirit implores for rest only to weep in silence:&nbsp; but is not



<P>labour better than the rest we covet? and are not those petty,



<P>tormenting cares less hurtful than a continual brooding over the



<P>great affliction that oppresses us?&nbsp; Besides, we cannot have cares,



<P>and anxieties, and toil, without hope - if it be but the hope of



<P>fulfilling our joyless task, accomplishing some needful project, or



<P>escaping some further annoyance.&nbsp; At any rate, I was glad my mother



<P>had so much employment for every faculty of her action-loving



<P>frame.&nbsp; Our kind neighbours lamented that she, once so exalted

in



<P>wealth and station, should be reduced to such extremity in her time



<P>of sorrow; but I am persuaded that she would have suffered thrice



<P>as much had she been left in affluence, with liberty to remain in



<P>that house, the scene of her early happiness and late affliction,



<P>and no stern necessity to prevent her from incessantly brooding



<P>over and lamenting her bereavement.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I will not dilate upon the feelings with which I left the old



<P>house, the well-known garden, the little village church - then



<P>doubly dear to me, because my father, who, for thirty years, had



<P>taught and prayed within its walls, lay slumbering now beneath its



<P>flags - and the old bare hills, delightful in their very



<P>desolation, with the narrow vales between, smiling in green wood



<P>and sparkling water - the house where I was born, the scene of all



<P>my early associations, the place where throughout life my earthly



<P>affections had been centred; - and left them to return no more!



<P>True, I was going back to Horton Lodge, where, amid many evils, one



<P>source of pleasure yet remained:&nbsp; but it was pleasure mingled with



<P>excessive pain; and my stay, alas! was limited to six weeks.&nbsp; And



<P>even of that precious time, day after day slipped by and I did not



<P>see him:&nbsp; except at church, I never saw him for a fortnight after



<P>my return.&nbsp; It seemed a long time to me:&nbsp; and, as I was often

out



<P>with my rambling pupil, of course hopes would keep rising, and



<P>disappointments would ensue; and then, I would say to my own heart,



<P>'Here is a convincing proof - if you would but have the sense to



<P>see it, or the candour to acknowledge it - that he does not care



<P>for you.&nbsp; If he only thought HALF as much about you as you do about



<P>him, he would have contrived to meet you many times ere this:&nbsp;

you



<P>must know that, by consulting your own feelings.&nbsp; Therefore, have



<P>done with this nonsense:&nbsp; you have no ground for hope:&nbsp; dismiss,

at



<P>once, these hurtful thoughts and foolish wishes from your mind, and



<P>turn to your own duty, and the dull blank life that lies before



<P>you.&nbsp; You might have known such happiness was not for you.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But I saw him at last.&nbsp; He came suddenly upon me as I was crossing



<P>a field in returning from a visit to Nancy Brown, which I had taken



<P>the opportunity of paying while Matilda Murray was riding her



<P>matchless mare.&nbsp; He must have heard of the heavy loss I had



<P>sustained:&nbsp; he expressed no sympathy, offered no condolence:&nbsp;

but



<P>almost the first words he uttered were, - 'How is your mother?'



<P>And this was no matter-of -course question, for I never told him



<P>that I had a mother:&nbsp; he must have learned the fact from others,

if



<P>he knew it at all; and, besides, there was sincere goodwill, and



<P>even deep, touching, unobtrusive sympathy in the tone and manner of



<P>the inquiry.&nbsp; I thanked him with due civility, and told him she

was



<P>as well as could be expected.&nbsp; 'What will she do?' was the next



<P>question.&nbsp; Many would have deemed it an impertinent one, and given



<P>an evasive reply; but such an idea never entered my head, and I



<P>gave a brief but plain statement of my mother's plans and



<P>prospects.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Then you will leave this place shortly?' said he.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, in a month.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He paused a minute, as if in thought.&nbsp; When he spoke again, I hoped



<P>it would be to express his concern at my departure; but it was only



<P>to say, - 'I should think you will be willing enough to go?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes - for some things,' I replied.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'For SOME things only - I wonder what should make you regret it?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I was annoyed at this in some degree; because it embarrassed me:&nbsp;

I



<P>had only one reason for regretting it; and that was a profound



<P>secret, which he had no business to trouble me about.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why,' said I - 'why should you suppose that I dislike the place?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You told me so yourself,' was the decisive reply.&nbsp; 'You said,

at



<P>least, that you could not live contentedly, without a friend; and



<P>that you had no friend here, and no possibility of making one -



<P>and, besides, I know you MUST dislike it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But if you remember rightly, I said, or meant to say, I could not



<P>live contentedly without a friend in the world:&nbsp; I was not so



<P>unreasonable as to require one always near me.&nbsp; I think I could

be



<P>happy in a house full of enemies, if - ' but no; that sentence must



<P>not be continued - I paused, and hastily added, - 'And, besides, we



<P>cannot well leave a place where we have lived for two or three



<P>years, without some feeling of regret.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Will you regret to part with Miss Murray, your sole remaining



<P>pupil and companion?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I dare say I shall in some degree:&nbsp; it was not without sorrow

I



<P>parted with her sister.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I can imagine that.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, Miss Matilda is quite as good - better in one respect.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What is that?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'She's honest.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And the other is not?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I should not call her DIShonest; but it must be confessed she's a



<P>little artful.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'ARTFUL is she? - I saw she was giddy and vain - and now,' he



<P>added, after a pause, 'I can well believe she was artful too; but



<P>so excessively so as to assume an aspect of extreme simplicity and



<P>unguarded openness.&nbsp; Yes,' continued he, musingly, 'that accounts



<P>for some little things that puzzled me a trifle before.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After that, he turned the conversation to more general subjects.



<P>He did not leave me till we had nearly reached the park-gates:&nbsp;

he



<P>had certainly stepped a little out of his way to accompany me so



<P>far, for he now went back and disappeared down Moss Lane, the



<P>entrance of which we had passed some time before.&nbsp; Assuredly I

did



<P>not regret this circumstance:&nbsp; if sorrow had any place in my heart,



<P>it was that he was gone at last - that he was no longer walking by



<P>my side, and that that short interval of delightful intercourse was



<P>at an end.&nbsp; He had not breathed a word of love, or dropped one

hint



<P>of tenderness or affection, and yet I had been supremely happy.&nbsp;

To



<P>be near him, to hear him talk as he did talk, and to feel that he



<P>thought me worthy to be so spoken to - capable of understanding and



<P>duly appreciating such discourse - was enough.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, Edward Weston, I could indeed be happy in a house full of



<P>enemies, if I had but one friend, who truly, deeply, and faithfully



<P>loved me; and if that friend were you - though we might be far



<P>apart - seldom to hear from each other, still more seldom to meet -



<P>though toil, and trouble, and vexation might surround me, still -



<P>it would be too much happiness for me to dream of!&nbsp; Yet who can



<P>tell,' said I within myself, as I proceeded up the park, - 'who can



<P>tell what this one month may bring forth?&nbsp; I have lived nearly



<P>three-and-twenty years, and I have suffered much, and tasted little



<P>pleasure yet; is it likely my life all through will be so clouded?



<P>Is it not possible that God may hear my prayers, disperse these



<P>gloomy shadows, and grant me some beams of heaven's sunshine yet?



<P>Will He entirely deny to me those blessings which are so freely



<P>given to others, who neither ask them nor acknowledge them when



<P>received?&nbsp; May I not still hope and trust?&nbsp; I did hope and

trust



<P>for a while:&nbsp; but, alas, alas! the time ebbed away:&nbsp; one week



<P>followed another, and, excepting one distant glimpse and two



<P>transient meetings - during which scarcely anything was said -



<P>while I was walking with Miss Matilda, I saw nothing of him:



<P>except, of course, at church.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And now, the last Sunday was come, and the last service.&nbsp; I was



<P>often on the point of melting into tears during the sermon - the



<P>last I was to hear from him:&nbsp; the best I should hear from anyone,

I



<P>was well assured.&nbsp; It was over - the congregation were departing;



<P>and I must follow.&nbsp; I had then seen him, and heard his voice, too,



<P>probably for the last time.&nbsp; In the churchyard, Matilda was pounced



<P>upon by the two Misses Green.&nbsp; They had many inquiries to make



<P>about her sister, and I know not what besides.&nbsp; I only wished they



<P>would have done, that we might hasten back to Horton Lodge:&nbsp; I



<P>longed to seek the retirement of my own room, or some sequestered



<P>nook in the grounds, that I might deliver myself up to my feelings



<P>- to weep my last farewell, and lament my false hopes and vain



<P>delusions.&nbsp; Only this once, and then adieu to fruitless dreaming

-



<P>thenceforth, only sober, solid, sad reality should occupy my mind.



<P>But while I thus resolved, a low voice close beside me said - 'I



<P>suppose you are going this week, Miss Grey?'&nbsp; 'Yes,' I replied.&nbsp;

I



<P>was very much startled; and had I been at all hysterically



<P>inclined, I certainly should have committed myself in some way



<P>then.&nbsp; Thank God, I was not.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well,' said Mr. Weston, 'I want to bid you good-bye - it is not



<P>likely I shall see you again before you go.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Good-bye, Mr. Weston,' I said.&nbsp; Oh, how I struggled to say it



<P>calmly!&nbsp; I gave him my hand.&nbsp; He retained it a few seconds

in his.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It is possible we may meet again,' said he; 'will it be of any



<P>consequence to you whether we do or not?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, I should be very glad to see you again.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I COULD say no less.&nbsp; He kindly pressed my hand, and went.&nbsp;

Now, I



<P>was happy again - though more inclined to burst into tears than



<P>ever.&nbsp; If I had been forced to speak at that moment, a succession



<P>of sobs would have inevitably ensued; and as it was, I could not



<P>keep the water out of my eyes.&nbsp; I walked along with Miss Murray,



<P>turning aside my face, and neglecting to notice several successive



<P>remarks, till she bawled out that I was either deaf or stupid; and



<P>then (having recovered my self-possession), as one awakened from a



<P>fit of abstraction, I suddenly looked up and asked what she had



<P>been saying.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter21</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XXI - THE SCHOOL</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>I LEFT Horton Lodge, and went to join my mother in our new abode at



<P>A-.&nbsp; I found her well in health, resigned in spirit, and even



<P>cheerful, though subdued and sober, in her general demeanour.&nbsp;

We



<P>had only three boarders and half a dozen day-pupils to commence



<P>with; but by due care and diligence we hoped ere long to increase



<P>the number of both.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I set myself with befitting energy to discharge the duties of this



<P>new mode of life.&nbsp; I call it NEW, for there was, indeed, a



<P>considerable difference between working with my mother in a school



<P>of our own, and working as a hireling among strangers, despised and



<P>trampled upon by old and young; and for the first few weeks I was



<P>by no means unhappy.&nbsp; 'It is possible we may meet again,' and 'will



<P>it be of any consequence to you whether we do or not?' - Those



<P>words still rang in my ear and rested on my heart:&nbsp; they were my



<P>secret solace and support.&nbsp; 'I shall see him again. - He will come;



<P>or he will write.'&nbsp; No promise, in fact, was too bright or too



<P>extravagant for Hope to whisper in my ear.&nbsp; I did not believe half



<P>of what she told me:&nbsp; I pretended to laugh at it all; but I was

far



<P>more credulous than I myself supposed; otherwise, why did my heart



<P>leap up when a knock was heard at the front door, and the maid, who



<P>opened it, came to tell my mother a gentleman wished to see her?



<P>and why was I out of humour for the rest of the day, because it



<P>proved to be a music-master come to offer his services to our



<P>school? and what stopped my breath for a moment, when the postman



<P>having brought a couple of letters, my mother said, 'Here, Agnes,



<P>this is for you,' and threw one of them to me? and what made the



<P>hot blood rush into my face when I saw it was directed in a



<P>gentleman's hand? and why - oh! why did that cold, sickening sense



<P>of disappointment fall upon me, when I had torn open the cover and



<P>found it was ONLY a letter from Mary, which, for some reason or



<P>other, her husband had directed for her?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Was it then come to this - that I should be DISAPPOINTED to receive



<P>a letter from my only sister:&nbsp; and because it was not written by

a



<P>comparative stranger?&nbsp; Dear Mary! and she had written it so kindly



<P>- and thinking I should be so pleased to have it! - I was not



<P>worthy to read it!&nbsp; And I believe, in my indignation against



<P>myself, I should have put it aside till I had schooled myself into



<P>a better frame of mind, and was become more deserving of the honour



<P>and privilege of its perusal:&nbsp; but there was my mother looking

on,



<P>and wishful to know what news it contained; so I read it and



<P>delivered it to her, and then went into the schoolroom to attend to



<P>the pupils:&nbsp; but amidst the cares of copies and sums - in the



<P>intervals of correcting errors here, and reproving derelictions of



<P>duty there, I was inwardly taking myself to task with far sterner



<P>severity.&nbsp; 'What a fool you must be,' said my head to my heart,

or



<P>my sterner to my softer self; - 'how could you ever dream that he



<P>would write to you?&nbsp; What grounds have you for such a hope - or



<P>that he will see you, or give himself any trouble about you - or



<P>even think of you again?'&nbsp; 'What grounds?' - and then Hope set



<P>before me that last, short interview, and repeated the words I had



<P>so faithfully treasured in my memory.&nbsp; 'Well, and what was there

in



<P>that? - Who ever hung his hopes upon so frail a twig?&nbsp; What was



<P>there in those words that any common acquaintance might not say to



<P>another?&nbsp; Of course, it was possible you might meet again:&nbsp;

he



<P>might have said so if you had been going to New Zealand; but that



<P>did not imply any INTENTION of seeing you - and then, as to the



<P>question that followed, anyone might ask that:&nbsp; and how did you



<P>answer? - Merely with a stupid, commonplace reply, such as you



<P>would have given to Master Murray, or anyone else you had been on



<P>tolerably civil terms with.'&nbsp; 'But, then,' persisted Hope, 'the



<P>tone and manner in which he spoke.'&nbsp; 'Oh, that is nonsense! he



<P>always speaks impressively; and at that moment there were the



<P>Greens and Miss Matilda Murray just before, and other people



<P>passing by, and he was obliged to stand close beside you, and to



<P>speak very low, unless he wished everybody to hear what he said,



<P>which - though it was nothing at all particular - of course, he



<P>would rather not.'&nbsp; But then, above all, that emphatic, yet gentle



<P>pressure of the hand, which seemed to say, 'TRUST me;' and many



<P>other things besides - too delightful, almost too flattering, to be



<P>repeated even to one's self.&nbsp; 'Egregious folly - too absurd to



<P>require contradiction - mere inventions of the imagination, which



<P>you ought to be ashamed of.&nbsp; If you would but consider your own



<P>unattractive exterior, your unamiable reserve, your foolish



<P>diffidence - which must make you appear cold, dull, awkward, and



<P>perhaps ill-tempered too; - if you had but rightly considered these



<P>from the beginning, you would never have harboured such



<P>presumptuous thoughts:&nbsp; and now that you have been so foolish,

pray



<P>repent and amend, and let us have no more of it!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I cannot say that I implicitly obeyed my own injunctions:&nbsp; but

such



<P>reasoning as this became more and more effective as time wore on,



<P>and nothing was seen or heard of Mr. Weston; until, at last, I gave



<P>up hoping, for even my heart acknowledged it was all in vain.&nbsp;

But



<P>still, I would think of him:&nbsp; I would cherish his image in my mind;



<P>and treasure every word, look, and gesture that my memory could



<P>retain; and brood over his excellences and his peculiarities, and,



<P>in fact, all I had seen, heard, or imagined respecting him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Agnes, this sea air and change of scene do you no good, I think:



<P>I never saw you look so wretched.&nbsp; It must be that you sit too



<P>much, and allow the cares of the schoolroom to worry you.&nbsp; You

must



<P>learn to take things easy, and to be more active and cheerful; you



<P>must take exercise whenever you can get it, and leave the most



<P>tiresome duties to me:&nbsp; they will only serve to exercise my



<P>patience, and, perhaps, try my temper a little.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So said my mother, as we sat at work one morning during the Easter



<P>holidays.&nbsp; I assured her that my employments were not at all



<P>oppressive; that I was well; or, if there was anything amiss, it



<P>would be gone as soon as the trying months of spring were over:



<P>when summer came I should be as strong and hearty as she could wish



<P>to see me:&nbsp; but inwardly her observation startled me.&nbsp; I knew

my



<P>strength was declining, my appetite had failed, and I was grown



<P>listless and desponding; - and if, indeed, he could never care for



<P>me, and I could never see him more - if I was forbidden to minister



<P>to his happiness - forbidden, for ever, to taste the joys of love,



<P>to bless, and to be blessed - then, life must be a burden, and if



<P>my heavenly Father would call me away, I should be glad to rest.



<P>But it would not do to die and leave my mother.&nbsp; Selfish, unworthy



<P>daughter, to forget her for a moment!&nbsp; Was not her happiness



<P>committed in a great measure to my charge? - and the welfare of our



<P>young pupils too?&nbsp; Should I shrink from the work that God had set



<P>before me, because it was not fitted to my taste?&nbsp; Did not He know



<P>best what I should do, and where I ought to labour? - and should I



<P>long to quit His service before I had finished my task, and expect



<P>to enter into His rest without having laboured to earn it?&nbsp; 'No;

by



<P>His help I will arise and address myself diligently to my appointed



<P>duty.&nbsp; If happiness in this world is not for me, I will endeavour



<P>to promote the welfare of those around me, and my reward shall be



<P>hereafter.'&nbsp; So said I in my heart; and from that hour I only



<P>permitted my thoughts to wander to Edward Weston - or at least to



<P>dwell upon him now and then - as a treat for rare occasions:&nbsp; and,



<P>whether it was really the approach of summer or the effect of these



<P>good resolutions, or the lapse of time, or all together,



<P>tranquillity of mind was soon restored; and bodily health and



<P>vigour began likewise, slowly, but surely, to return.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Early in June, I received a letter from Lady Ashby, late Miss



<P>Murray.&nbsp; She had written to me twice or thrice before, from the



<P>different stages of her bridal tour, always in good spirits, and



<P>professing to be very happy.&nbsp; I wondered every time that she had



<P>not forgotten me, in the midst of so much gaiety and variety of



<P>scene.&nbsp; At length, however, there was a pause; and it seemed she



<P>had forgotten me, for upwards of seven months passed away and no



<P>letter.&nbsp; Of course, I did not break my heart about THAT, though

I



<P>often wondered how she was getting on; and when this last epistle



<P>so unexpectedly arrived, I was glad enough to receive it.&nbsp; It was



<P>dated from Ashby Park, where she was come to settle down at last,



<P>having previously divided her time between the continent and the



<P>metropolis.&nbsp; She made many apologies for having neglected me so



<P>long, assured me she had not forgotten me, and had often intended



<P>to write, &amp;c. &amp;c., but had always been prevented by something.&nbsp;

She



<P>acknowledged that she had been leading a very dissipated life, and



<P>I should think her very wicked and very thoughtless; but,



<P>notwithstanding that, she thought a great deal, and, among other



<P>things, that she should vastly like to see me.&nbsp; 'We have been



<P>several days here already,' wrote she.&nbsp; 'We have not a single



<P>friend with us, and are likely to be very dull.&nbsp; You know I never



<P>had a fancy for living with my husband like two turtles in a nest,



<P>were he the most delightful creature that ever wore a coat; so do



<P>take pity upon me and come.&nbsp; I suppose your Midsummer holidays



<P>commence in June, the same as other people's; therefore you cannot



<P>plead want of time; and you must and shall come - in fact, I shall



<P>die if you don't.&nbsp; I want you to visit me as a friend, and stay

a



<P>long time.&nbsp; There is nobody with me, as I told you before, but

Sir



<P>Thomas and old Lady Ashby:&nbsp; but you needn't mind them - they'll



<P>trouble us but little with their company.&nbsp; And you shall have a



<P>room to yourself, whenever you like to retire to it, and plenty of



<P>books to read when my company is not sufficiently amusing.&nbsp; I



<P>forget whether you like babies; if you do, you may have the



<P>pleasure of seeing mine - the most charming child in the world, no



<P>doubt; and all the more so, that I am not troubled with nursing it



<P>- I was determined I wouldn't be bothered with that.



<P>Unfortunately, it is a girl, and Sir Thomas has never forgiven me:



<P>but, however, if you will only come, I promise you shall be its



<P>governess as soon as it can speak; and you shall bring it up in the



<P>way it should go, and make a better woman of it than its mamma.



<P>And you shall see my poodle, too:&nbsp; a splendid little charmer



<P>imported from Paris:&nbsp; and two fine Italian paintings of great value



<P>- I forget the artist.&nbsp; Doubtless you will be able to discover



<P>prodigious beauties in them, which you must point out to me, as I



<P>only admire by hearsay; and many elegant curiosities besides, which



<P>I purchased at Rome and elsewhere; and, finally, you shall see my



<P>new home - the splendid house and grounds I used to covet so



<P>greatly.&nbsp; Alas! how far the promise of anticipation exceeds the



<P>pleasure of possession!&nbsp; There's a fine sentiment!&nbsp; I assure

you I



<P>am become quite a grave old matron:&nbsp; pray come, if it be only to



<P>witness the wonderful change.&nbsp; Write by return of post, and tell

me



<P>when your vacation commences, and say that you will come the day



<P>after, and stay till the day before it closes - in mercy to

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yours affectionately,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'ROSALIE ASHBY.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I showed this strange epistle to my mother, and consulted her on



<P>what I ought to do.&nbsp; She advised me to go; and I went - willing



<P>enough to see Lady Ashby, and her baby, too, and to do anything I



<P>could to benefit her, by consolation or advice; for I imagined she



<P>must be unhappy, or she would not have applied to me thus - but



<P>feeling, as may readily be conceived, that, in accepting the



<P>invitation, I made a great sacrifice for her, and did violence to



<P>my feelings in many ways, instead of being delighted with the



<P>honourable distinction of being entreated by the baronet's lady to



<P>visit her as a friend.&nbsp; However, I determined my visit should be



<P>only for a few days at most; and I will not deny that I derived



<P>some consolation from the idea that, as Ashby Park was not very far



<P>from Horton, I might possibly see Mr. Weston, or, at least, hear



<P>something about him.



<P>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER IX - THE BALL</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>'NOW, Miss Grey,' exclaimed Miss Murray, immediately I entered the



<P>schoolroom, after having taken off my outdoor garments, upon



<P>returning from my four weeks' recreation, 'Now - shut the door, and



<P>sit down, and I'll tell you all about the ball.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No - damn it, no!' shouted Miss Matilda.&nbsp; 'Hold your tongue, can't



<P>ye? and let me tell her about my new mare - SUCH a splendour, Miss



<P>Grey! a fine blood mare - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Do be quiet, Matilda; and let me tell my news first.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, no, Rosalie; you'll be such a damned long time over it - she



<P>shall hear me first - I'll be hanged if she doesn't!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I'm sorry to hear, Miss Matilda, that you've not got rid of that



<P>shocking habit yet.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, I can't help it:&nbsp; but I'll never say a wicked word again,

if



<P>you'll only listen to me, and tell Rosalie to hold her confounded



<P>tongue.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Rosalie remonstrated, and I thought I should have been torn in



<P>pieces between them; but Miss Matilda having the loudest voice, her



<P>sister at length gave in, and suffered her to tell her story first:



<P>so I was doomed to hear a long account of her splendid mare, its



<P>breeding and pedigree, its paces, its action, its spirit, &amp;c., and



<P>of her own amazing skill and courage in riding it; concluding with



<P>an assertion that she could clear a five-barred gate 'like



<P>winking,' that papa said she might hunt the next time the hounds



<P>met, and mamma had ordered a bright scarlet hunting-habit for her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, Matilda! what stories you are telling!' exclaimed her sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well,' answered she, no whit abashed, 'I know I COULD clear a



<P>five-barred gate, if I tried, and papa WILL say I may hunt, and



<P>mamma WILL order the habit when I ask it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, now get along,' replied Miss Murray; 'and do, dear Matilda,



<P>try to be a little more lady-like.&nbsp; Miss Grey, I wish you would



<P>tell her not to use such shocking words; she will call her horse a



<P>mare:&nbsp; it is so inconceivably shocking! and then she uses such



<P>dreadful expressions in describing it:&nbsp; she must have learned it



<P>from the grooms.&nbsp; It nearly puts me into fits when she begins.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I learned it from papa, you ass! and his jolly friends,' said the



<P>young lady, vigorously cracking a hunting-whip, which she



<P>habitually carried in her hand.&nbsp; 'I'm as good judge of horseflesh



<P>as the best of 'm.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, now get along, you shocking girl!&nbsp; I really shall take a

fit



<P>if you go on in such a way.&nbsp; And now, Miss Grey, attend to me;

I'm



<P>going to tell you about the ball.&nbsp; You must be dying to hear about



<P>it, I know.&nbsp; Oh, SUCH a ball!&nbsp; You never saw or heard, or

read, or



<P>dreamt of anything like it in all your life.&nbsp; The decorations,

the



<P>entertainment, the supper, the music were indescribable! and then



<P>the guests!&nbsp; There were two noblemen, three baronets, and five



<P>titled ladies, and other ladies and gentlemen innumerable.&nbsp; The



<P>ladies, of course, were of no consequence to me, except to put me



<P>in a good humour with myself, by showing how ugly and awkward most



<P>of them were; and the best, mamma told me, - the most transcendent



<P>beauties among them, were nothing to me.&nbsp; As for me, Miss Grey

-



<P>I'm so SORRY you didn't see me!&nbsp; I was CHARMING - wasn't I,



<P>Matilda?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Middling.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, but I really was - at least so mamma said - and Brown and



<P>Williamson.&nbsp; Brown said she was sure no gentleman could set eyes

on



<P>me without falling in love that minute; and so I may be allowed to



<P>be a little vain.&nbsp; I know you think me a shocking, conceited,



<P>frivolous girl; but then, you know, I don't attribute it ALL to my



<P>personal attractions:&nbsp; I give some praise to the hairdresser, and



<P>some to my exquisitely lovely dress - you must see it to-morrow -



<P>white gauze over pink satin - and so SWEETLY made! and a necklace



<P>and bracelet of beautiful, large pearls!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I have no doubt you looked very charming:&nbsp; but should that delight



<P>you so very much?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, no! - not that alone:&nbsp; but, then, I was so much admired; and

I



<P>made so MANY conquests in that one night - you'd be astonished to



<P>hear - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But what good will they do you?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What good!&nbsp; Think of any woman asking that!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, I should think one conquest would be enough; and too much,



<P>unless the subjugation were mutual.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, but you know I never agree with you on those points.&nbsp; Now,



<P>wait a bit, and I'll tell you my principal admirers - those who



<P>made themselves very conspicuous that night and after:&nbsp; for I've



<P>been to two parties since.&nbsp; Unfortunately the two noblemen, Lord

G-



<P>and Lord F-, were married, or I might have condescended to be



<P>particularly gracious to THEM; as it was, I did not:&nbsp; though Lord



<P>F-, who hates his wife, was evidently much struck with me.&nbsp; He



<P>asked me to dance with him twice - he is a charming dancer, by-the-



<P>by, and so am I:&nbsp; you can't think how well I did - I was astonished



<P>at myself.&nbsp; My lord was very complimentary too - rather too much

so



<P>in fact - and I thought proper to be a little haughty and



<P>repellent; but I had the pleasure of seeing his nasty, cross wife



<P>ready to perish with spite and vexation - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, Miss Murray! you don't mean to say that such a thing could



<P>really give you pleasure?&nbsp; However cross or - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, I know it's very wrong; - but never mind!&nbsp; I mean to be

good



<P>some time - only don't preach now, there's a good creature.&nbsp; I



<P>haven't told you half yet.&nbsp; Let me see.&nbsp; Oh! I was going to

tell



<P>you how many unmistakeable admirers I had:- Sir Thomas Ashby was



<P>one, - Sir Hugh Meltham and Sir Broadley Wilson are old codgers,



<P>only fit companions for papa and mamma.&nbsp; Sir Thomas is young, rich,



<P>and gay; but an ugly beast, nevertheless:&nbsp; however, mamma says

I



<P>should not mind that after a few months' acquaintance.&nbsp; Then, there



<P>was Henry Meltham, Sir Hugh's younger son; rather good-looking, and



<P>a pleasant fellow to flirt with:&nbsp; but BEING a younger son, that

is



<P>all he is good for; then there was young Mr. Green, rich enough,



<P>but of no family, and a great stupid fellow, a mere country booby!



<P>and then, our good rector, Mr. Hatfield:&nbsp; an HUMBLE admirer he



<P>ought to consider himself; but I fear he has forgotten to number



<P>humility among his stock of Christian virtues.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Was Mr. Hatfield at the ball?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, to he sure.&nbsp; Did you think he was too good to go?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I thought be might consider it unclerical.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'By no means.&nbsp; He did not profane his cloth by dancing; but it

was



<P>with difficulty he could refrain, poor man:&nbsp; he looked as if he



<P>were dying to ask my hand just for ONE set; and - oh! by-the-by -



<P>he's got a new curate:&nbsp; that seedy old fellow Mr. Bligh has got

his



<P>long-wished-for living at last, and is gone.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And what is the new one like?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, SUCH a beast!&nbsp; Weston his name is.&nbsp; I can give you his



<P>description in three words - an insensate, ugly, stupid blockhead.



<P>That's four, but no matter - enough of HIM now.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then she returned to the ball, and gave me a further account of her



<P>deportment there, and at the several parties she had since



<P>attended; and further particulars respecting Sir Thomas Ashby and



<P>Messrs. Meltham, Green, and Hatfield, and the ineffaceable



<P>impression she had wrought upon each of them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, which of the four do you like best?' said I, suppressing my



<P>third or fourth yawn.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I detest them all!' replied she, shaking her bright ringlets in



<P>vivacious scorn.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'That means, I suppose, "I like them all" - but which most?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, I really detest them all; but Harry Meltham is the handsomest



<P>and most amusing, and Mr. Hatfield the cleverest, Sir Thomas the



<P>wickedest, and Mr. Green the most stupid.&nbsp; But the one I'm to have,



<P>I suppose, if I'm doomed to have any of them, is Sir Thomas Ashby.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Surely not, if he's so wicked, and if you dislike him?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, I don't mind his being wicked:&nbsp; he's all the better for that;



<P>and as for disliking him - I shouldn't greatly object to being Lady



<P>Ashby of Ashby Park, if I must marry.&nbsp; But if I could be always



<P>young, I would be always single.&nbsp; I should like to enjoy myself



<P>thoroughly, and coquet with all the world, till I am on the verge



<P>of being called an old maid; and then, to escape the infamy of



<P>that, after having made ten thousand conquests, to break all their



<P>hearts save one, by marrying some high-born, rich, indulgent



<P>husband, whom, on the other hand, fifty ladies were dying to have.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, as long as you entertain these views, keep single by all



<P>means, and never marry at all:&nbsp; not even to escape the infamy of



<P>old-maidenhood.'



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter23</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XXIII - THE PARK</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>I CAME down a little before eight, next morning, as I knew by the



<P>striking of a distant clock.&nbsp; There was no appearance of breakfast.



<P>I waited above an hour before it came, still vainly longing for



<P>access to the library; and, after that lonely repast was concluded,



<P>I waited again about an hour and a half in great suspense and



<P>discomfort, uncertain what to do.&nbsp; At length Lady Ashby came to

bid



<P>me good-morning.&nbsp; She informed me she had only just breakfasted,



<P>and now wanted me to take an early walk with her in the park.&nbsp;

She



<P>asked how long I had been up, and on receiving my answer, expressed



<P>the deepest regret, and again promised to show me the library.&nbsp;

I



<P>suggested she had better do so at once, and then there would be no



<P>further trouble either with remembering or forgetting.&nbsp; She



<P>complied, on condition that I would not think of reading, or



<P>bothering with the books now; for she wanted to show me the



<P>gardens, and take a walk in the park with me, before it became too



<P>hot for enjoyment; which, indeed, was nearly the case already.&nbsp;

Of



<P>course I readily assented; and we took our walk accordingly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As we were strolling in the park, talking of what my companion had



<P>seen and heard during her travelling experience, a gentleman on



<P>horseback rode up and passed us.&nbsp; As he turned, in passing, and



<P>stared me full in the face, I had a good opportunity of seeing what



<P>he was like.&nbsp; He was tall, thin, and wasted, with a slight stoop

in



<P>the shoulders, a pale face, but somewhat blotchy, and disagreeably



<P>red about the eyelids, plain features, and a general appearance of



<P>languor and flatness, relieved by a sinister expression in the



<P>mouth and the dull, soulless eyes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I detest that man!' whispered Lady Ashby, with bitter emphasis, as



<P>he slowly trotted by.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Who is it?' I asked, unwilling to suppose that she should so speak



<P>of her husband.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Sir Thomas Ashby,' she replied, with dreary composure.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And do you DETEST him, Miss Murray?' said I, for I was too much



<P>shocked to remember her name at the moment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, I do, Miss Grey, and despise him too; and if you knew him you



<P>would not blame me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But you knew what he was before you married him.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No; I only thought so:&nbsp; I did not half know him really.&nbsp;

I know



<P>you warned me against it, and I wish I had listened to you:&nbsp; but



<P>it's too late to regret that now.&nbsp; And besides, mamma ought to

have



<P>known better than either of us, and she never said anything against



<P>it - quite the contrary.&nbsp; And then I thought he adored me, and



<P>would let me have my own way:&nbsp; he did pretend to do so at first,



<P>but now he does not care a bit about me.&nbsp; Yet I should not care

for



<P>that:&nbsp; he might do as he pleased, if I might only be free to amuse



<P>myself and to stay in London, or have a few friends down here:&nbsp;

but



<P>HE WILL do as he pleases, and I must be a prisoner and a slave.



<P>The moment he saw I could enjoy myself without him, and that others



<P>knew my value better than himself, the selfish wretch began to



<P>accuse me of coquetry and extravagance; and to abuse Harry Meltham,



<P>whose shoes he was not worthy to clean.&nbsp; And then he must needs



<P>have me down in the country, to lead the life of a nun, lest I



<P>should dishonour him or bring him to ruin; as if he had not been



<P>ten times worse every way, with his betting-book, and his gaming-



<P>table, and his opera-girls, and his Lady This and Mrs. That - yes,



<P>and his bottles of wine, and glasses of brandy-and-water too!&nbsp;

Oh,



<P>I would give ten thousand worlds to be Mss Murray again!&nbsp; It is

TOO



<P>bad to feel life, health, and beauty wasting away, unfelt and



<P>unenjoyed, for such a brute as that!' exclaimed she, fairly



<P>bursting into tears in the bitterness of her vexation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Of course, I pitied her exceedingly; as well for her false idea of



<P>happiness and disregard of duty, as for the wretched partner with



<P>whom her fate was linked.&nbsp; I said what I could to comfort her,

and



<P>offered such counsels as I thought she most required:&nbsp; advising



<P>her, first, by gentle reasoning, by kindness, example, and



<P>persuasion, to try to ameliorate her husband; and then, when she



<P>had done all she could, if she still found him incorrigible, to



<P>endeavour to abstract herself from him - to wrap herself up in her



<P>own integrity, and trouble herself as little about him as possible.



<P>I exhorted her to seek consolation in doing her duty to God and



<P>man, to put her trust in Heaven, and solace herself with the care



<P>and nurture of her little daughter; assuring her she would be amply



<P>rewarded by witnessing its progress in strength and wisdom, and



<P>receiving its genuine affection.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But I can't devote myself entirely to a child,' said she; 'it may



<P>die - which is not at all improbable.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But, with care, many a delicate infant has become a strong man or



<P>woman.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But it may grow so intolerably like its father that I shall hate



<P>it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'That is not likely; it is a little girl, and strongly resembles



<P>its mother.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No matter; I should like it better if it were a boy - only that



<P>its father will leave it no inheritance that he can possibly



<P>squander away.&nbsp; What pleasure can I have in seeing a girl grow

up



<P>to eclipse me, and enjoy those pleasures that I am for ever



<P>debarred from?&nbsp; But supposing I could be so generous as to take



<P>delight in this, still it is ONLY a child; and I can't centre all



<P>my hopes in a child:&nbsp; that is only one degree better than devoting



<P>oneself to a dog.&nbsp; And as for all the wisdom and goodness you have



<P>been trying to instil into me - that is all very right and proper,



<P>I daresay, and if I were some twenty years older, I might fructify



<P>by it:&nbsp; but people must enjoy themselves when they are young; and



<P>if others won't let them - why, they must hate them for it!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'The best way to enjoy yourself is to do what is right and hate



<P>nobody.&nbsp; The end of Religion is not to teach us how to die, but

how



<P>to live; and the earlier you become wise and good, the more of



<P>happiness you secure.&nbsp; And now, Lady Ashby, I have one more piece



<P>of advice to offer you, which is, that you will not make an enemy



<P>of your mother-in-law.&nbsp; Don't get into the way of holding her at



<P>arms' length, and regarding her with jealous distrust.&nbsp; I never

saw



<P>her, but I have heard good as well as evil respecting her; and I



<P>imagine that, though cold and haughty in her general demeanour, and



<P>even exacting in her requirements, she has strong affections for



<P>those who can reach them; and, though so blindly attached to her



<P>son, she is not without good principles, or incapable of hearing



<P>reason.&nbsp; If you would but conciliate her a little, and adopt a



<P>friendly, open manner - and even confide your grievances to her -



<P>real grievances, such as you have a right to complain of - it is my



<P>firm belief that she would, in time, become your faithful friend,



<P>and a comfort and support to you, instead of the incubus you



<P>describe her.'&nbsp; But I fear my advice had little effect upon the



<P>unfortunate young lady; and, finding I could render myself so



<P>little serviceable, my residence at Ashby Park became doubly



<P>painful.&nbsp; But still, I must stay out that day and the following



<P>one, as I had promised to do so:&nbsp; though, resisting all entreaties



<P>and inducements to prolong my visit further, I insisted upon



<P>departing the next morning; affirming that my mother would be



<P>lonely without me, and that she impatiently expected my return.



<P>Nevertheless, it was with a heavy heart that I bade adieu to poor



<P>Lady Ashby, and left her in her princely home.&nbsp; It was no slight



<P>additional proof of her unhappiness, that she should so cling to



<P>the consolation of my presence, and earnestly desire the company of



<P>one whose general tastes and ideas were so little congenial to her



<P>own - whom she had completely forgotten in her hour of prosperity,



<P>and whose presence would be rather a nuisance than a pleasure, if



<P>she could but have half her heart's desire.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter22</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XXII - THE VISIT</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>ASHBY PARK was certainly a very delightful residence.&nbsp; The mansion



<P>was stately without, commodious and elegant within; the park was



<P>spacious and beautiful, chiefly on account of its magnificent old



<P>trees, its stately herds of deer, its broad sheet of water, and the



<P>ancient woods that stretched beyond it:&nbsp; for there was no broken



<P>ground to give variety to the landscape, and but very little of



<P>that undulating swell which adds so greatly to the charm of park



<P>scenery.&nbsp; And so, this was the place Rosalie Murray had so longed



<P>to call her own, that she must have a share of it, on whatever



<P>terms it might be offered - whatever price was to be paid for the



<P>title of mistress, and whoever was to be her partner in the honour



<P>and bliss of such a possession!&nbsp; Well I am not disposed to censure



<P>her now.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She received me very kindly; and, though I was a poor clergyman's



<P>daughter, a governess, and a schoolmistress, she welcomed me with



<P>unaffected pleasure to her home; and - what surprised me rather -



<P>took some pains to make my visit agreeable.&nbsp; I could see, it is



<P>true, that she expected me to be greatly struck with the



<P>magnificence that surrounded her; and, I confess, I was rather



<P>annoyed at her evident efforts to reassure me, and prevent me from



<P>being overwhelmed by so much grandeur - too much awed at the idea



<P>of encountering her husband and mother-in-law, or too much ashamed



<P>of my own humble appearance.&nbsp; I was not ashamed of it at all; for,



<P>though plain, I had taken good care not to shabby or mean, and



<P>should have been pretty considerably at my ease, if my



<P>condescending hostess had not taken such manifest pains to make me



<P>so; and, as for the magnificence that surrounded her, nothing that



<P>met my eyes struck me or affected me half so much as her own



<P>altered appearance.&nbsp; Whether from the influence of fashionable



<P>dissipation, or some other evil, a space of little more than twelve



<P>months had had the effect that might be expected from as many



<P>years, in reducing the plumpness of her form, the freshness of her



<P>complexion, the vivacity of her movements, and the exuberance of



<P>her spirits.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I wished to know if she was unhappy; but I felt it was not my



<P>province to inquire:&nbsp; I might endeavour to win her confidence;

but,



<P>if she chose to conceal her matrimonial cares from me, I would



<P>trouble her with no obtrusive questions.&nbsp; I, therefore, at first,



<P>confined myself to a few general inquiries about her health and



<P>welfare, and a few commendations on the beauty of the park, and of



<P>the little girl that should have been a boy:&nbsp; a small delicate



<P>infant of seven or eight weeks old, whom its mother seemed to



<P>regard with no remarkable degree of interest or affection, though



<P>full as much as I expected her to show.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Shortly after my arrival, she commissioned her maid to conduct me



<P>to my room and see that I had everything I wanted; it was a small,



<P>unpretending, but sufficiently comfortable apartment.&nbsp; When I



<P>descended thence - having divested myself of all travelling



<P>encumbrances, and arranged my toilet with due consideration for the



<P>feelings of my lady hostess, she conducted me herself to the room I



<P>was to occupy when I chose to be alone, or when she was engaged



<P>with visitors, or obliged to be with her mother-in-law, or



<P>otherwise prevented, as she said, from enjoying the pleasure of my



<P>society.&nbsp; It was a quiet, tidy little sitting-room; and I was not



<P>sorry to be provided with such a harbour of refuge.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And some time,' said she, 'I will show you the library:&nbsp; I never



<P>examined its shelves, but, I daresay, it is full of wise books; and



<P>you may go and burrow among them whenever you please.&nbsp; And now

you



<P>shall have some tea - it will soon be dinner-time, but I thought,



<P>as you were accustomed to dine at one, you would perhaps like



<P>better to have a cup of tea about this time, and to dine when we



<P>lunch:&nbsp; and then, you know, you can have your tea in this room,

and



<P>that will save you from having to dine with Lady Ashby and Sir



<P>Thomas:&nbsp; which would be rather awkward - at least, not awkward,

but



<P>rather - a - you know what I mean.&nbsp; I thought you mightn't like

it



<P>so well - especially as we may have other ladies and gentlemen to



<P>dine with us occasionally.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Certainly,' said I, 'I would much rather have it as you say, and,



<P>if you have no objection, I should prefer having all my meals in



<P>this room.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why so?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Because, I imagine, it would be more agreeable to Lady Ashby and



<P>Sir Thomas.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Nothing of the kind.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'At any rate it would be more agreeable to me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She made some faint objections, but soon conceded; and I could see



<P>that the proposal was a considerable relief to her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Now, come into the drawing-room,' said she.&nbsp; 'There's the dressing



<P>bell; but I won't go yet:&nbsp; it's no use dressing when there's no

one



<P>to see you; and I want to have a little discourse.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The drawing-room was certainly an imposing apartment, and very



<P>elegantly furnished; but I saw its young mistress glance towards me



<P>as we entered, as if to notice how I was impressed by the



<P>spectacle, and accordingly I determined to preserve an aspect of



<P>stony indifference, as if I saw nothing at all remarkable.&nbsp; But



<P>this was only for a moment:&nbsp; immediately conscience whispered,

'Why



<P>should I disappoint her to save my pride?&nbsp; No - rather let me



<P>sacrifice my pride to give her a little innocent gratification.'



<P>And I honestly looked round, and told her it was a noble room, and



<P>very tastefully furnished.&nbsp; She said little, but I saw she was



<P>pleased.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She showed me her fat French poodle, that lay curled up on a silk



<P>cushion, and the two fine Italian paintings:&nbsp; which, however, she



<P>would not give me time to examine, but, saying I must look at them



<P>some other day, insisted upon my admiring the little jewelled watch



<P>she had purchased in Geneva; and then she took me round the room to



<P>point out sundry articles of VERTU she had brought from Italy:&nbsp;

an



<P>elegant little timepiece, and several busts, small graceful



<P>figures, and vases, all beautifully carved in white marble.&nbsp; She



<P>spoke of these with animation, and heard my admiring comments with



<P>a smile of pleasure:&nbsp; that soon, however, vanished, and was



<P>followed by a melancholy sigh; as if in consideration of the



<P>insufficiency of all such baubles to the happiness of the human



<P>heart, and their woeful inability to supply its insatiate demands.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then, stretching herself upon a couch, she motioned me to a



<P>capacious easy-chair that stood opposite - not before the fire, but



<P>before a wide open window; for it was summer, be it remembered; a



<P>sweet, warm evening in the latter half of June.&nbsp; I sat for a moment



<P>in silence, enjoying the still, pure air, and the delightful



<P>prospect of the park that lay before me, rich in verdure and



<P>foliage, and basking in yellow sunshine, relieved by the long



<P>shadows of declining day.&nbsp; But I must take advantage of this pause:



<P>I had inquiries to make, and, like the substance of a lady's



<P>postscript, the most important must come last.&nbsp; So I began with



<P>asking after Mr. and Mrs. Murray, and Miss Matilda and the young



<P>gentlemen.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I was told that papa had the gout, which made him very ferocious;



<P>and that he would not give up his choice wines, and his substantial



<P>dinners and suppers, and had quarrelled with his physician, because



<P>the latter had dared to say that no medicine could cure him while



<P>he lived so freely; that mamma and the rest were well.&nbsp; Matilda

was



<P>still wild and reckless, but she had got a fashionable governess,



<P>and was considerably improved in her manners, and soon to be



<P>introduced to the world; and John and Charles (now at home for the



<P>holidays) were, by all accounts, 'fine, bold, unruly, mischievous



<P>boys.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And how are the other people getting on?' said I - 'the Greens,



<P>for instance?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Ah! Mr. Green is heart-broken, you know,' replied she, with a



<P>languid smile:&nbsp; 'he hasn't got over his disappointment yet, and



<P>never will, I suppose.&nbsp; He's doomed to be an old bachelor; and

his



<P>sisters are doing their best to get married.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And the Melthams?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, they're jogging on as usual, I suppose:&nbsp; but I know very



<P>little about any of them - except Harry,' said she, blushing



<P>slightly, and smiling again.&nbsp; 'I saw a great deal of him while

we



<P>were in London; for, as soon as he heard we were there, he came up



<P>under pretence of visiting his brother, and either followed me,



<P>like a shadow, wherever I went, or met me, like a reflection, at



<P>every turn.&nbsp; You needn't look so shocked, Miss Grey; I was very



<P>discreet, I assure you, but, you know, one can't help being



<P>admired.&nbsp; Poor fellow!&nbsp; He was not my only worshipper; though

he



<P>was certainly the most conspicuous, and, I think, the most devoted



<P>among them all.&nbsp; And that detestable - ahem - and Sir Thomas chose



<P>to take offence at him - or my profuse expenditure, or something -



<P>I don't exactly know what - and hurried me down to the country at a



<P>moment's notice; where I'm to play the hermit, I suppose, for



<P>life.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And she bit her lip, and frowned vindictively upon the fair domain



<P>she had once so coveted to call her own.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And Mr. Hatfield,' said I, 'what is become of him?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Again she brightened up, and answered gaily - 'Oh! he made up to an



<P>elderly spinster, and married her, not long since; weighing her



<P>heavy purse against her faded charms, and expecting to find that



<P>solace in gold which was denied him in love - ha, ha!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, and I think that's all - except Mr. Weston:&nbsp; what is he



<P>doing?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I don't know, I'm sure.&nbsp; He's gone from Horton.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'How long since? and where is he gone to?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I know nothing about him,' replied she, yawning - 'except that he



<P>went about a month ago - I never asked where' (I would have asked



<P>whether it was to a living or merely another curacy, but thought it



<P>better not); 'and the people made a great rout about his leaving,'



<P>continued she, 'much to Mr. Hatfield's displeasure; for Hatfield



<P>didn't like him, because he had too much influence with the common



<P>people, and because he was not sufficiently tractable and



<P>submissive to him - and for some other unpardonable sins, I don't



<P>know what.&nbsp; But now I positively must go and dress:&nbsp; the second



<P>bell will ring directly, and if I come to dinner in this guise, I



<P>shall never hear the end of it from Lady Ashby.&nbsp; It's a strange



<P>thing one can't be mistress in one's own house!&nbsp; Just ring the



<P>bell, and I'll send for my maid, and tell them to get you some tea.



<P>Only think of that intolerable woman - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Who - your maid?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No; - my mother-in-law - and my unfortunate mistake!&nbsp; Instead

of



<P>letting her take herself off to some other house, as she offered to



<P>do when I married, I was fool enough to ask her to live here still,



<P>and direct the affairs of the house for me; because, in the first



<P>place, I hoped we should spend the greater part of the year, in



<P>town, and in the second place, being so young and inexperienced, I



<P>was frightened at the idea of having a houseful of servants to



<P>manage, and dinners to order, and parties to entertain, and all the



<P>rest of it, and I thought she might assist me with her experience;



<P>never dreaming she would prove a usurper, a tyrant, an incubus, a



<P>spy, and everything else that's detestable.&nbsp; I wish she was dead!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She then turned to give her orders to the footman, who had been



<P>standing bolt upright within the door for the last half minute, and



<P>had heard the latter part of her animadversions; and, of course,



<P>made his own reflections upon them, notwithstanding the inflexible,



<P>wooden countenance he thought proper to preserve in the drawing-



<P>room.&nbsp; On my remarking afterwards that he must have heard her,

she



<P>replied - 'Oh, no matter!&nbsp; I never care about the footmen; they're



<P>mere automatons:&nbsp; it's nothing to them what their superiors say

or



<P>do; they won't dare to repeat it; and as to what they think - if



<P>they presume to think at all - of course, nobody cares for that.



<P>It would be a pretty thing indeed, it we were to be tongue-tied by



<P>our servants!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So saying, she ran off to make her hasty toilet, leaving me to



<P>pilot my way back to my sitting-room, where, in due time, I was



<P>served with a cup of tea.&nbsp; After that, I sat musing on Lady Ashby's



<P>past and present condition; and on what little information I had



<P>obtained respecting Mr. Weston, and the small chance there was of



<P>ever seeing or hearing anything more of him throughout my quiet,



<P>drab-colour life:&nbsp; which, henceforth, seemed to offer no



<P>alternative between positive rainy days, and days of dull grey



<P>clouds without downfall.&nbsp; At length, however, I began to weary

of



<P>my thoughts, and to wish I knew where to find the library my



<P>hostess had spoken of; and to wonder whether I was to remain there



<P>doing nothing till bedtime.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As I was not rich enough to possess a watch, I could not tell how



<P>time was passing, except by observing the slowly lengthening



<P>shadows from the window; which presented a side view, including a



<P>corner of the park, a clump of trees whose topmost branches had



<P>been colonized by an innumerable company of noisy rooks, and a high



<P>wall with a massive wooden gate:&nbsp; no doubt communicating with the



<P>stable-yard, as a broad carriage-road swept up to it from the park.



<P>The shadow of this wall soon took posession of the whole of the



<P>ground as far as I could see, forcing the golden sunlight to



<P>retreat inch by inch, and at last take refuge in the very tops of



<P>the trees.&nbsp; Ere long, even they were left in shadow - the shadow

of



<P>the distant hills, or of the earth itself; and, in sympathy for the



<P>busy citizens of the rookery, I regretted to see their habitation,



<P>so lately bathed in glorious light, reduced to the sombre, work-a-



<P>day hue of the lower world, or of my own world within.&nbsp; For a



<P>moment, such birds as soared above the rest might still receive the



<P>lustre on their wings, which imparted to their sable plumage the



<P>hue and brilliance of deep red gold; at last, that too departed.



<P>Twilight came stealing on; the rooks became more quiet; I became



<P>more weary, and wished I were going home to-morrow.&nbsp; At length

it



<P>grew dark; and I was thinking of ringing for a candle, and betaking



<P>myself to bed, when my hostess appeared, with many apologies for



<P>having neglected me so long, and laying all the blame upon that



<P>'nasty old woman,' as she called her mother-in-law.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'If I didn't sit with her in the drawing-room while Sir Thomas is



<P>taking his wine,' said she, 'she would never forgive me; and then,



<P>if I leave the room the instant he comes - as I have done once or



<P>twice - it is an unpardonable offence against her dear Thomas.&nbsp;

SHE



<P>never showed such disrespect to HER husband:&nbsp; and as for affection,



<P>wives never think of that now-a-days, she supposes:&nbsp; but things



<P>were different in HER time - as if there was any good to be done by



<P>staying in the room, when he does nothing but grumble and scold



<P>when he's in a bad humour, talk disgusting nonsense when he's in a



<P>good one, and go to sleep on the sofa when he's too stupid for



<P>either; which is most frequently the case now, when he has nothing



<P>to do but to sot over his wine.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But could you not try to occupy his mind with something better;



<P>and engage him to give up such habits?&nbsp; I'm sure you have powers

of



<P>persuasion, and qualifications for amusing a gentleman, which many



<P>ladies would be glad to possess.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And so you think I would lay myself out for his amusement!&nbsp; No:



<P>that's not MY idea of a wife.&nbsp; It's the husband's part to please



<P>the wife, not hers to please him; and if he isn't satisfied with



<P>her as she is - and thankful to possess her too - he isn't worthy



<P>of her, that's all.&nbsp; And as for persuasion, I assure you I shan't



<P>trouble myself with that:&nbsp; I've enough to do to bear with him as

he



<P>is, without attempting to work a reform.&nbsp; But I'm sorry I left

you



<P>so long alone, Miss Grey.&nbsp; How have you passed the time?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Chiefly in watching the rooks.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Mercy, how dull you must have been!&nbsp; I really must show you the



<P>library; and you must ring for everything you want, just as you



<P>would in an inn, and make yourself comfortable.&nbsp; I have selfish



<P>reasons for wishing to make you happy, because I want you to stay



<P>with me, and not fulfil your horrid threat of running away in a day



<P>or two.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, don't let me keep you out of the drawing-room any longer to-



<P>night, for at present I am tired and wish to go to bed.'



<P>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">
