</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XIII - THE PRIMROSES</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>MISS MURRAY now always went twice to church, for she so loved



<P>admiration that she could not bear to lose a single opportunity of



<P>obtaining it; and she was so sure of it wherever she showed



<P>herself, that, whether Harry Meltham and Mr. Green were there or



<P>not, there was certain to be somebody present who would not be



<P>insensible to her charms, besides the Rector, whose official



<P>capacity generally obliged him to attend.&nbsp; Usually, also, if the



<P>weather permitted, both she and her sister would walk home;



<P>Matilda, because she hated the confinement of the carriage; she,



<P>because she disliked the privacy of it, and enjoyed the company



<P>that generally enlivened the first mile of the journey in walking



<P>from the church to Mr. Green's park-gates:&nbsp; near which commenced



<P>the private road to Horton Lodge, which lay in the opposite



<P>direction, while the highway conducted in a straightforward course



<P>to the still more distant mansion of Sir Hugh Meltham.&nbsp; Thus there



<P>was always a chance of being accompanied, so far, either by Harry



<P>Meltham, with or without Miss Meltham, or Mr. Green, with perhaps



<P>one or both of his sisters, and any gentlemen visitors they might



<P>have.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Whether I walked with the young ladies or rode with their parents,



<P>depended upon their own capricious will:&nbsp; if they chose to 'take'



<P>me, I went; if, for reasons best known to themselves, they chose to



<P>go alone, I took my seat in the carriage.&nbsp; I liked walking better,



<P>but a sense of reluctance to obtrude my presence on anyone who did



<P>not desire it, always kept me passive on these and similar



<P>occasions; and I never inquired into the causes of their varying



<P>whims.&nbsp; Indeed, this was the best policy - for to submit and oblige



<P>was the governess's part, to consult their own pleasure was that of



<P>the pupils.&nbsp; But when I did walk, the first half of journey was



<P>generally a great nuisance to me.&nbsp; As none of the before-mentioned



<P>ladies and gentlemen ever noticed me, it was disagreeable to walk



<P>beside them, as if listening to what they said, or wishing to be



<P>thought one of them, while they talked over me, or across; and if



<P>their eyes, in speaking, chanced to fall on me, it seemed as if



<P>they looked on vacancy - as if they either did not see me, or were



<P>very desirous to make it appear so.&nbsp; It was disagreeable, too,

to



<P>walk behind, and thus appear to acknowledge my own inferiority;



<P>for, in truth, I considered myself pretty nearly as good as the



<P>best of them, and wished them to know that I did so, and not to



<P>imagine that I looked upon myself as a mere domestic, who knew her



<P>own place too well to walk beside such fine ladies and gentlemen as



<P>they were - though her young ladies might choose to have her with



<P>them, and even condescend to converse with her when no better



<P>company were at hand.&nbsp; Thus - I am almost ashamed to confess it

-



<P>but indeed I gave myself no little trouble in my endeavours (if I



<P>did keep up with them) to appear perfectly unconscious or



<P>regardless of their presence, as if I were wholly absorbed in my



<P>own reflections, or the contemplation of surrounding objects; or,



<P>if I lingered behind, it was some bird or insect, some tree or



<P>flower, that attracted my attention, and having duly examined that,



<P>I would pursue my walk alone, at a leisurely pace, until my pupils



<P>had bidden adieu to their companions and turned off into the quiet



<P>private road.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One such occasion I particularly well remember; it was a lovely



<P>afternoon about the close of March; Mr. Green and his sisters had



<P>sent their carriage back empty, in order to enjoy the bright



<P>sunshine and balmy air in a sociable walk home along with their



<P>visitors, Captain Somebody and Lieutenant Somebody-else (a couple



<P>of military fops), and the Misses Murray, who, of course, contrived



<P>to join them.&nbsp; Such a party was highly agreeable to Rosalie; but



<P>not finding it equally suitable to my taste, I presently fell back,



<P>and began to botanise and entomologise along the green banks and



<P>budding hedges, till the company was considerably in advance of me,



<P>and I could hear the sweet song of the happy lark; then my spirit



<P>of misanthropy began to melt away beneath the soft, pure air and



<P>genial sunshine; but sad thoughts of early childhood, and yearnings



<P>for departed joys, or for a brighter future lot, arose instead.&nbsp;

As



<P>my eyes wandered over the steep banks covered with young grass and



<P>green-leaved plants, and surmounted by budding hedges, I longed



<P>intensely for some familiar flower that might recall the woody



<P>dales or green hill-sides of home:&nbsp; the brown moorlands, of course,



<P>were out of the question.&nbsp; Such a discovery would make my eyes

gush



<P>out with water, no doubt; but that was one of my greatest



<P>enjoyments now.&nbsp; At length I descried, high up between the twisted



<P>roots of an oak, three lovely primroses, peeping so sweetly from



<P>their hiding-place that the tears already started at the sight; but



<P>they grew so high above me, that I tried in vain to gather one or



<P>two, to dream over and to carry with me:&nbsp; I could not reach them



<P>unless I climbed the bank, which I was deterred from doing by



<P>hearing a footstep at that moment behind me, and was, therefore,



<P>about to turn away, when I was startled by the words, 'Allow me to



<P>gather them for you, Miss Grey,' spoken in the grave, low tones of



<P>a well-known voice.&nbsp; Immediately the flowers were gathered, and

in



<P>my hand.&nbsp; It was Mr. Weston, of course - who else would trouble



<P>himself to do so much for ME?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I thanked him; whether warmly or coldly, I cannot tell:&nbsp; but



<P>certain I am that I did not express half the gratitude I felt.&nbsp;

It



<P>was foolish, perhaps, to feel any gratitude at all; but it seemed



<P>to me, at that moment, as if this were a remarkable instance of his



<P>good-nature:&nbsp; an act of kindness, which I could not repay, but



<P>never should forget:&nbsp; so utterly unaccustomed was I to receive

such



<P>civilities, so little prepared to expect them from anyone within



<P>fifty miles of Horton Lodge.&nbsp; Yet this did not prevent me from



<P>feeling a little uncomfortable in his presence; and I proceeded to



<P>follow my pupils at a much quicker pace than before; though,



<P>perhaps, if Mr. Weston had taken the hint, and let me pass without



<P>another word, I might have repeated it an hour after:&nbsp; but he did



<P>not.&nbsp; A somewhat rapid walk for me was but an ordinary pace for



<P>him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Your young ladies have left you alone,' said he.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, they are occupied with more agreeable company.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Then don't trouble yourself to overtake them.'&nbsp; I slackened my



<P>pace; but next moment regretted having done so:&nbsp; my companion did



<P>not speak; and I had nothing in the world to say, and feared he



<P>might be in the same predicament.&nbsp; At length, however, he broke

the



<P>pause by asking, with a certain quiet abruptness peculiar to



<P>himself, if I liked flowers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes; very much,' I answered, 'wild-flowers especially.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I like wild-flowers,' said he; 'others I don't care about, because



<P>I have no particular associations connected with them - except one



<P>or two.&nbsp; What are your favourite flowers?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Primroses, blue-bells, and heath-blossoms.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Not violets?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No; because, as you say, I have no particular associations



<P>connected with them; for there are no sweet violets among the hills



<P>and valleys round my home.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It must be a great consolation to you to have a home, Miss Grey,'



<P>observed my companion after a short pause:&nbsp; 'however remote, or



<P>however seldom visited, still it is something to look to.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It is so much that I think I could not live without it,' replied



<P>I, with an enthusiasm of which I immediately repented; for I



<P>thought it must have sounded essentially silly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, yes, you could,' said he, with a thoughtful smile.&nbsp; 'The ties



<P>that bind us to life are tougher than you imagine, or than anyone



<P>can who has not felt how roughly they may be pulled without



<P>breaking.&nbsp; You might be miserable without a home, but even YOU



<P>could live; and not so miserably as you suppose.&nbsp; The human heart



<P>is like india-rubber; a little swells it, but a great deal will not



<P>burst it.&nbsp; If "little more than nothing will disturb it, little



<P>less than all things will suffice" to break it.&nbsp; As in the outer



<P>members of our frame, there is a vital power inherent in itself



<P>that strengthens it against external violence.&nbsp; Every blow that



<P>shakes it will serve to harden it against a future stroke; as



<P>constant labour thickens the skin of the hand, and strengthens its



<P>muscles instead of wasting them away:&nbsp; so that a day of arduous



<P>toil, that might excoriate a lady's palm, would make no sensible



<P>impression on that of a hardy ploughman.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I speak from experience - partly my own.&nbsp; There was a time when

I



<P>thought as you do - at least, I was fully persuaded that home and



<P>its affections were the only things that made life tolerable:



<P>that, if deprived of these, existence would become a burden hard to



<P>be endured; but now I have no home - unless you would dignify my



<P>two hired rooms at Horton by such a name; - and not twelve months



<P>ago I lost the last and dearest of my early friends; and yet, not



<P>only I live, but I am not wholly destitute of hope and comfort,



<P>even for this life:&nbsp; though I must acknowledge that I can seldom



<P>enter even an humble cottage at the close of day, and see its



<P>inhabitants peaceably gathered around their cheerful hearth,



<P>without a feeling ALMOST of envy at their domestic enjoyment.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You don't know what happiness lies before you yet,' said I:&nbsp; 'you



<P>are now only in the commencement of your journey.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'The best of happiness,' replied he, 'is mine already - the power



<P>and the will to be useful.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>We now approached a stile communicating with a footpath that



<P>conducted to a farm-house, where, I suppose, Mr. Weston purposed to



<P>make himself 'useful;' for he presently took leave of me, crossed



<P>the stile, and traversed the path with his usual firm, elastic



<P>tread, leaving me to ponder his words as I continued my course



<P>alone.&nbsp; I had heard before that he had lost his mother not many



<P>months before he came.&nbsp; She then was the last and dearest of his



<P>early friends; and he had NO HOME.&nbsp; I pitied him from my heart:&nbsp;

I



<P>almost wept for sympathy.&nbsp; And this, I thought, accounted for the



<P>shade of premature thoughtfulness that so frequently clouded his



<P>brow, and obtained for him the reputation of a morose and sullen



<P>disposition with the charitable Miss Murray and all her kin.



<P>'But,' thought I, 'he is not so miserable as I should be under such



<P>a deprivation:&nbsp; he leads an active life; and a wide field for



<P>useful exertion lies before him.&nbsp; He can MAKE friends; and he can



<P>make a home too, if he pleases; and, doubtless, he will please some



<P>time.&nbsp; God grant the partner of that home may be worthy of his



<P>choice, and make it a happy one - such a home as he deserves to



<P>have!&nbsp; And how delightful it would be to - '&nbsp; But no matter

what I



<P>thought.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I began this book with the intention of concealing nothing; that



<P>those who liked might have the benefit of perusing a fellow-



<P>creature's heart:&nbsp; but we have some thoughts that all the angels

in



<P>heaven are welcome to behold, but not our brother-men - not even



<P>the best and kindest amongst them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>By this time the Greens had taken themselves to their own abode,



<P>and the Murrays had turned down the private road, whither I



<P>hastened to follow them.&nbsp; I found the two girls warm in an animated



<P>discussion on the respective merits of the two young officers; but



<P>on seeing me Rosalie broke off in the middle of a sentence to



<P>exclaim, with malicious glee -

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh-ho, Miss Grey! you're come at last, are you?&nbsp; No WONDER you



<P>lingered so long behind; and no WONDER you always stand up so



<P>vigorously for Mr. Weston when I abuse him.&nbsp; Ah-ha!&nbsp; I see

it all



<P>now!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Now, come, Miss Murray, don't be foolish,' said I, attempting a



<P>good-natured laugh; 'you know such nonsense can make no impression



<P>on me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But she still went on talking such intolerable stuff - her sister



<P>helping her with appropriate fiction coined for the occasion - that



<P>I thought it necessary to say something in my own justification.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What folly all this is!' I exclaimed.&nbsp; 'If Mr. Weston's road



<P>happened to be the same as mine for a few yards, and if he chose to



<P>exchange a word or two in passing, what is there so remarkable in



<P>that?&nbsp; I assure you, I never spoke to him before:&nbsp; except

once.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Where? where? and when?' cried they eagerly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'In Nancy's cottage.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Ah-ha! you've met him there, have you?' exclaimed Rosalie, with



<P>exultant laughter.&nbsp; 'Ah! now, Matilda, I've found out why she's

so



<P>fond of going to Nancy Brown's!&nbsp; She goes there to flirt with Mr.



<P>Weston.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Really, that is not worth contradicting - I only saw him there



<P>once, I tell you - and how could I know he was coming?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Irritated as I was at their foolish mirth and vexatious



<P>imputations, the uneasiness did not continue long:&nbsp; when they had



<P>had their laugh out, they returned again to the captain and



<P>lieutenant; and, while they disputed and commented upon them, my



<P>indignation rapidly cooled; the cause of it was quickly forgotten,



<P>and I turned my thoughts into a pleasanter channel.&nbsp; Thus we



<P>proceeded up the park, and entered the hall; and as I ascended the



<P>stairs to my own chamber, I had but one thought within me:&nbsp; my



<P>heart was filled to overflowing with one single earnest wish.



<P>Having entered the room, and shut the door, I fell upon my knees



<P>and offered up a fervent but not impetuous prayer:&nbsp; 'Thy will be



<P>done,' I strove to say throughout; but, 'Father, all things are



<P>possible with Thee, and may it be Thy will,' was sure to follow.



<P>That wish - that prayer - both men and women would have scorned me



<P>for - 'But, Father, THOU wilt NOT despise!' I said, and felt that



<P>it was true.&nbsp; It seemed to me that another's welfare was at least



<P>as ardently implored for as my own; nay, even THAT was the



<P>principal object of my heart's desire.&nbsp; I might have been deceiving



<P>myself; but that idea gave me confidence to ask, and power to hope



<P>I did not ask in vain.&nbsp; As for the primroses, I kept two of them

in



<P>a glass in my room until they were completely withered, and the



<P>housemaid threw them out; and the petals of the other I pressed



<P>between the leaves of my Bible - I have them still, and mean to



<P>keep them always.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter07</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER VII - HORTON LODGE</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE 31st of January was a wild, tempestuous day:&nbsp; there was a



<P>strong north wind, with a continual storm of snow drifting on the



<P>ground and whirling through the air.&nbsp; My friends would have had

me



<P>delay my departure, but fearful of prejudicing my employers against



<P>me by such want of punctuality at the commencement of my



<P>undertaking, I persisted in keeping the appointment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I will not inflict upon my readers an account of my leaving home on



<P>that dark winter morning:&nbsp; the fond farewells, the long, long



<P>journey to O-, the solitary waitings in inns for coaches or trains



<P>- for there were some railways then - and, finally, the meeting at



<P>O- with Mr. Murray's servant, who had been sent with the phaeton to



<P>drive me from thence to Horton Lodge.&nbsp; I will just state that the



<P>heavy snow had thrown such impediments in the way of both horses



<P>and steam-engines, that it was dark some hours before I reached my



<P>journey's end, and that a most bewildering storm came on at last,



<P>which made the few miles' space between O- and Horton Lodge a long



<P>and formidable passage.&nbsp; I sat resigned, with the cold, sharp snow



<P>drifting through my veil and filling my lap, seeing nothing, and



<P>wondering how the unfortunate horse and driver could make their way



<P>even as well as they did; and indeed it was but a toilsome,



<P>creeping style of progression, to say the best of it.&nbsp; At length

we



<P>paused; and, at the call of the driver, someone unlatched and



<P>rolled back upon their creaking hinges what appeared to be the park



<P>gates.&nbsp; Then we proceeded along a smoother road, whence,



<P>occasionally, I perceived some huge, hoary mass gleaming through



<P>the darkness, which I took to be a portion of a snow-clad tree.



<P>After a considerable time we paused again, before the stately



<P>portico of a large house with long windows descending to the



<P>ground.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I rose with some difficulty from under the superincumbent



<P>snowdrift, and alighted from the carriage, expecting that a kind



<P>and hospitable reception would indemnify me for the toils and



<P>hardships of the day.&nbsp; A gentleman person in black opened the door,



<P>and admitted me into a spacious hall, lighted by an amber-coloured



<P>lamp suspended from the ceiling; he led me through this, along a



<P>passage, and opening the door of a back room, told me that was the



<P>schoolroom.&nbsp; I entered, and found two young ladies and two young



<P>gentlemen - my future pupils, I supposed.&nbsp; After a formal greeting,



<P>the elder girl, who was trifling over a piece of canvas and a



<P>basket of German wools, asked if I should like to go upstairs.&nbsp;

I



<P>replied in the affirmative, of course.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Matilda, take a candle, and show her her room,' said she.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Miss Matilda, a strapping hoyden of about fourteen, with a short



<P>frock and trousers, shrugged her shoulders and made a slight



<P>grimace, but took a candle and proceeded before me up the back



<P>stairs (a long, steep, double flight), and through a long, narrow



<P>passage, to a small but tolerably comfortable room.&nbsp; She then asked



<P>me if I would take some tea or coffee.&nbsp; I was about to answer No;



<P>but remembering that I had taken nothing since seven o'clock that



<P>morning, and feeling faint in consequence, I said I would take a



<P>cup of tea.&nbsp; Saying she would tell 'Brown,' the young lady



<P>departed; and by the time I had divested myself of my heavy, wet



<P>cloak, shawl, bonnet, &amp;c., a mincing damsel came to say the young



<P>ladies desired to know whether I would take my tea up there or in



<P>the schoolroom.&nbsp; Under the plea of fatigue I chose to take it



<P>there.&nbsp; She withdrew; and, after a while, returned again with a



<P>small tea-tray, and placed it on the chest of drawers, which served



<P>as a dressing-table.&nbsp; Having civilly thanked her, I asked at what



<P>time I should be expected to rise in the morning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'The young ladies and gentlemen breakfast at half-past eight,



<P>ma'am,' said she; 'they rise early; but, as they seldom do any



<P>lessons before breakfast, I should think it will do if you rise



<P>soon after seven.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I desired her to be so kind as to call me at seven, and, promising



<P>to do so, she withdrew.&nbsp; Then, having broken my long fast on a

cup



<P>of tea and a little thin bread and butter, I sat down beside the



<P>small, smouldering fire, and amused myself with a hearty fit of



<P>crying; after which, I said my prayers, and then, feeling



<P>considerably relieved, began to prepare for bed.&nbsp; Finding that

none



<P>of my luggage was brought up, I instituted a search for the bell;



<P>and failing to discover any signs of such a convenience in any



<P>corner of the room, I took my candle and ventured through the long



<P>passage, and down the steep stairs, on a voyage of discovery.



<P>Meeting a well-dressed female on the way, I told her what I wanted;



<P>but not without considerable hesitation, as I was not quite sure



<P>whether it was one of the upper servants, or Mrs. Murray herself:



<P>it happened, however, to be the lady's-maid.&nbsp; With the air of one



<P>conferring an unusual favour, she vouchsafed to undertake the



<P>sending up of my things; and when I had re-entered my room, and



<P>waited and wondered a long time (greatly fearing that she had



<P>forgotten or neglected to perform her promise, and doubting whether



<P>to keep waiting or go to bed, or go down again), my hopes, at



<P>length, were revived by the sound of voices and laughter,



<P>accompanied by the tramp of feet along the passage; and presently



<P>the luggage was brought in by a rough-looking maid and a man,



<P>neither of them very respectful in their demeanour to me.&nbsp; Having



<P>shut the door upon their retiring footsteps, and unpacked a few of



<P>my things, I betook myself to rest; gladly enough, for I was weary



<P>in body and mind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was with a strange feeling of desolation, mingled with a strong



<P>sense of the novelty of my situation, and a joyless kind of



<P>curiosity concerning what was yet unknown, that I awoke the next



<P>morning; feeling like one whirled away by enchantment, and suddenly



<P>dropped from the clouds into a remote and unknown land, widely and



<P>completely isolated from all he had ever seen or known before; or



<P>like a thistle-seed borne on the wind to some strange nook of



<P>uncongenial soil, where it must lie long enough before it can take



<P>root and germinate, extracting nourishment from what appears so



<P>alien to its nature:&nbsp; if, indeed, it ever can.&nbsp; But this gives

no



<P>proper idea of my feelings at all; and no one that has not lived



<P>such a retired, stationary life as mine, can possibly imagine what



<P>they were:&nbsp; hardly even if he has known what it is to awake some



<P>morning, and find himself in Port Nelson, in New Zealand, with a



<P>world of waters between himself and all that knew him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I shall not soon forget the peculiar feeling with which I raised my



<P>blind and looked out upon the unknown world:&nbsp; a wide, white



<P>wilderness was all that met my gaze; a waste of

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Deserts tossed in snow,



<P>And heavy laden groves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I descended to the schoolroom with no remarkable eagerness to join



<P>my pupils, though not without some feeling of curiosity respecting



<P>what a further acquaintance would reveal.&nbsp; One thing, among others



<P>of more obvious importance, I determined with myself - I must begin



<P>with calling them Miss and Master.&nbsp; It seemed to me a chilling

and



<P>unnatural piece of punctilio between the children of a family and



<P>their instructor and daily companion; especially where the former



<P>were in their early childhood, as at Wellwood House; but even



<P>there, my calling the little Bloomfields by their simple names had



<P>been regarded as an offensive liberty:&nbsp; as their parents had taken



<P>care to show me, by carefully designating them MASTER and MISS



<P>Bloomfield, &amp;c., in speaking to me.&nbsp; I had been very slow to

take



<P>the hint, because the whole affair struck me as so very absurd; but



<P>now I determined to be wiser, and begin at once with as much form



<P>and ceremony as any member of the family would be likely to



<P>require:&nbsp; and, indeed, the children being so much older, there



<P>would be less difficulty; though the little words Miss and Master



<P>seemed to have a surprising effect in repressing all familiar,



<P>open-hearted kindness, and extinguishing every gleam of cordiality



<P>that might arise between us.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As I cannot, like Dogberry, find it in my heart to bestow all my



<P>tediousness upon the reader, I will not go on to bore him with a



<P>minute detail of all the discoveries and proceedings of this and



<P>the following day.&nbsp; No doubt he will be amply satisfied with a



<P>slight sketch of the different members of the family, and a general



<P>view of the first year or two of my sojourn among them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To begin with the head:&nbsp; Mr. Murray was, by all accounts, a



<P>blustering, roystering, country squire:&nbsp; a devoted fox-hunter,

a



<P>skilful horse-jockey and farrier, an active, practical farmer, and



<P>a hearty BON VIVANT.&nbsp; By all accounts, I say; for, except on



<P>Sundays, when he went to church, I never saw him from month to



<P>month:&nbsp; unless, in crossing the hall or walking in the grounds,

the



<P>figure of a tall, stout gentleman, with scarlet cheeks and crimson



<P>nose, happened to come across me; on which occasions, if he passed



<P>near enough to speak, an unceremonious nod, accompanied by a



<P>'Morning, Miss Grey,' or some such brief salutation, was usually



<P>vouchsafed.&nbsp;&nbsp; Frequently, indeed, his loud laugh reached me

from



<P>afar; and oftener still I heard him swearing and blaspheming



<P>against the footmen, groom, coachman, or some other hapless



<P>dependant.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs. Murray was a handsome, dashing lady of forty, who certainly



<P>required neither rouge nor padding to add to her charms; and whose



<P>chief enjoyments were, or seemed to be, in giving or frequenting



<P>parties, and in dressing at the very top of the fashion.&nbsp; I did

not



<P>see her till eleven o'clock on the morning after my arrival; when



<P>she honoured me with a visit, just as my mother might step into the



<P>kitchen to see a new servant-girl:&nbsp; yet not so, either, for my



<P>mother would have seen her immediately after her arrival, and not



<P>waited till the next day; and, moreover, she would have addressed



<P>her in a more kind and friendly manner, and given her some words of



<P>comfort as well as a plain exposition of her duties; but Mrs.



<P>Murray did neither the one nor the other.&nbsp; She just stepped into



<P>the schoolroom on her return from ordering dinner in the



<P>housekeeper's room, bade me good-morning, stood for two minutes by



<P>the fire, said a few words about the weather and the 'rather rough'



<P>journey I must have had yesterday; petted her youngest child - a



<P>boy of ten - who had just been wiping his mouth and hands on her



<P>gown, after indulging in some savoury morsel from the house-



<P>keeper's store; told me what a sweet, good boy he was; and then



<P>sailed out, with a self-complacent smile upon her face:&nbsp; thinking,



<P>no doubt, that she had done quite enough for the present, and had



<P>been delightfully condescending into the bargain.&nbsp; Her children



<P>evidently held the same opinion, and I alone thought otherwise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After this she looked in upon me once or twice, during the absence



<P>of my pupils, to enlighten me concerning my duties towards them.



<P>For the girls she seemed anxious only to render them as



<P>superficially attractive and showily accomplished as they could



<P>possibly be made, without present trouble or discomfort to



<P>themselves; and I was to act accordingly - to study and strive to



<P>amuse and oblige, instruct, refine, and polish, with the least



<P>possible exertion on their part, and no exercise of authority on



<P>mine.&nbsp; With regard to the two boys, it was much the same; only



<P>instead of accomplishments, I was to get the greatest possible



<P>quantity of Latin grammar and Valpy's Delectus into their heads, in



<P>order to fit them for school - the greatest possible quantity at



<P>least WITHOUT trouble to themselves.&nbsp; John might be a 'little high-



<P>spirited,' and Charles might be a little 'nervous and tedious - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But at all events, Miss Grey,' said she, 'I hope YOU will keep



<P>your temper, and be mild and patient throughout; especially with



<P>the dear little Charles; he is so extremely nervous and



<P>susceptible, and so utterly unaccustomed to anything but the



<P>tenderest treatment.&nbsp; You will excuse my naming these things to



<P>you; for the fact is, I have hitherto found all the governesses,



<P>even the very best of them, faulty in this particular.&nbsp; They wanted



<P>that meek and quiet spirit, which St. Matthew, or some of them,



<P>says is better than the putting on of apparel - you will know the



<P>passage to which I allude, for you are a clergyman's daughter.&nbsp;

But



<P>I have no doubt you will give satisfaction in this respect as well



<P>as the rest.&nbsp; And remember, on all occasions, when any of the young



<P>people do anything improper, if persuasion and gentle remonstrance



<P>will not do, let one of the others come and tell me; for I can



<P>speak to them more plainly than it would be proper for you to do.



<P>And make them as happy as you can, Miss Grey, and I dare say you



<P>will do very well.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I observed that while Mrs. Murray was so extremely solicitous for



<P>the comfort and happiness of her children, and continually talking



<P>about it, she never once mentioned mine; though they were at home,



<P>surrounded by friends, and I an alien among strangers; and I did



<P>not yet know enough of the world, not to be considerably surprised



<P>at this anomaly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Miss Murray, otherwise Rosalie, was about sixteen when I came, and



<P>decidedly a very pretty girl; and in two years longer, as time more



<P>completely developed her form and added grace to her carriage and



<P>deportment, she became positively beautiful; and that in no common



<P>degree.&nbsp; She was tall and slender, yet not thin; perfectly formed,



<P>exquisitely fair, though not without a brilliant, healthy bloom;



<P>her hair, which she wore in a profusion of long ringlets, was of a



<P>very light brown inclining to yellow; her eyes were pale blue, but



<P>so clear and bright that few would wish them darker; the rest of



<P>her features were small, not quite regular, and not remarkably



<P>otherwise:&nbsp; but altogether you could not hesitate to pronounce

her



<P>a very lovely girl.&nbsp; I wish I could say as much for mind and



<P>disposition as I can for her form and face.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Yet think not I have any dreadful disclosures to make:&nbsp; she was



<P>lively, light-hearted, and could be very agreeable, with those who



<P>did not cross her will.&nbsp; Towards me, when I first came, she was



<P>cold and haughty, then insolent and overbearing; but, on a further



<P>acquaintance, she gradually laid aside her airs, and in time became



<P>as deeply attached to me as it was possible for HER to be to one of



<P>my character and position:&nbsp; for she seldom lost sight, for above



<P>half an hour at a time, of the fact of my being a hireling and a



<P>poor curate's daughter.&nbsp; And yet, upon the whole, I believe she



<P>respected me more than she herself was aware of; because I was the



<P>only person in the house who steadily professed good principles,



<P>habitually spoke the truth, and generally endeavoured to make



<P>inclination bow to duty; and this I say, not, of course, in



<P>commendation of myself, but to show the unfortunate state of the



<P>family to which my services were, for the present, devoted.&nbsp; There



<P>was no member of it in whom I regretted this sad want of principle



<P>so much as Miss Murray herself; not only because she had taken a



<P>fancy to me, but because there was so much of what was pleasant and



<P>prepossessing in herself, that, in spite of her failings, I really



<P>liked her - when she did not rouse my indignation, or ruffle my



<P>temper by TOO great a display of her faults.&nbsp; These, however, I



<P>would fain persuade myself were rather the effect of her education



<P>than her disposition:&nbsp; she had never been perfectly taught the



<P>distinction between right and wrong; she had, like her brothers and



<P>sisters, been suffered, from infancy, to tyrannize over nurses,



<P>governesses, and servants; she had not been taught to moderate her



<P>desires, to control her temper or bridle her will, or to sacrifice



<P>her own pleasure for the good of others.&nbsp; Her temper being



<P>naturally good, she was never violent or morose, but from constant



<P>indulgence, and habitual scorn of reason, she was often testy and



<P>capricious; her mind had never been cultivated:&nbsp; her intellect,

at



<P>best, was somewhat shallow; she possessed considerable vivacity,



<P>some quickness of perception, and some talent for music and the



<P>acquisition of languages, but till fifteen she had troubled herself



<P>to acquire nothing; - then the love of display had roused her



<P>faculties, and induced her to apply herself, but only to the more



<P>showy accomplishments.&nbsp; And when I came it was the same:



<P>everything was neglected but French, German, music, singing,



<P>dancing, fancy-work, and a little drawing - such drawing as might



<P>produce the greatest show with the smallest labour, and the



<P>principal parts of which were generally done by me.&nbsp; For music

and



<P>singing, besides my occasional instructions, she had the attendance



<P>of the best master the country afforded; and in these



<P>accomplishments, as well as in dancing, she certainly attained



<P>great proficiency.&nbsp; To music, indeed, she devoted too much of her



<P>time, as, governess though I was, I frequently told her; but her



<P>mother thought that if SHE liked it, she COULD not give too much



<P>time to the acquisition of so attractive an art.&nbsp; Of fancy-work

I



<P>knew nothing but what I gathered from my pupil and my own



<P>observation; but no sooner was I initiated, than she made me useful



<P>in twenty different ways:&nbsp; all the tedious parts of her work were



<P>shifted on to my shoulders; such as stretching the frames,



<P>stitching in the canvas, sorting the wools and silks, putting in



<P>the grounds, counting the stitches, rectifying mistakes, and



<P>finishing the pieces she was tired of.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At sixteen, Miss Murray was something of a romp, yet not more so



<P>than is natural and allowable for a girl of that age, but at



<P>seventeen, that propensity, like all other things, began to give



<P>way to the ruling passion, and soon was swallowed up in the all-



<P>absorbing ambition to attract and dazzle the other sex.&nbsp; But enough



<P>of her:&nbsp; now let us turn to her sister.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Miss Matilda Murray was a veritable hoyden, of whom little need be



<P>said.&nbsp; She was about two years and a half younger than her sister;



<P>her features were larger, her complexion much darker.&nbsp; She might



<P>possibly make a handsome woman; but she was far too big-boned and



<P>awkward ever to be called a pretty girl, and at present she cared



<P>little about it.&nbsp; Rosalie knew all her charms, and thought them



<P>even greater than they were, and valued them more highly than she



<P>ought to have done, had they been three times as great; Matilda



<P>thought she was well enough, but cared little about the matter;



<P>still less did she care about the cultivation of her mind, and the



<P>acquisition of ornamental accomplishments.&nbsp; The manner in which

she



<P>learnt her lessons and practised her music was calculated to drive



<P>any governess to despair.&nbsp; Short and easy as her tasks were, if



<P>done at all, they were slurred over, at any time and in any way;



<P>but generally at the least convenient times, and in the way least



<P>beneficial to herself, and least satisfactory to me:&nbsp; the short



<P>half-hour of practising was horribly strummed through; she,



<P>meantime, unsparingly abusing me, either for interrupting her with



<P>corrections, or for not rectifying her mistakes before they were



<P>made, or something equally unreasonable.&nbsp; Once or twice, I ventured



<P>to remonstrate with her seriously for such irrational conduct; but



<P>on each of those occasions, I received such reprehensive



<P>expostulations from her mother, as convinced me that, if I wished



<P>to keep the situation, I must even let Miss Matilda go on in her



<P>own way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When her lessons were over, however, her ill-humour was generally



<P>over too:&nbsp; while riding her spirited pony, or romping with the

dogs



<P>or her brothers and sister, but especially with her dear brother



<P>John, she was as happy as a lark.&nbsp; As an animal, Matilda was all



<P>right, full of life, vigour, and activity; as an intelligent being,



<P>she was barbarously ignorant, indocile, careless and irrational;



<P>and, consequently, very distressing to one who had the task of



<P>cultivating her understanding, reforming her manners, and aiding



<P>her to acquire those ornamental attainments which, unlike her



<P>sister, she despised as much as the rest.&nbsp; Her mother was partly



<P>aware of her deficiencies, and gave me many a lecture as to how I



<P>should try to form her tastes, and endeavour to rouse and cherish



<P>her dormant vanity; and, by insinuating, skilful flattery, to win



<P>her attention to the desired objects - which I would not do; and



<P>how I should prepare and smooth the path of learning till she could



<P>glide along it without the least exertion to herself:&nbsp; which I



<P>could not, for nothing can be taught to any purpose without some



<P>little exertion on the part of the learner.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As a moral agent, Matilda was reckless, headstrong, violent, and



<P>unamenable to reason.&nbsp; One proof of the deplorable state of her



<P>mind was, that from her father's example she had learned to swear



<P>like a trooper.&nbsp; Her mother was greatly shocked at the 'unlady-like



<P>trick,' and wondered 'how she had picked it up.'&nbsp; 'But you can

soon



<P>break her of it, Miss Grey,' said she:&nbsp; 'it is only a habit; and

if



<P>you will just gently remind her every time she does so, I am sure



<P>she will soon lay it aside.'&nbsp; I not only 'gently reminded' her,

I



<P>tried to impress upon her how wrong it was, and how distressing to



<P>the ears of decent people:&nbsp; but all in vain:&nbsp; I was only answered



<P>by a careless laugh, and, 'Oh, Miss Grey, how shocked you are!&nbsp;

I'm



<P>so glad!' or, 'Well!&nbsp; I can't help it; papa shouldn't have taught



<P>me:&nbsp; I learned it all from him; and maybe a bit from the coachman.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her brother John, ALIAS Master Murray, was about eleven when I



<P>came:&nbsp; a fine, stout, healthy boy, frank and good-natured in the



<P>main, and might have been a decent lad had he been properly



<P>educated; but now he was as rough as a young bear, boisterous,



<P>unruly, unprincipled, untaught, unteachable - at least, for a



<P>governess under his mother's eye.&nbsp; His masters at school might

be



<P>able to manage him better - for to school he was sent, greatly to



<P>my relief, in the course of a year; in a state, it is true, of



<P>scandalous ignorance as to Latin, as well as the more useful though



<P>more neglected things:&nbsp; and this, doubtless, would all be laid

to



<P>the account of his education having been entrusted to an ignorant



<P>female teacher, who had presumed to take in hand what she was



<P>wholly incompetent to perform.&nbsp; I was not delivered from his



<P>brother till full twelve months after, when he also was despatched



<P>in the same state of disgraceful ignorance as the former.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Master Charles was his mother's peculiar darling.&nbsp; He was little



<P>more than a year younger than John, but much smaller, paler, and



<P>less active and robust; a pettish, cowardly, capricious, selfish



<P>little fellow, only active in doing mischief, and only clever in



<P>inventing falsehoods:&nbsp; not simply to hide his faults, but, in mere



<P>malicious wantonness, to bring odium upon others.&nbsp; In fact, Master



<P>Charles was a very great nuisance to me:&nbsp; it was a trial of



<P>patience to live with him peaceably; to watch over him was worse;



<P>and to teach him, or pretend to teach him, was inconceivable.&nbsp;

At



<P>ten years old, he could not read correctly the easiest line in the



<P>simplest book; and as, according to his mother's principle, he was



<P>to be told every word, before he had time to hesitate or examine



<P>its orthography, and never even to be informed, as a stimulant to



<P>exertion, that other boys were more forward than he, it is not



<P>surprising that he made but little progress during the two years I



<P>had charge of his education.&nbsp; His minute portions of Latin grammar,



<P>&amp;c., were to be repeated over to him, till he chose to say he knew



<P>them, and then he was to be helped to say them; if he made mistakes



<P>in his little easy sums in arithmetic, they were to be shown him at



<P>once, and the sum done for him, instead of his being left to



<P>exercise his faculties in finding them out himself; so that, of



<P>course, he took no pains to avoid mistakes, but frequently set down



<P>his figures at random, without any calculation at all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I did not invariably confine myself to these rules:&nbsp; it was against



<P>my conscience to do so; but I seldom could venture to deviate from



<P>them in the slightest degree, without incurring the wrath of my



<P>little pupil, and subsequently of his mamma; to whom he would



<P>relate my transgressions maliciously exaggerated, or adorned with



<P>embellishments of his own; and often, in consequence, was I on the



<P>point of losing or resigning my situation.&nbsp; But, for their sakes

at



<P>home, I smothered my pride and suppressed my indignation, and



<P>managed to struggle on till my little tormentor was despatched to



<P>school; his father declaring that home education was 'no go; for



<P>him, it was plain; his mother spoiled him outrageously, and his



<P>governess could make no hand of him at all.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A few more observations about Horton Lodge and its ongoings, and I



<P>have done with dry description for the present.&nbsp; The house was

a



<P>very respectable one; superior to Mr. Bloomfield's, both in age,



<P>size, and magnificence:&nbsp; the garden was not so tastefully laid

out;



<P>but instead of the smooth-shaven lawn, the young trees guarded by



<P>palings, the grove of upstart poplars, and the plantation of firs,



<P>there was a wide park, stocked with deer, and beautified by fine



<P>old trees.&nbsp; The surrounding country itself was pleasant, as far

as



<P>fertile fields, flourishing trees, quiet green lanes, and smiling



<P>hedges with wild-flowers scattered along their banks, could make



<P>it; but it was depressingly flat to one born and nurtured among the



<P>rugged hills of -.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>We were situated nearly two miles from the village church, and,



<P>consequently, the family carriage was put in requisition every



<P>Sunday morning, and sometimes oftener.&nbsp; Mr. and Mrs. Murray



<P>generally thought it sufficient to show themselves at church once



<P>in the course of the day; but frequently the children preferred



<P>going a second time to wandering about the grounds all the day with



<P>nothing to do.&nbsp; If some of my pupils chose to walk and take me

with



<P>them, it was well for me; for otherwise my position in the carriage



<P>was to be crushed into the corner farthest from the open window,



<P>and with my back to the horses:&nbsp; a position which invariably made



<P>me sick; and if I were not actually obliged to leave the church in



<P>the middle of the service, my devotions were disturbed with a



<P>feeling of languor and sickliness, and the tormenting fear of its



<P>becoming worse:&nbsp; and a depressing headache was generally my



<P>companion throughout the day, which would otherwise have been one



<P>of welcome rest, and holy, calm enjoyment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It's very odd, Miss Grey, that the carriage should always make you



<P>sick:&nbsp; it never makes ME,' remarked Miss Matilda,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Nor me either,' said her sister; 'but I dare say it would, if I



<P>sat where she does - such a nasty, horrid place, Miss Grey; I



<P>wonder how you can bear it!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I am obliged to bear it, since no choice is left me,' - I might



<P>have answered; but in tenderness for their feelings I only replied,



<P>- 'Oh! it is but a short way, and if I am not sick in church, I



<P>don't mind it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>If I were called upon to give a description of the usual divisions



<P>and arrangements of the day, I should find it a very difficult



<P>matter.&nbsp; I had all my meals in the schoolroom with my pupils, at



<P>such times as suited their fancy:&nbsp; sometimes they would ring for



<P>dinner before it was half cooked; sometimes they would keep it



<P>waiting on the table for above an hour, and then be out of humour



<P>because the potatoes were cold, and the gravy covered with cakes of



<P>solid fat; sometimes they would have tea at four; frequently, they



<P>would storm at the servants because it was not in precisely at



<P>five; and when these orders were obeyed, by way of encouragement to



<P>punctuality, they would keep it on the table till seven or eight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Their hours of study were managed in much the same way; my judgment



<P>or convenience was never once consulted.&nbsp; Sometimes Matilda and



<P>John would determine 'to get all the plaguy business over before



<P>breakfast,' and send the maid to call me up at half-past five,



<P>without any scruple or apology; sometimes, I was told to be ready



<P>precisely at six, and, having dressed in a hurry, came down to an



<P>empty room, and after waiting a long time in suspense, discovered



<P>that they had changed their minds, and were still in bed; or,



<P>perhaps, if it were a fine summer morning, Brown would come to tell



<P>me that the young ladies and gentlemen had taken a holiday, and



<P>were gone out; and then I was kept waiting for breakfast till I was



<P>almost ready to faint:&nbsp; they having fortified themselves with



<P>something before they went.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Often they would do their lessons in the open air; which I had



<P>nothing to say against:&nbsp; except that I frequently caught cold by



<P>sitting on the damp grass, or from exposure to the evening dew, or



<P>some insidious draught, which seemed to have no injurious effect on



<P>them.&nbsp; It was quite right that they should be hardy; yet, surely,



<P>they might have been taught some consideration for others who were



<P>less so.&nbsp; But I must not blame them for what was, perhaps, my own



<P>fault; for I never made any particular objections to sitting where



<P>they pleased; foolishly choosing to risk the consequences, rather



<P>than trouble them for my convenience.&nbsp; Their indecorous manner

of



<P>doing their lessons was quite as remarkable as the caprice



<P>displayed in their choice of time and place.&nbsp; While receiving my



<P>instructions, or repeating what they had learned, they would lounge



<P>upon the sofa, lie on the rug, stretch, yawn, talk to each other,



<P>or look out of the window; whereas, I could not so much as stir the



<P>fire, or pick up the handkerchief I had dropped, without being



<P>rebuked for inattention by one of my pupils, or told that 'mamma



<P>would not like me to be so careless.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The servants, seeing in what little estimation the governess was



<P>held by both parents and children, regulated their behaviour by the



<P>same standard.&nbsp; I have frequently stood up for them, at the risk

of



<P>some injury to myself, against the tyranny and injustice of their



<P>young masters and mistresses; and I always endeavoured to give them



<P>as little trouble as possible:&nbsp; but they entirely neglected my



<P>comfort, despised my requests, and slighted my directions.&nbsp; All



<P>servants, I am convinced, would not have done so; but domestics in



<P>general, being ignorant and little accustomed to reason and



<P>reflection, are too easily corrupted by the carelessness and bad



<P>example of those above them; and these, I think, were not of the



<P>best order to begin with.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I sometimes felt myself degraded by the life I led, and ashamed of



<P>submitting to so many indignities; and sometimes I thought myself a



<P>fool for caring so much about them, and feared I must be sadly



<P>wanting in Christian humility, or that charity which 'suffereth



<P>long and is kind, seeketh not her own, is not easily provoked,



<P>beareth all things, endureth all things.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But, with time and patience, matters began to be slightly



<P>ameliorated:&nbsp; slowly, it is true, and almost imperceptibly; but

I



<P>got rid of my male pupils (that was no trifling advantage), and the



<P>girls, as I intimated before concerning one of them, became a



<P>little less insolent, and began to show some symptoms of esteem.



<P>'Miss Grey was a queer creature:&nbsp; she never flattered, and did

not



<P>praise them half enough; but whenever she did speak favourably of



<P>them, or anything belonging to them, they could be quite sure her



<P>approbation was sincere.&nbsp; She was very obliging, quiet, and



<P>peaceable in the main, but there were some things that put her out



<P>of temper:&nbsp; they did not much care for that, to be sure, but still



<P>it was better to keep her in tune; as when she was in a good humour



<P>she would talk to them, and be very agreeable and amusing



<P>sometimes, in her way; which was quite different to mamma's, but



<P>still very well for a change.&nbsp; She had her own opinions on every



<P>subject, and kept steadily to them - very tiresome opinions they



<P>often were; as she was always thinking of what was right and what



<P>was wrong, and had a strange reverence for matters connected with



<P>religion, and an unaccountable liking to good people.'



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter06</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER VI - THE PARSONAGE AGAIN</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>FOR a few months I remained peaceably at home, in the quiet



<P>enjoyment of liberty and rest, and genuine friendship, from all of



<P>which I had fasted so long; and in the earnest prosecution of my



<P>studies, to recover what I had lost during my stay at Wellwood



<P>House, and to lay in new stores for future use.&nbsp; My father's health



<P>was still very infirm, but not materially worse than when I last



<P>saw him; and I was glad I had it in my power to cheer him by my



<P>return, and to amuse him with singing his favourite songs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>No one triumphed over my failure, or said I had better have taken



<P>his or her advice, and quietly stayed at home.&nbsp; All were glad to



<P>have me back again, and lavished more kindness than ever upon me,



<P>to make up for the sufferings I had undergone; but not one would



<P>touch a shilling of what I had so cheerfully earned and so



<P>carefully saved, in the hope of sharing it with them.&nbsp; By dint

of



<P>pinching here, and scraping there, our debts were already nearly



<P>paid.&nbsp; Mary had had good success with her drawings; but our father



<P>had insisted upon HER likewise keeping all the produce of her



<P>industry to herself.&nbsp; All we could spare from the supply of our



<P>humble wardrobe and our little casual expenses, he directed us to



<P>put into the savings'-bank; saying, we knew not how soon we might



<P>be dependent on that alone for support:&nbsp; for he felt he had not



<P>long to be with us, and what would become of our mother and us when



<P>he was gone, God only knew!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Dear papa! if he had troubled himself less about the afflictions



<P>that threatened us in case of his death, I am convinced that



<P>dreaded event would not have taken place so soon.&nbsp; My mother would



<P>never suffer him to ponder on the subject if she could help it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, Richard!' exclaimed she, on one occasion, 'if you would but



<P>dismiss such gloomy subjects from your mind, you would live as long



<P>as any of us; at least you would live to see the girls married, and



<P>yourself a happy grandfather, with a canty old dame for your



<P>companion.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>My mother laughed, and so did my father:&nbsp; but his laugh soon



<P>perished in a dreary sigh.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'THEY married - poor penniless things!' said he; 'who will take



<P>them I wonder!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why, nobody shall that isn't thankful for them.&nbsp; Wasn't I



<P>penniless when you took me? and you PRETENDED, at least, to be



<P>vastly pleased with your acquisition.&nbsp; But it's no matter whether



<P>they get married or not:&nbsp; we can devise a thousand honest ways

of



<P>making a livelihood.&nbsp; And I wonder, Richard, you can think of



<P>bothering your head about our POVERTY in case of your death; as if



<P>THAT would be anything compared with the calamity of losing you -



<P>an affliction that you well know would swallow up all others, and



<P>which you ought to do your utmost to preserve us from:&nbsp; and there



<P>is nothing like a cheerful mind for keeping the body in health.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I know, Alice, it is wrong to keep repining as I do, but I cannot



<P>help it:&nbsp; you must bear with me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I WON'T bear with you, if I can alter you,' replied my mother:



<P>but the harshness of her words was undone by the earnest affection



<P>of her tone and pleasant smile, that made my father smile again,



<P>less sadly and less transiently than was his wont.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Mamma,' said I, as soon as I could find an opportunity of speaking



<P>with her alone, 'my money is but little, and cannot last long; if I



<P>could increase it, it would lessen papa's anxiety, on one subject



<P>at least.&nbsp; I cannot draw like Mary, and so the best thing I could



<P>do would be to look out for another situation.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And so you would actually try again, Agnes?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Decidedly, I would.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why, my dear, I should have thought you had had enough of it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I know,' said I, 'everybody is not like Mr. and Mrs. Bloomfield -



<P>'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Some are worse,' interrupted my mother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But not many, I think,' replied I, 'and I'm sure all children are



<P>not like theirs; for I and Mary were not:&nbsp; we always did as you

bid



<P>us, didn't we?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Generally:&nbsp; but then, I did not spoil you; and you were not



<P>perfect angels after all:&nbsp; Mary had a fund of quiet obstinacy,

and



<P>you were somewhat faulty in regard to temper; but you were very



<P>good children on the whole.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I know I was sulky sometimes, and I should have been glad to see



<P>these children sulky sometimes too; for then I could have



<P>understood them:&nbsp; but they never were, for they COULD not be



<P>offended, nor hurt, nor ashamed:&nbsp; they could not be unhappy in

any



<P>way, except when they were in a passion.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, if they COULD not, it was not their fault:&nbsp; you cannot



<P>expect stone to be as pliable as clay.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, but still it is very unpleasant to live with such



<P>unimpressible, incomprehensible creatures.&nbsp; You cannot love them;



<P>and if you could, your love would be utterly thrown away:&nbsp; they



<P>could neither return it, nor value, nor understand it.&nbsp; But,



<P>however, even if I should stumble on such a family again, which is



<P>quite unlikely, I have all this experience to begin with, and I



<P>should manage better another time; and the end and aim of this



<P>preamble is, let me try again.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, my girl, you are not easily discouraged, I see:&nbsp; I am glad



<P>of that.&nbsp; But, let me tell you, you are a good deal paler and



<P>thinner than when you first left home; and we cannot have you



<P>undermining your health to hoard up money either for yourself or



<P>others.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Mary tells me I am changed too; and I don't much wonder at it, for



<P>I was in a constant state of agitation and anxiety all day long:



<P>but next time I am determined to take things coolly.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After some further discussion, my mother promised once more to



<P>assist me, provided I would wait and be patient; and I left her to



<P>broach the matter to my father, when and how she deemed it most



<P>advisable:&nbsp; never doubting her ability to obtain his consent.



<P>Meantime, I searched, with great interest, the advertising columns



<P>of the newspapers, and wrote answers to every 'Wanted a Governess'



<P>that appeared at all eligible; but all my letters, as well as the



<P>replies, when I got any, were dutifully shown to my mother; and



<P>she, to my chagrin, made me reject the situations one after



<P>another:&nbsp; these were low people, these were too exacting in their



<P>demands, and these too niggardly in their remuneration.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Your talents are not such as every poor clergyman's daughter



<P>possesses, Agnes,' she would say, 'and you must not throw them



<P>away.&nbsp; Remember, you promised to be patient:&nbsp; there is no

need of



<P>hurry:&nbsp; you have plenty of time before you, and may have many



<P>chances yet.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At length, she advised me to put an advertisement, myself, in the



<P>paper, stating my qualifications, &amp;c.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Music, singing, drawing, French, Latin, and German,' said she,



<P>'are no mean assemblage:&nbsp; many will be glad to have so much in

one



<P>instructor; and this time, you shall try your fortune in a somewhat



<P>higher family in that of some genuine, thoroughbred gentleman; for



<P>such are far more likely to treat you with proper respect and



<P>consideration than those purse-proud tradespeople and arrogant



<P>upstarts.&nbsp; I have known several among the higher ranks who treated



<P>their governesses quite as one of the family; though some, I allow,



<P>are as insolent and exacting as any one else can be:&nbsp; for there

are



<P>bad and good in all classes.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The advertisement was quickly written and despatched.&nbsp; Of the two



<P>parties who answered it, but one would consent to give me fifty



<P>pounds, the sum my mother bade me name as the salary I should



<P>require; and here, I hesitated about engaging myself, as I feared



<P>the children would be too old, and their parents would require some



<P>one more showy, or more experienced, if not more accomplished than



<P>I.&nbsp; But my mother dissuaded me from declining it on that account:



<P>I should do vastly well, she said, if I would only throw aside my



<P>diffidence, and acquire a little more confidence in myself.&nbsp; I

was



<P>just to give a plain, true statement of my acquirements and



<P>qualifications, and name what stipulations I chose to make, and



<P>then await the result.&nbsp; The only stipulation I ventured to propose,



<P>was that I might be allowed two months' holidays during the year to



<P>visit my friends, at Midsummer and Christmas.&nbsp; The unknown lady,

in



<P>her reply, made no objection to this, and stated that, as to my



<P>acquirements, she had no doubt I should be able to give



<P>satisfaction; but in the engagement of governesses she considered



<P>those things as but subordinate points; as being situated in the



<P>neighbourhood of O-, she could get masters to supply any



<P>deficiencies in that respect:&nbsp; but, in her opinion, next to



<P>unimpeachable morality, a mild and cheerful temper and obliging



<P>disposition were the most essential requisities.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>My mother did not relish this at all, and now made many objections



<P>to my accepting the situation; in which my sister warmly supported



<P>her:&nbsp; but, unwilling to be balked again, I overruled them all;

and,



<P>having first obtained the consent of my father (who had, a short



<P>time previously, been apprised of these transactions), I wrote a



<P>most obliging epistle to my unknown correspondent, and, finally,



<P>the bargain was concluded.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was decreed that on the last day of January I was to enter upon



<P>my new office as governess in the family of Mr. Murray, of Horton



<P>Lodge, near O-, about seventy miles from our village:&nbsp; a formidable



<P>distance to me, as I had never been above twenty miles from home in



<P>all the course of my twenty years' sojourn on earth; and as,



<P>moreover, every individual in that family and in the neighbourhood



<P>was utterly unknown to myself and all my acquaintances.&nbsp; But this



<P>rendered it only the more piquant to me.&nbsp; I had now, in some



<P>measure, got rid of the MAUVAISE HONTE that had formerly oppressed



<P>me so much; there was a pleasing excitement in the idea of entering



<P>these unknown regions, and making my way alone among its strange



<P>inhabitants.&nbsp; I now flattered myself I was going to see something



<P>in the world:&nbsp; Mr. Murray's residence was near a large town, and



<P>not in a manufacturing district, where the people had nothing to do



<P>but to make money; his rank from what I could gather, appeared to



<P>be higher than that of Mr. Bloomfield; and, doubtless, he was one



<P>of those genuine thorough-bred gentry my mother spoke of, who would



<P>treat his governess with due consideration as a respectable well-



<P>educated lady, the instructor and guide of his children, and not a



<P>mere upper servant.&nbsp; Then, my pupils being older, would be more



<P>rational, more teachable, and less troublesome than the last; they



<P>would be less confined to the schoolroom, and not require that



<P>constant labour and incessant watching; and, finally, bright



<P>visions mingled with my hopes, with which the care of children and



<P>the mere duties of a governess had little or nothing to do.&nbsp; Thus,



<P>the reader will see that I had no claim to be regarded as a martyr



<P>to filial piety, going forth to sacrifice peace and liberty for the



<P>sole purpose of laying up stores for the comfort and support of my



<P>parents:&nbsp; though certainly the comfort of my father, and the future



<P>support of my mother, had a large share in my calculations; and



<P>fifty pounds appeared to me no ordinary sum.&nbsp; I must have decent



<P>clothes becoming my station; I must, it seemed, put out my washing,



<P>and also pay for my four annual journeys between Horton Lodge and



<P>home; but with strict attention to economy, surely twenty pounds,



<P>or little more, would cover those expenses, and then there would be



<P>thirty for the bank, or little less:&nbsp; what a valuable addition

to



<P>our stock!&nbsp; Oh, I must struggle to keep this situation, whatever

it



<P>might be! both for my own honour among my friends and for the solid



<P>services I might render them by my continuance there.



<P>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">
