<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XII - THE SHOWER</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE next visit I paid to Nancy Brown was in the second week in



<P>March:&nbsp; for, though I had many spare minutes during the day, I



<P>seldom could look upon an hour as entirely my own; since, where



<P>everything was left to the caprices of Miss Matilda and her sister,



<P>there could be no order or regularity.&nbsp; Whatever occupation I



<P>chose, when not actually busied about them or their concerns, I



<P>had, as it were, to keep my loins girded, my shoes on my feet, and



<P>my staff in my hand; for not to be immediately forthcoming when



<P>called for, was regarded as a grave and inexcusable offence:&nbsp; not



<P>only by my pupils and their mother, but by the very servant, who



<P>came in breathless haste to call me, exclaiming, 'You're to go to



<P>the schoolroom DIRECTLY, mum, the young ladies is WAITING!!'



<P>Climax of horror! actually waiting for their governess!!!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But this time I was pretty sure of an hour or two to myself; for



<P>Matilda was preparing for a long ride, and Rosalie was dressing for



<P>a dinner-party at Lady Ashby's:&nbsp; so I took the opportunity of



<P>repairing to the widow's cottage, where I found her in some anxiety



<P>about her cat, which had been absent all day.&nbsp; I comforted her

with



<P>as many anecdotes of that animal's roving propensities as I could



<P>recollect.&nbsp; 'I'm feared o' th' gamekeepers,' said she:&nbsp; 'that's

all



<P>'at I think on.&nbsp; If th' young gentlemen had been at home, I should



<P>a' thought they'd been setting their dogs at her, an' worried her,



<P>poor thing, as they did MANY a poor thing's cat; but I haven't that



<P>to be feared on now.'&nbsp; Nancy's eyes were better, but still far

from



<P>well:&nbsp; she had been trying to make a Sunday shirt for her son,

but



<P>told me she could only bear to do a little bit at it now and then,



<P>so that it progressed but slowly, though the poor lad wanted it



<P>sadly.&nbsp; So I proposed to help her a little, after I had read to



<P>her, for I had plenty of time that evening, and need not return



<P>till dusk.&nbsp; She thankfully accepted the offer.&nbsp; 'An' you'll

be a



<P>bit o' company for me too, Miss,' said she; 'I like as I feel



<P>lonesome without my cat.'&nbsp; But when I had finished reading, and



<P>done the half of a seam, with Nancy's capacious brass thimble



<P>fitted on to my finger by means of a roll of paper, I was disturbed



<P>by the entrance of Mr. Weston, with the identical cat in his arms.



<P>I now saw that he could smile, and very pleasantly too.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I've done you a piece of good service, Nancy,' he began:&nbsp; then



<P>seeing me, he acknowledged my presence by a slight bow.&nbsp; I should



<P>have been invisible to Hatfield, or any other gentleman of those



<P>parts.&nbsp; 'I've delivered your cat,' he continued, 'from the hands,



<P>or rather the gun, of Mr. Murray's gamekeeper.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'God bless you, sir!' cried the grateful old woman, ready to weep



<P>for joy as she received her favourite from his arms.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Take care of it,' said he, 'and don't let it go near the rabbit-



<P>warren, for the gamekeeper swears he'll shoot it if he sees it



<P>there again:&nbsp; he would have done so to-day, if I had not been in



<P>time to stop him.&nbsp; I believe it is raining, Miss Grey,' added he,



<P>more quietly, observing that I had put aside my work, and was



<P>preparing to depart.&nbsp; 'Don't let me disturb you - I shan't stay

two



<P>minutes.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You'll BOTH stay while this shower gets owered,' said Nancy, as



<P>she stirred the fire, and placed another chair beside it; 'what!



<P>there's room for all.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I can see better here, thank you, Nancy,' replied I, taking my



<P>work to the window, where she had the goodness to suffer me to



<P>remain unmolested, while she got a brush to remove the cat's hairs



<P>from Mr. Weston's coat, carefully wiped the rain from his hat, and



<P>gave the cat its supper, busily talking all the time:&nbsp; now thanking



<P>her clerical friend for what he had done; now wondering how the cat



<P>had found out the warren; and now lamenting the probable



<P>consequences of such a discovery.&nbsp; He listened with a quiet, good-



<P>natured smile, and at length took a seat in compliance with her



<P>pressing invitations, but repeated that he did not mean to stay.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I have another place to go to,' said he, 'and I see' (glancing at



<P>the book on the table) 'someone else has been reading to you.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, sir; Miss Grey has been as kind as read me a chapter; an' now



<P>she's helping me with a shirt for our Bill - but I'm feared she'll



<P>be cold there.&nbsp; Won't you come to th' fire, Miss?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, thank you, Nancy, I'm quite warm.&nbsp; I must go as soon as this



<P>shower is over.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, Miss!&nbsp; You said you could stop while dusk!' cried the



<P>provoking old woman, and Mr. Weston seized his hat.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Nay, sir,' exclaimed she, 'pray don't go now, while it rains so



<P>fast.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But it strikes me I'm keeping your visitor away from the fire.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, you're not, Mr. Weston,' replied I, hoping there was no harm



<P>in a falsehood of that description.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, sure!' cried Nancy.&nbsp; 'What, there's lots o' room!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Miss Grey,' said he, half-jestingly, as if he felt it necessary to



<P>change the present subject, whether he had anything particular to



<P>say or not, 'I wish you would make my peace with the squire, when



<P>you see him.&nbsp; He was by when I rescued Nancy's cat, and did not



<P>quite approve of the deed.&nbsp; I told him I thought he might better



<P>spare all his rabbits than she her cat, for which audacious



<P>assertion he treated me to some rather ungentlemanly language; and



<P>I fear I retorted a trifle too warmly.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, lawful sir!&nbsp; I hope you didn't fall out wi' th' maister for



<P>sake o' my cat! he cannot bide answering again - can th' maister.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh! it's no matter, Nancy:&nbsp; I don't care about it, really; I said



<P>nothing VERY uncivil; and I suppose Mr. Murray is accustomed to use



<P>rather strong language when he's heated.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Ay, sir:&nbsp; it's a pity.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And now, I really must go.&nbsp; I have to visit a place a mile beyond



<P>this; and you would not have me to return in the dark:&nbsp; besides,

it



<P>has nearly done raining now - so good-evening, Nancy.&nbsp; Good-



<P>evening, Miss Grey.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Good-evening, Mr. Weston; but don't depend upon me for making your



<P>peace with Mr. Murray, for I never see him - to speak to.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Don't you; it can't be helped then,' replied he, in dolorous



<P>resignation:&nbsp; then, with a peculiar half-smile, he added, 'But



<P>never mind; I imagine the squire has more to apologise for than I;'



<P>and left the cottage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I went on with my sewing as long as I could see, and then bade



<P>Nancy good-evening; checking her too lively gratitude by the



<P>undeniable assurance that I had only done for her what she would



<P>have done for me, if she had been in my place and I in hers.&nbsp; I



<P>hastened back to Horton Lodge, where, having entered the



<P>schoolroom, I found the tea-table all in confusion, the tray



<P>flooded with slops, and Miss Matilda in a most ferocious humour.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Miss Grey, whatever have you been about?&nbsp; I've had tea half an



<P>hour ago, and had to make it myself, and drink it all alone!&nbsp; I



<P>wish you would come in sooner!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I've been to see Nancy Brown.&nbsp; I thought you would not be back



<P>from your ride.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'How could I ride in the rain, I should like to know.&nbsp; That damned



<P>pelting shower was vexatious enough - coming on when I was just in



<P>full swing:&nbsp; and then to come and find nobody in to tea! and you



<P>know I can't make the tea as I like it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I didn't think of the shower,' replied I (and, indeed, the thought



<P>of its driving her home had never entered my head).

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, of course; you were under shelter yourself, and you never



<P>thought of other people.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I bore her coarse reproaches with astonishing equanimity, even with



<P>cheerfulness; for I was sensible that I had done more good to Nancy



<P>Brown than harm to her:&nbsp; and perhaps some other thoughts assisted



<P>to keep up my spirits, and impart a relish to the cup of cold,



<P>overdrawn tea, and a charm to the otherwise unsightly table; and -



<P>I had almost said - to Miss Matilda's unamiable face.&nbsp; But she

soon



<P>betook herself to the stables, and left me to the quiet enjoyment



<P>of my solitary meal.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter04</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER IV - THE GRANDMAMMA</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>I SPARE my readers the account of my delight on coming home, my



<P>happiness while there - enjoying a brief space of rest and liberty



<P>in that dear, familiar place, among the loving and the loved - and



<P>my sorrow on being obliged to bid them, once more, a long adieu.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I returned, however, with unabated vigour to my work - a more



<P>arduous task than anyone can imagine, who has not felt something



<P>like the misery of being charged with the care and direction of a



<P>set of mischievous, turbulent rebels, whom his utmost exertions



<P>cannot bind to their duty; while, at the same time, he is



<P>responsible for their conduct to a higher power, who exacts from



<P>him what cannot be achieved without the aid of the superior's more



<P>potent authority; which, either from indolence, or the fear of



<P>becoming unpopular with the said rebellious gang, the latter



<P>refuses to give.&nbsp; I can conceive few situations more harassing

than



<P>that wherein, however you may long for success, however you may



<P>labour to fulfil your duty, your efforts are baffled and set at



<P>nought by those beneath you, and unjustly censured and misjudged by



<P>those above.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I have not enumerated half the vexatious propensities of my pupils,



<P>or half the troubles resulting from my heavy responsibilities, for



<P>fear of trespassing too much upon the reader's patience; as,



<P>perhaps, I have already done; but my design in writing the few last



<P>pages was not to amuse, but to benefit those whom it might concern;



<P>he that has no interest in such matters will doubtless have skipped



<P>them over with a cursory glance, and, perhaps, a malediction



<P>against the prolixity of the writer; but if a parent has,



<P>therefrom, gathered any useful hint, or an unfortunate governess



<P>received thereby the slightest benefit, I am well rewarded for my



<P>pains.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To avoid trouble and confusion, I have taken my pupils one by one,



<P>and discussed their various qualities; but this can give no



<P>adequate idea of being worried by the whole three together; when,



<P>as was often the case, all were determined to 'be naughty, and to



<P>tease Miss Grey, and put her in a passion.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sometimes, on such occasions, the thought has suddenly occurred to



<P>me - 'If they could see me now!' meaning, of course, my friends at



<P>home; and the idea of how they would pity me has made me pity



<P>myself - so greatly that I have had the utmost difficulty to



<P>restrain my tears:&nbsp; but I have restrained them, till my little



<P>tormentors were gone to dessert, or cleared off to bed (my only



<P>prospects of deliverance), and then, in all the bliss of solitude,



<P>I have given myself up to the luxury of an unrestricted burst of



<P>weeping.&nbsp; But this was a weakness I did not often indulge:&nbsp;

my



<P>employments were too numerous, my leisure moments too precious, to



<P>admit of much time being given to fruitless lamentations.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I particularly remember one wild, snowy afternoon, soon after my



<P>return in January:&nbsp; the children had all come up from dinner,



<P>loudly declaring that they meant 'to be naughty;' and they had well



<P>kept their resolution, though I had talked myself hoarse, and



<P>wearied every muscle in my throat, in the vain attempt to reason



<P>them out of it.&nbsp; I had got Tom pinned up in a corner, whence, I



<P>told him, he should not escape till he had done his appointed task.



<P>Meantime, Fanny had possessed herself of my work-bag, and was



<P>rifling its contents - and spitting into it besides.&nbsp; I told her

to



<P>let it alone, but to no purpose, of course.&nbsp; 'Burn it, Fanny!'



<P>cried Tom:&nbsp; and THIS command she hastened to obey.&nbsp; I sprang

to



<P>snatch it from the fire, and Tom darted to the door.&nbsp; 'Mary Ann,



<P>throw her desk out of the window!' cried he:&nbsp; and my precious desk,



<P>containing my letters and papers, my small amount of cash, and all



<P>my valuables, was about to be precipitated from the three-storey



<P>window.&nbsp; I flew to rescue it.&nbsp; Meanwhile Tom had left the

room, and



<P>was rushing down the stairs, followed by Fanny.&nbsp; Having secured

my



<P>desk, I ran to catch them, and Mary Ann came scampering after.&nbsp;

All



<P>three escaped me, and ran out of the house into the garden, where



<P>they plunged about in the snow, shouting and screaming in exultant



<P>glee.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>What must I do?&nbsp; If I followed them, I should probably be unable

to



<P>capture one, and only drive them farther away; if I did not, how



<P>was I to get them in?&nbsp; And what would their parents think of me,

if



<P>they saw or heard the children rioting, hatless, bonnetless,



<P>gloveless, and bootless, in the deep soft snow?&nbsp; While I stood

in



<P>this perplexity, just without the door, trying, by grim looks and



<P>angry words, to awe them into subjection, I heard a voice behind



<P>me, in harshly piercing tones, exclaiming, -

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Miss Grey!&nbsp; Is it possible?&nbsp; What, in the devil's name, can

you be



<P>thinking about?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I can't get them in, sir,' said I, turning round, and beholding



<P>Mr. Bloomfield, with his hair on end, and his pale blue eyes



<P>bolting from their sockets.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But I INSIST upon their being got in!' cried he, approaching



<P>nearer, and looking perfectly ferocious.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Then, sir, you must call them yourself, if you please, for they



<P>won't listen to me,' I replied, stepping back.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Come in with you, you filthy brats; or I'll horsewhip you every



<P>one!' roared he; and the children instantly obeyed.&nbsp; 'There, you



<P>see! - they come at the first word!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, when YOU speak.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And it's very strange, that when you've the care of 'em you've no



<P>better control over 'em than that! - Now, there they are - gone up-



<P>stairs with their nasty snowy feet!&nbsp; Do go after 'em and see them



<P>made decent, for heaven's sake!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>That gentleman's mother was then staying in the house; and, as I



<P>ascended the stairs and passed the drawing-room door, I had the



<P>satisfaction of hearing the old lady declaiming aloud to her



<P>daughter-in-law to this effect (for I could only distinguish the



<P>most emphatic words) -

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Gracious heavens! - never in all my life - ! - get their death as



<P>sure as - !&nbsp; Do you think, my dear, she's a PROPER PERSON?&nbsp;

Take my



<P>word for it - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I heard no more; but that sufficed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The senior Mrs. Bloomfield had been very attentive and civil to me;



<P>and till now I had thought her a nice, kind-hearted, chatty old



<P>body.&nbsp; She would often come to me and talk in a confidential



<P>strain; nodding and shaking her head, and gesticulating with hands



<P>and eyes, as a certain class of old ladies are won't to do; though



<P>I never knew one that carried the peculiarity to so great an



<P>extent.&nbsp; She would even sympathise with me for the trouble I had



<P>with the children, and express at times, by half sentences,



<P>interspersed with nods and knowing winks, her sense of the



<P>injudicious conduct of their mamma in so restricting my power, and



<P>neglecting to support me with her authority.&nbsp; Such a mode of



<P>testifying disapprobation was not much to my taste; and I generally



<P>refused to take it in, or understand anything more than was openly



<P>spoken; at least, I never went farther than an implied



<P>acknowledgment that, if matters were otherwise ordered my task



<P>would be a less difficult one, and I should be better able to guide



<P>and instruct my charge; but now I must be doubly cautious.



<P>Hitherto, though I saw the old lady had her defects (of which one



<P>was a proneness to proclaim her perfections), I had always been



<P>wishful to excuse them, and to give her credit for all the virtues



<P>she professed, and even imagine others yet untold.&nbsp; Kindness, which



<P>had been the food of my life through so many years, had lately been



<P>so entirely denied me, that I welcomed with grateful joy the



<P>slightest semblance of it.&nbsp; No wonder, then, that my heart warmed



<P>to the old lady, and always gladdened at her approach and regretted



<P>her departure.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But now, the few words luckily or unluckily heard in passing had



<P>wholly revolutionized my ideas respecting her:&nbsp; now I looked upon



<P>her as hypocritical and insincere, a flatterer, and a spy upon my



<P>words and deeds.&nbsp; Doubtless it would have been my interest still

to



<P>meet her with the same cheerful smile and tone of respectful



<P>cordiality as before; but I could not, if I would:&nbsp; my manner



<P>altered with my feelings, and became so cold and shy that she could



<P>not fail to notice it.&nbsp; She soon did notice it, and HER manner



<P>altered too:&nbsp; the familiar nod was changed to a stiff bow, the



<P>gracious smile gave place to a glare of Gorgon ferocity; her



<P>vivacious loquacity was entirely transferred from me to 'the



<P>darling boy and girls,' whom she flattered and indulged more



<P>absurdly than ever their mother had done.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I confess I was somewhat troubled at this change:&nbsp; I feared the



<P>consequences of her displeasure, and even made some efforts to



<P>recover the ground I had lost - and with better apparent success



<P>than I could have anticipated.&nbsp; At one time, I, merely in common



<P>civility, asked after her cough; immediately her long visage



<P>relaxed into a smile, and she favoured me with a particular history



<P>of that and her other infirmities, followed by an account of her



<P>pious resignation, delivered in the usual emphatic, declamatory



<P>style, which no writing can portray.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But there's one remedy for all, my dear, and that's resignation'



<P>(a toss of the head), 'resignation to the will of heaven!' (an



<P>uplifting of the hands and eyes).&nbsp; 'It has always supported me



<P>through all my trials, and always will do' (a succession of nods).



<P>'But then, it isn't everybody that can say that' (a shake of the



<P>head); 'but I'm one of the pious ones, Miss Grey!' (a very



<P>significant nod and toss).&nbsp; 'And, thank heaven, I always was'



<P>(another nod), 'and I glory in it!' (an emphatic clasping of the



<P>hands and shaking of the head).&nbsp; And with several texts of



<P>Scripture, misquoted or misapplied, and religious exclamations so



<P>redolent of the ludicrous in the style of delivery and manner of



<P>bringing in, if not in the expressions themselves, that I decline



<P>repeating them, she withdrew; tossing her large head in high good-



<P>humour - with herself at least - and left me hoping that, after



<P>all, she was rather weak than wicked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At her next visit to Wellwood House, I went so far as to say I was



<P>glad to see her looking so well.&nbsp; The effect of this was magical:



<P>the words, intended as a mark of civility, were received as a



<P>flattering compliment; her countenance brightened up, and from that



<P>moment she became as gracious and benign as heart could wish - in



<P>outward semblance at least.&nbsp; From what I now saw of her, and what

I



<P>heard from the children, I know that, in order to gain her cordial



<P>friendship, I had but to utter a word of flattery at each



<P>convenient opportunity:&nbsp; but this was against my principles; and



<P>for lack of this, the capricious old dame soon deprived me of her



<P>favour again, and I believe did me much secret injury.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She could not greatly influence her daughter-in-law against me,



<P>because, between that lady and herself there was a mutual dislike -



<P>chiefly shown by her in secret detractions and calumniations; by



<P>the other, in an excess of frigid formality in her demeanour; and



<P>no fawning flattery of the elder could thaw away the wall of ice



<P>which the younger interposed between them.&nbsp; But with her son, the



<P>old lady had better success:&nbsp; he would listen to all she had to



<P>say, provided she could soothe his fretful temper, and refrain from



<P>irritating him by her own asperities; and I have reason to believe



<P>that she considerably strengthened his prejudice against me.&nbsp; She



<P>would tell him that I shamefully neglected the children, and even



<P>his wife did not attend to them as she ought; and that he must look



<P>after them himself, or they would all go to ruin.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Thus urged, he would frequently give himself the trouble of



<P>watching them from the windows during their play; at times, he



<P>would follow them through the grounds, and too often came suddenly



<P>upon them while they were dabbling in the forbidden well, talking



<P>to the coachman in the stables, or revelling in the filth of the



<P>farm-yard - and I, meanwhile, wearily standing, by, having



<P>previously exhausted my energy in vain attempts to get them away.



<P>Often, too, he would unexpectedly pop his head into the schoolroom



<P>while the young people were at meals, and find them spilling their



<P>milk over the table and themselves, plunging their fingers into



<P>their own or each other's mugs, or quarrelling over their victuals



<P>like a set of tiger's cubs.&nbsp; If I were quiet at the moment, I was



<P>conniving at their disorderly conduct; if (as was frequently the



<P>case) I happened to be exalting my voice to enforce order, I was



<P>using undue violence, and setting the girls a bad example by such



<P>ungentleness of tone and language.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I remember one afternoon in spring, when, owing to the rain, they



<P>could not go out; but, by some amazing good fortune, they had all



<P>finished their lessons, and yet abstained from running down to



<P>tease their parents - a trick that annoyed me greatly, but which,



<P>on rainy days, I seldom could prevent their doing; because, below,



<P>they found novelty and amusement - especially when visitors were in



<P>the house; and their mother, though she bid me keep them in the



<P>schoolroom, would never chide them for leaving it, or trouble



<P>herself to send them back.&nbsp; But this day they appeared satisfied



<P>with, their present abode, and what is more wonderful still, seemed



<P>disposed to play together without depending on me for amusement,



<P>and without quarrelling with each other.&nbsp; Their occupation was

a



<P>somewhat puzzling one:&nbsp; they were all squatted together on the



<P>floor by the window, over a heap of broken toys and a quantity of



<P>birds' eggs - or rather egg-shells, for the contents had luckily



<P>been abstracted.&nbsp; These shells they had broken up and were pounding



<P>into small fragments, to what end I could not imagine; but so long



<P>as they were quiet and not in positive mischief, I did not care;



<P>and, with a feeling of unusual repose, I sat by the fire, putting



<P>the finishing stitches to a frock for Mary Ann's doll; intending,



<P>when that was done, to begin a letter to my mother.&nbsp; Suddenly the



<P>door opened, and the dingy head of Mr. Bloomfield looked in.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'All very quiet here!&nbsp; What are you doing?' said he.&nbsp; 'No

harm TO-



<P>DAY, at least,' thought I.&nbsp; But he was of a different opinion.



<P>Advancing to the window, and seeing the children's occupations, he



<P>testily exclaimed - 'What in the world are you about?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'We're grinding egg-shells, papa!' cried Tom.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'How DARE you make such a mess, you little devils?&nbsp; Don't you see



<P>what confounded work you're making of the carpet?' (the carpet was



<P>a plain brown drugget).&nbsp; 'Miss Grey, did you know what they were



<P>doing?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, sir.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You knew it?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You knew it! and you actually sat there and permitted them to go



<P>on without a word of reproof!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I didn't think they were doing any harm.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Any harm!&nbsp; Why, look there!&nbsp; Just look at that carpet, and

see -



<P>was there ever anything like it in a Christian house before?&nbsp; No



<P>wonder your room is not fit for a pigsty - no wonder your pupils



<P>are worse than a litter of pigs! - no wonder - oh! I declare, it



<P>puts me quite past my patience' and he departed, shutting the door



<P>after him with a bang that made the children laugh.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It puts me quite past my patience too!' muttered I, getting up;



<P>and, seizing the poker, I dashed it repeatedly into the cinders,



<P>and stirred them up with unwonted energy; thus easing my irritation



<P>under pretence of mending the fire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After this, Mr. Bloomfield was continually looking in to see if the



<P>schoolroom was in order; and, as the children were continually



<P>littering the floor with fragments of toys, sticks, stones,



<P>stubble, leaves, and other rubbish, which I could not prevent their



<P>bringing, or oblige them to gather up, and which the servants



<P>refused to 'clean after them,' I had to spend a considerable



<P>portion of my valuable leisure moments on my knees upon the floor,



<P>in painsfully reducing things to order.&nbsp; Once I told them that

they



<P>should not taste their supper till they had picked up everything



<P>from the carpet; Fanny might have hers when she had taken up a



<P>certain quantity, Mary Ann when she had gathered twice as many, and



<P>Tom was to clear away the rest.&nbsp; Wonderful to state, the girls

did



<P>their part; but Tom was in such a fury that he flew upon the table,



<P>scattered the bread and milk about the floor, struck his sisters,



<P>kicked the coals out of the coal-pan, attempted to overthrow the



<P>table and chairs, and seemed inclined to make a Douglas-larder of



<P>the whole contents of the room:&nbsp; but I seized upon him, and,



<P>sending Mary Ann to call her mamma, held him, in spite of kicks,



<P>blows, yells, and execrations, till Mrs. Bloomfield made her



<P>appearance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What is the matter with my boy?' said she.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And when the matter was explained to her, all she did was to send



<P>for the nursery-maid to put the room in order, and bring Master



<P>Bloomfield his supper.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'There now,' cried Tom, triumphantly, looking up from his viands



<P>with his mouth almost too full for speech.&nbsp; 'There now, Miss Grey!



<P>you see I've got my supper in spite of you:&nbsp; and I haven't picked



<P>up a single thing!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The only person in the house who had any real sympathy for me was



<P>the nurse; for she had suffered like afflictions, though in a



<P>smaller degree; as she had not the task of teaching, nor was she so



<P>responsible for the conduct of her charge.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, Miss Grey!' she would say, 'you have some trouble with them



<P>childer!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I have, indeed, Betty; and I daresay you know what it is.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Ay, I do so!&nbsp; But I don't vex myself o'er 'em as you do.&nbsp;

And



<P>then, you see, I hit 'em a slap sometimes:&nbsp; and them little 'uns

-



<P>I gives 'em a good whipping now and then:&nbsp; there's nothing else



<P>will do for 'em, as what they say.&nbsp; Howsoever, I've lost my place



<P>for it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Have you, Betty?&nbsp; I heard you were going to leave.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Eh, bless you, yes!&nbsp; Missis gave me warning a three wik sin'.&nbsp;

She



<P>told me afore Christmas how it mud be, if I hit 'em again; but I



<P>couldn't hold my hand off 'em at nothing.&nbsp; I know not how YOU do,



<P>for Miss Mary Ann's worse by the half nor her sisters!'



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER X - THE CHURCH</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>'WELL, Miss Grey, what do you think of the new curate?' asked Miss



<P>Murray, on our return from church the Sunday after the



<P>recommencement of our duties.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I can scarcely tell,' was my reply:&nbsp; 'I have not even heard him



<P>preach.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, but you saw him, didn't you?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, but I cannot pretend to judge of a man's character by a



<P>single cursory glance at his face.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But isn't he ugly?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'He did not strike me as being particularly so; I don't dislike



<P>that cast of countenance:&nbsp; but the only thing I particularly



<P>noticed about him was his style of reading; which appeared to me



<P>good - infinitely better, at least, than Mr. Hatfield's.&nbsp; He read



<P>the Lessons as if he were bent on giving full effect to every



<P>passage; it seemed as if the most careless person could not have



<P>helped attending, nor the most ignorant have failed to understand;



<P>and the prayers he read as if he were not reading at all, but



<P>praying earnestly and sincerely from his own heart.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, yes, that's all he is good for:&nbsp; he can plod through the



<P>service well enough; but he has not a single idea beyond it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'How do you know?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh! I know perfectly well; I am an excellent judge in such



<P>matters.&nbsp; Did you see how he went out of church? stumping along

-



<P>as if there were nobody there but himself - never looking to the



<P>right hand or the left, and evidently thinking of nothing but just



<P>getting out of the church, and, perhaps, home to his dinner:&nbsp; his



<P>great stupid head could contain no other idea.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I suppose you would have had him cast a glance into the squire's



<P>pew,' said I, laughing at the vehemence of her hostility.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Indeed! I should have been highly indignant if he had dared to do



<P>such a thing!' replied she, haughtily tossing her head; then, after



<P>a moment's reflection, she added - 'Well, well!&nbsp; I suppose he's



<P>good enough for his place:&nbsp; but I'm glad I'm not dependent on HIM



<P>for amusement - that's all.&nbsp; Did you see how Mr. Hatfield hurried



<P>out to get a bow from me, and be in time to put us into the



<P>carriage?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes,' answered I; internally adding, 'and I thought it somewhat



<P>derogatory to his dignity as a clergyman to come flying from the



<P>pulpit in such eager haste to shake hands with the squire, and hand



<P>his wife and daughters into their carriage:&nbsp; and, moreover, I owe



<P>him a grudge for nearly shutting me out of it'; for, in fact,



<P>though I was standing before his face, close beside the carriage



<P>steps, waiting to get in, he would persist in putting them up and



<P>closing the door, till one of the family stopped him by calling out



<P>that the governess was not in yet; then, without a word of apology,



<P>he departed, wishing them good-morning, and leaving the footman to



<P>finish the business.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>NOTA BENE. - Mr. Hatfield never spoke to me, neither did Sir Hugh



<P>or Lady Meltham, nor Mr. Harry or Miss Meltham, nor Mr. Green or



<P>his sisters, nor any other lady or gentleman who frequented that



<P>church:&nbsp; nor, in fact, any one that visited at Horton Lodge.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Miss Murray ordered the carriage again, in the afternoon, for



<P>herself and her sister:&nbsp; she said it was too cold for them to enjoy



<P>themselves in the garden; and besides, she believed Harry Meltham



<P>would be at church.&nbsp; 'For,' said she, smiling slyly at her own

fair



<P>image in the glass, 'he has been a most exemplary attendant at



<P>church these last few Sundays:&nbsp; you would think he was quite a

good



<P>Christian.&nbsp; And you may go with us, Miss Grey:&nbsp; I want you

to see



<P>him; he is so greatly improved since he returned from abroad - you



<P>can't think!&nbsp; And besides, then you will have an opportunity of



<P>seeing the beautiful Mr. Weston again, and of hearing him preach.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I did hear him preach, and was decidedly pleased with the



<P>evangelical truth of his doctrine, as well as the earnest



<P>simplicity of his manner, and the clearness and force of his style.



<P>It was truly refreshing to hear such a sermon, after being so long



<P>accustomed to the dry, prosy discourses of the former curate, and



<P>the still less edifying harangues of the rector.&nbsp; Mr. Hatfield



<P>would come sailing up the aisle, or rather sweeping along like a



<P>whirlwind, with his rich silk gown flying behind him and rustling



<P>against the pew doors, mount the pulpit like a conqueror ascending



<P>his triumphal car; then, sinking on the velvet cushion in an



<P>attitude of studied grace, remain in silent prostration for a



<P>certain time; then mutter over a Collect, and gabble through the



<P>Lord's Prayer, rise, draw off one bright lavender glove, to give



<P>the congregation the benefit of his sparkling rings, lightly pass



<P>his fingers through his well-curled hair, flourish a cambric



<P>handkerchief, recite a very short passage, or, perhaps, a mere



<P>phrase of Scripture, as a head-piece to his discourse, and,



<P>finally, deliver a composition which, as a composition, might be



<P>considered good, though far too studied and too artificial to be



<P>pleasing to me:&nbsp; the propositions were well laid down, the



<P>arguments logically conducted; and yet, it was sometimes hard to



<P>listen quietly throughout, without some slight demonstrations of



<P>disapproval or impatience.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His favourite subjects were church discipline, rites and



<P>ceremonies, apostolical succession, the duty of reverence and



<P>obedience to the clergy, the atrocious criminality of dissent, the



<P>absolute necessity of observing all the forms of godliness, the



<P>reprehensible presumption of individuals who attempted to think for



<P>themselves in matters connected with religion, or to be guided by



<P>their own interpretations of Scripture, and, occasionally (to



<P>please his wealthy parishioners) the necessity of deferential



<P>obedience from the poor to the rich - supporting his maxims and



<P>exhortations throughout with quotations from the Fathers:&nbsp; with



<P>whom he appeared to be far better acquainted than with the Apostles



<P>and Evangelists, and whose importance he seemed to consider at



<P>least equal to theirs.&nbsp; But now and then he gave us a sermon of

a



<P>different order - what some would call a very good one; but sunless



<P>and severe:&nbsp; representing the Deity as a terrible taskmaster rather



<P>than a benevolent father.&nbsp; Yet, as I listened, I felt inclined

to



<P>think the man was sincere in all he said:&nbsp; he must have changed

his



<P>views, and become decidedly religious, gloomy and austere, yet



<P>still devout.&nbsp; But such illusions were usually dissipated, on



<P>coming out of church, by hearing his voice in jocund colloquy with



<P>some of the Melthams or Greens, or, perhaps, the Murrays



<P>themselves; probably laughing at his own sermon, and hoping that he



<P>had given the rascally people something to think about; perchance,



<P>exulting in the thought that old Betty Holmes would now lay aside



<P>the sinful indulgence of her pipe, which had been her daily solace



<P>for upwards of thirty years:&nbsp; that George Higgins would be



<P>frightened out of his Sabbath evening walks, and Thomas Jackson



<P>would be sorely troubled in his conscience, and shaken in his sure



<P>and certain hope of a joyful resurrection at the last day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Thus, I could not but conclude that Mr. Hatfield was one of those



<P>who 'bind heavy burdens, and grievous to be borne, and lay them



<P>upon men's shoulders, while they themselves will not move them with



<P>one of their fingers'; and who 'make the word of God of none effect



<P>by their traditions, teaching for doctrines the commandments of



<P>men.'&nbsp; I was well pleased to observe that the new curate resembled



<P>him, as far as I could see, in none of these particulars.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, Miss Grey, what do you think of him now?' said Miss Murray,



<P>as we took our places in the carriage after service.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No harm still,' replied I.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No harm!' repeated she in amazement.&nbsp; 'What do you mean?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I mean, I think no worse of him than I did before.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No worse!&nbsp; I should think not indeed - quite the contrary!&nbsp;

Is he



<P>not greatly improved?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, yes; very much indeed,' replied I; for I had now discovered



<P>that it was Harry Meltham she meant, not Mr. Weston.&nbsp; That



<P>gentleman had eagerly come forward to speak to the young ladies:&nbsp;

a



<P>thing he would hardly have ventured to do had their mother been



<P>present; he had likewise politely handed them into the carriage.



<P>He had not attempted to shut me out, like Mr. Hatfield; neither, of



<P>course, had he offered me his assistance (I should not have



<P>accepted it, if he had), but as long as the door remained open he



<P>had stood smirking and chatting with them, and then lifted his hat



<P>and departed to his own abode:&nbsp; but I had scarcely noticed him

all



<P>the time.&nbsp; My companions, however, had been more observant; and,

as



<P>we rolled along, they discussed between them not only his looks,



<P>words, and actions, but every feature of his face, and every



<P>article of his apparel.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You shan't have him all to yourself, Rosalie,' said Miss Matilda



<P>at the close of this discussion; 'I like him:&nbsp; I know he'd make

a



<P>nice, jolly companion for me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, you're quite welcome to him, Matilda,' replied her sister,



<P>in a tone of affected indifference.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And I'm sure,' continued the other, 'he admires me quite as much



<P>as he does you; doesn't he, Miss Grey?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I don't know; I'm not acquainted with his sentiments.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, but he DOES though.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'My DEAR Matilda! nobody will ever admire you till you get rid of



<P>your rough, awkward manners.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, stuff!&nbsp; Harry Meltham likes such manners; and so do papa's



<P>friends.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, you MAY captivate old men, and younger sons; but nobody



<P>else, I am sure, will ever take a fancy to you.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I don't care:&nbsp; I'm not always grabbing after money, like you and



<P>mamma.&nbsp; If my husband is able to keep a few good horses and dogs,

I



<P>shall be quite satisfied; and all the rest may go to the devil!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, if you use such shocking expressions, I'm sure no real



<P>gentleman will ever venture to come near you.&nbsp; Really, Miss Grey,



<P>you should not let her do so.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I can't possibly prevent it, Miss Murray.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And you're quite mistaken, Matilda, in supposing that Harry



<P>Meltham admires you:&nbsp; I assure you he does nothing of the kind.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Matilda was beginning an angry reply; but, happily, our journey was



<P>now at an end; and the contention was cut short by the footman



<P>opening the carriage-door, and letting down the steps for our



<P>descent.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XI - THE COTTAGERS</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>AS I had now only one regular pupil - though she contrived to give



<P>me as much trouble as three or four ordinary ones, and though her



<P>sister still took lessons in German and drawing - I had



<P>considerably more time at my own disposal than I had ever been



<P>blessed with before, since I had taken upon me the governess's



<P>yoke; which time I devoted partly to correspondence with my



<P>friends, partly to reading, study, and the practice of music,



<P>singing, &amp;c., partly to wandering in the grounds or adjacent



<P>fields, with my pupils if they wanted me, alone if they did not.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Often, when they had no more agreeable occupation at hand, the



<P>Misses Murray would amuse themselves with visiting the poor



<P>cottagers on their father's estate, to receive their flattering



<P>homage, or to hear the old stories or gossiping news of the



<P>garrulous old women; or, perhaps, to enjoy the purer pleasure of



<P>making the poor people happy with their cheering presence and their



<P>occasional gifts, so easily bestowed, so thankfully received.



<P>Sometimes, I was called upon to accompany one or both of the



<P>sisters in these visits; and sometimes I was desired to go alone,



<P>to fulfil some promise which they had been more ready to make than



<P>to perform; to carry some small donation, or read to one who was



<P>sick or seriously disposed:&nbsp; and thus I made a few acquaintances



<P>among the cottagers; and, occasionally, I went to see them on my



<P>own account.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I generally had more satisfaction in going alone than with either



<P>of the young ladies; for they, chiefly owing to their defective



<P>education, comported themselves towards their inferiors in a manner



<P>that was highly disagreeable for me to witness.&nbsp; They never, in



<P>thought, exchanged places with them; and, consequently, had no



<P>consideration for their feelings, regarding them as an order of



<P>beings entirely different from themselves.&nbsp; They would watch the



<P>poor creatures at their meals, making uncivil remarks about their



<P>food, and their manner of eating; they would laugh at their simple



<P>notions and provincial expressions, till some of them scarcely



<P>durst venture to speak; they would call the grave elderly men and



<P>women old fools and silly old blockheads to their faces:&nbsp; and all



<P>this without meaning to offend.&nbsp; I could see that the people were



<P>often hurt and annoyed by such conduct, though their fear of the



<P>'grand ladies' prevented them from testifying any resentment; but



<P>THEY never perceived it.&nbsp; They thought that, as these cottagers



<P>were poor and untaught, they must be stupid and brutish; and as



<P>long as they, their superiors, condescended to talk to them, and to



<P>give them shillings and half-crowns, or articles of clothing, they



<P>had a right to amuse themselves, even at their expense; and the



<P>people must adore them as angels of light, condescending to



<P>minister to their necessities, and enlighten their humble



<P>dwellings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I made many and various attempts to deliver my pupils from these



<P>delusive notions without alarming their pride - which was easily



<P>offended, and not soon appeased - but with little apparent result;



<P>and I know not which was the more reprehensible of the two:



<P>Matilda was more rude and boisterous; but from Rosalie's womanly



<P>age and lady-like exterior better things were expected:&nbsp; yet she



<P>was as provokingly careless and inconsiderate as a giddy child of



<P>twelve.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One bright day in the last week of February, I was walking in the



<P>park, enjoying the threefold luxury of solitude, a book, and



<P>pleasant weather; for Miss Matilda had set out on her daily ride,



<P>and Miss Murray was gone in the carriage with her mamma to pay some



<P>morning calls.&nbsp; But it struck me that I ought to leave these



<P>selfish pleasures, and the park with its glorious canopy of bright



<P>blue sky, the west wind sounding through its yet leafless branches,



<P>the snow-wreaths still lingering in its hollows, but melting fast



<P>beneath the sun, and the graceful deer browsing on its moist



<P>herbage already assuming the freshness and verdure of spring - and



<P>go to the cottage of one Nancy Brown, a widow, whose son was at



<P>work all day in the fields, and who was afflicted with an



<P>inflammation in the eyes; which had for some time incapacitated her



<P>from reading:&nbsp; to her own great grief, for she was a woman of a



<P>serious, thoughtful turn of mind.&nbsp; I accordingly went, and found



<P>her alone, as usual, in her little, close, dark cottage, redolent



<P>of smoke and confined air, but as tidy and clean as she could make



<P>it.&nbsp; She was seated beside her little fire (consisting of a few

red



<P>cinders and a bit of stick), busily knitting, with a small



<P>sackcloth cushion at her feet, placed for the accommodation of her



<P>gentle friend the cat, who was seated thereon, with her long tail



<P>half encircling her velvet paws, and her half-closed eyes dreamily



<P>gazing on the low, crooked fender.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, Nancy, how are you to-day?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why, middling, Miss, i' myseln - my eyes is no better, but I'm a



<P>deal easier i' my mind nor I have been,' replied she, rising to



<P>welcome me with a contented smile; which I was glad to see, for



<P>Nancy had been somewhat afflicted with religious melancholy.&nbsp; I



<P>congratulated her upon the change.&nbsp; She agreed that it was a great



<P>blessing, and expressed herself 'right down thankful for it';



<P>adding, 'If it please God to spare my sight, and make me so as I



<P>can read my Bible again, I think I shall be as happy as a queen.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I hope He will, Nancy,' replied I; 'and, meantime, I'll come and



<P>read to you now and then, when I have a little time to spare.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>With expressions of grateful pleasure, the poor woman moved to get



<P>me a chair; but, as I saved her the trouble, she busied herself



<P>with stirring the fire, and adding a few more sticks to the



<P>decaying embers; and then, taking her well-used Bible from the



<P>shelf, dusted it carefully, and gave it me.&nbsp; On my asking if there



<P>was any particular part she should like me to read, she answered -

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, Miss Grey, if it's all the same to you, I should like to



<P>hear that chapter in the First Epistle of St. John, that says, "God



<P>is love, and he that dwelleth in love dwelleth in God, and God in



<P>him."'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>With a little searching, I found these words in the fourth chapter.



<P>When I came to the seventh verse she interrupted me, and, with



<P>needless apologies for such a liberty, desired me to read it very



<P>slowly, that she might take it all in, and dwell on every word;



<P>hoping I would excuse her, as she was but a 'simple body.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'The wisest person,' I replied, 'might think over each of these



<P>verses for an hour, and be all the better for it; and I would



<P>rather read them slowly than not.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Accordingly, I finished the chapter as slowly as need be, and at



<P>the same time as impressively as I could; my auditor listened most



<P>attentively all the while, and sincerely thanked me when I had



<P>done.&nbsp; I sat still about half a minute to give her time to reflect



<P>upon it; when, somewhat to my surprise, she broke the pause by



<P>asking me how I liked Mr. Weston?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I don't know,' I replied, a little startled by the suddenness of



<P>the question; 'I think he preaches very well.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Ay, he does so; and talks well too.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Does he?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'He does.&nbsp; Maybe, you haven't seen him - not to talk to him much,



<P>yet?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, I never see any one to talk to - except the young ladies of



<P>the Hall.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Ah; they're nice, kind young ladies; but they can't talk as he



<P>does.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Then he comes to see you, Nancy?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'He does, Miss; and I'se thankful for it.&nbsp; He comes to see all

us



<P>poor bodies a deal ofter nor Maister Bligh, or th' Rector ever did;



<P>an' it's well he does, for he's always welcome:&nbsp; we can't say as



<P>much for th' Rector - there is 'at says they're fair feared on him.



<P>When he comes into a house, they say he's sure to find summut



<P>wrong, and begin a-calling 'em as soon as he crosses th' doorstuns:



<P>but maybe he thinks it his duty like to tell 'em what's wrong.&nbsp;

And



<P>very oft he comes o' purpose to reprove folk for not coming to



<P>church, or not kneeling an' standing when other folk does, or going



<P>to the Methody chapel, or summut o' that sort:&nbsp; but I can't say

'at



<P>he ever fund much fault wi' me.&nbsp; He came to see me once or twice,



<P>afore Maister Weston come, when I was so ill troubled in my mind;



<P>and as I had only very poor health besides, I made bold to send for



<P>him - and he came right enough.&nbsp; I was sore distressed, Miss Grey

-



<P>thank God, it's owered now - but when I took my Bible, I could get



<P>no comfort of it at all.&nbsp; That very chapter 'at you've just been



<P>reading troubled me as much as aught - "He that loveth not, knoweth



<P>not God."&nbsp; It seemed fearsome to me; for I felt that I loved



<P>neither God nor man as I should do, and could not, if I tried ever



<P>so.&nbsp; And th' chapter afore, where it says, - "He that is born of



<P>God cannot commit sin."&nbsp; And another place where it says, - "Love



<P>is the fulfilling of the Law."&nbsp; And many, many others, Miss:&nbsp;

I



<P>should fair weary you out, if I was to tell them all.&nbsp; But all



<P>seemed to condemn me, and to show me 'at I was not in the right



<P>way; and as I knew not how to get into it, I sent our Bill to beg



<P>Maister Hatfield to be as kind as look in on me some day and when



<P>he came, I telled him all my troubles.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And what did he say, Nancy?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why, Miss, he seemed to scorn me.&nbsp; I might be mista'en - but he



<P>like gave a sort of a whistle, and I saw a bit of a smile on his



<P>face; and he said, "Oh, it's all stuff!&nbsp; You've been among the



<P>Methodists, my good woman."&nbsp; But I telled him I'd never been near



<P>the Methodies.&nbsp; And then he said, - "Well," says he, "you must

come



<P>to church, where you'll hear the Scriptures properly explained,



<P>instead of sitting poring over your Bible at home."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But I telled him I always used coming to church when I had my



<P>health; but this very cold winter weather I hardly durst venture so



<P>far - and me so bad wi' th' rheumatic and all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But he says, "It'll do your rheumatiz good to hobble to church:



<P>there's nothing like exercise for the rheumatiz.&nbsp; You can walk



<P>about the house well enough; why can't you walk to church?&nbsp; The



<P>fact is," says he, "you're getting too fond of your ease.&nbsp; It's



<P>always easy to find excuses for shirking one's duty."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But then, you know, Miss Grey, it wasn't so.&nbsp; However, I telled



<P>him I'd try.&nbsp; "But please, sir," says I, "if I do go to church,



<P>what the better shall I be?&nbsp; I want to have my sins blotted out,



<P>and to feel that they are remembered no more against me, and that



<P>the love of God is shed abroad in my heart; and if I can get no



<P>good by reading my Bible an' saying my prayers at home, what good



<P>shall I get by going to church?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"The church," says he, "is the place appointed by God for His



<P>worship.&nbsp; It's your duty to go there as often as you can.&nbsp;

If you



<P>want comfort, you must seek it in the path of duty," - an' a deal



<P>more he said, but I cannot remember all his fine words.&nbsp; However,



<P>it all came to this, that I was to come to church as oft as ever I



<P>could, and bring my prayer-book with me, an' read up all the



<P>sponsers after the clerk, an' stand, an' kneel, an' sit, an' do all



<P>as I should, and take the Lord's Supper at every opportunity, an'



<P>hearken his sermons, and Maister Bligh's, an' it 'ud be all right:



<P>if I went on doing my duty, I should get a blessing at last.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"But if you get no comfort that way," says he, "it's all up."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Then, sir," says I, "should you think I'm a reprobate?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Why," says he - he says, "if you do your best to get to heaven



<P>and can't manage it, you must be one of those that seek to enter in



<P>at the strait gate and shall not be able."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'An' then he asked me if I'd seen any of the ladies o' th' Hall



<P>about that mornin'; so I telled him where I had seen the young



<P>misses go on th' Moss Lane; - an' he kicked my poor cat right



<P>across th' floor, an' went after 'em as gay as a lark:&nbsp; but I was



<P>very sad.&nbsp; That last word o' his fair sunk into my heart, an' lay



<P>there like a lump o' lead, till I was weary to bear it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Howsever, I follered his advice:&nbsp; I thought he meant it all for



<P>th' best, though he HAD a queer way with him.&nbsp; But you know, Miss,



<P>he's rich an' young, and such like cannot right understand the



<P>thoughts of a poor old woman such as me.&nbsp; But, howsever, I did

my



<P>best to do all as he bade me - but maybe I'm plaguing you, Miss,



<P>wi' my chatter.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, no, Nancy!&nbsp; Go on, and tell me all.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, my rheumatiz got better - I know not whether wi' going to



<P>church or not, but one frosty Sunday I got this cold i' my eyes.



<P>Th' inflammation didn't come on all at once like, but bit by bit -



<P>but I wasn't going to tell you about my eyes, I was talking about



<P>my trouble o' mind; - and to tell the truth, Miss Grey, I don't



<P>think it was anyways eased by coming to church - nought to speak



<P>on, at least:&nbsp; I like got my health better; but that didn't mend

my



<P>soul.&nbsp; I hearkened and hearkened the ministers, and read an' read



<P>at my prayer-book; but it was all like sounding brass and a



<P>tinkling cymbal:&nbsp; the sermons I couldn't understand, an' th'



<P>prayer-book only served to show me how wicked I was, that I could



<P>read such good words an' never be no better for it, and oftens feel



<P>it a sore labour an' a heavy task beside, instead of a blessing and



<P>a privilege as all good Christians does.&nbsp; It seemed like as all



<P>were barren an' dark to me.&nbsp; And then, them dreadful words, "Many



<P>shall seek to enter in, and shall not be able."&nbsp; They like as they



<P>fair dried up my sperrit.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But one Sunday, when Maister Hatfield gave out about the



<P>sacrament, I noticed where he said, "If there be any of you that



<P>cannot quiet his own conscience, but requireth further comfort or



<P>counsel, let him come to me, or some other discreet and learned



<P>minister of God's word, and open his grief!"&nbsp; So next Sunday



<P>morning, afore service, I just looked into the vestry, an' began a-



<P>talking to th' Rector again.&nbsp; I hardly could fashion to take such

a



<P>liberty, but I thought when my soul was at stake I shouldn't stick



<P>at a trifle.&nbsp; But he said he hadn't time to attend to me then.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"And, indeed," says he, "I've nothing to say to you but what I've



<P>said before.&nbsp; Take the sacrament, of course, and go on doing your



<P>duty; and if that won't serve you, nothing will.&nbsp; So don't bother



<P>me any more."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'So then, I went away.&nbsp; But I heard Maister Weston - Maister Weston



<P>was there, Miss - this was his first Sunday at Horton, you know,



<P>an' he was i' th' vestry in his surplice, helping th' Rector on



<P>with his gown - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, Nancy.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And I heard him ask Maister Hatfield who I was, an' he says, "Oh,



<P>she's a canting old fool."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And I was very ill grieved, Miss Grey; but I went to my seat, and



<P>I tried to do my duty as aforetime:&nbsp; but I like got no peace.&nbsp;

An'



<P>I even took the sacrament; but I felt as though I were eating and



<P>drinking to my own damnation all th' time.&nbsp; So I went home, sorely



<P>troubled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But next day, afore I'd gotten fettled up - for indeed, Miss, I'd



<P>no heart to sweeping an' fettling, an' washing pots; so I sat me



<P>down i' th' muck - who should come in but Maister Weston!&nbsp; I



<P>started siding stuff then, an' sweeping an' doing; and I expected



<P>he'd begin a-calling me for my idle ways, as Maister Hatfield would



<P>a' done; but I was mista'en:&nbsp; he only bid me good-mornin' like,

in



<P>a quiet dacent way.&nbsp; So I dusted him a chair, an' fettled up th'



<P>fireplace a bit; but I hadn't forgotten th' Rector's words, so says



<P>I, "I wonder, sir, you should give yourself that trouble, to come



<P>so far to see a 'canting old fool,' such as me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'He seemed taken aback at that; but he would fain persuade me 'at



<P>the Rector was only in jest; and when that wouldn't do, he says,



<P>"Well, Nancy, you shouldn't think so much about it:&nbsp; Mr. Hatfield



<P>was a little out of humour just then:&nbsp; you know we're none of us



<P>perfect - even Moses spoke unadvisedly with his lips.&nbsp; But now

sit



<P>down a minute, if you can spare the time, and tell me all your



<P>doubts and fears; and I'll try to remove them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'So I sat me down anent him.&nbsp; He was quite a stranger, you know,



<P>Miss Grey, and even YOUNGER nor Maister Hatfield, I believe; and I



<P>had thought him not so pleasant-looking as him, and rather a bit



<P>crossish, at first, to look at; but he spake so civil like - and



<P>when th' cat, poor thing, jumped on to his knee, he only stroked



<P>her, and gave a bit of a smile:&nbsp; so I thought that was a good sign;



<P>for once, when she did so to th' Rector, he knocked her off, like



<P>as it might be in scorn and anger, poor thing.&nbsp; But you can't



<P>expect a cat to know manners like a Christian, you know, Miss



<P>Grey.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No; of course not, Nancy.&nbsp; But what did Mr. Weston say then?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'He said nought; but he listened to me as steady an' patient as



<P>could be, an' never a bit o' scorn about him; so I went on, an'



<P>telled him all, just as I've telled you - an' more too.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Well," says he, "Mr. Hatfield was quite right in telling you to



<P>persevere in doing your duty; but in advising you to go to church



<P>and attend to the service, and so on, he didn't mean that was the



<P>whole of a Christian's duty:&nbsp; he only thought you might there learn



<P>what more was to be done, and be led to take delight in those



<P>exercises, instead of finding them a task and a burden.&nbsp; And if

you



<P>had asked him to explain those words that trouble you so much, I



<P>think he would have told you, that if many shall seek to enter in



<P>at the strait gate and shall not be able, it is their own sins that



<P>hinder them; just as a man with a large sack on his back might wish



<P>to pass through a narrow doorway, and find it impossible to do so



<P>unless he would leave his sack behind him.&nbsp; But you, Nancy, I dare



<P>say, have no sins that you would not gladly throw aside, if you



<P>knew how?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Indeed, sir, you speak truth," said I.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Well," says he, "you know the first and great commandment - and



<P>the second, which is like unto it - on which two commandments hang



<P>all the law and the prophets?&nbsp; You say you cannot love God; but

it



<P>strikes me that if you rightly consider who and what He is, you



<P>cannot help it.&nbsp; He is your father, your best friend:&nbsp; every



<P>blessing, everything good, pleasant, or useful, comes from Him; and



<P>everything evil, everything you have reason to hate, to shun, or to



<P>fear, comes from Satan - HIS enemy as well as ours.&nbsp; And for THIS



<P>cause was God manifest in the flesh, that He might destroy the



<P>works of the Devil:&nbsp; in one word, God is LOVE; and the more of

love



<P>we have within us, the nearer we are to Him and the more of His



<P>spirit we possess."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Well, sir," I said, "if I can always think on these things, I



<P>think I might well love God:&nbsp; but how can I love my neighbours,



<P>when they vex me, and be so contrary and sinful as some on 'em is?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"It may seem a hard matter," says he, "to love our neighbours, who



<P>have so much of what is evil about them, and whose faults so often



<P>awaken the evil that lingers within ourselves; but remember that HE



<P>made them, and HE loves them; and whosoever loveth him that begat,



<P>loveth him that is begotten also.&nbsp; And if God so loveth us, that

He



<P>gave His only begotten Son to die for us, we ought also to love one



<P>another.&nbsp; But if you cannot feel positive affection for those who



<P>do not care for you, you can at least try to do to them as you



<P>would they should do unto you:&nbsp; you can endeavour to pity their



<P>failings and excuse their offences, and to do all the good you can



<P>to those about you.&nbsp; And if you accustom yourself to this, Nancy,



<P>the very effort itself will make you love them in some degree - to



<P>say nothing of the goodwill your kindness would beget in them,



<P>though they might have little else that is good about them.&nbsp; If

we



<P>love God and wish to serve Him, let us try to be like Him, to do



<P>His work, to labour for His glory - which is the good of man - to



<P>hasten the coming of His kingdom, which is the peace and happiness



<P>of all the world:&nbsp; however powerless we may seem to be, in doing



<P>all the good we can through life, the humblest of us may do much



<P>towards it:&nbsp; and let us dwell in love, that He may dwell in us

and



<P>we in Him.&nbsp; The more happiness we bestow, the more we shall



<P>receive, even here; and the greater will be our reward in heaven



<P>when we rest from our labours."&nbsp; I believe, Miss, them is his very



<P>words, for I've thought 'em ower many a time.&nbsp; An' then he took



<P>that Bible, an' read bits here and there, an' explained 'em as



<P>clear as the day:&nbsp; and it seemed like as a new light broke in on

my



<P>soul; an' I felt fair aglow about my heart, an' only wished poor



<P>Bill an' all the world could ha' been there, an' heard it all, and



<P>rejoiced wi' me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'After he was gone, Hannah Rogers, one o' th' neighbours, came in



<P>and wanted me to help her to wash.&nbsp; I telled her I couldn't just



<P>then, for I hadn't set on th' potaties for th' dinner, nor washed



<P>up th' breakfast stuff yet.&nbsp; So then she began a-calling me for

my



<P>nasty idle ways.&nbsp; I was a little bit vexed at first, but I never



<P>said nothing wrong to her:&nbsp; I only telled her like all in a quiet



<P>way, 'at I'd had th' new parson to see me; but I'd get done as



<P>quick as ever I could, an' then come an' help her.&nbsp; So then she



<P>softened down; and my heart like as it warmed towards her, an' in a



<P>bit we was very good friends.&nbsp; An' so it is, Miss Grey, "a soft



<P>answer turneth away wrath; but grievous words stir up anger."&nbsp;

It



<P>isn't only in them you speak to, but in yourself.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Very true, Nancy, if we could always remember it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Ay, if we could!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And did Mr. Weston ever come to see you again?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, many a time; and since my eyes has been so bad, he's sat an'



<P>read to me by the half-hour together:&nbsp; but you know, Miss, he has



<P>other folks to see, and other things to do - God bless him!&nbsp; An'



<P>that next Sunday he preached SUCH a sermon!&nbsp; His text was, "Come



<P>unto me all ye that labour and are heavy laden, and I will give you



<P>rest," and them two blessed verses that follows.&nbsp; You wasn't there,



<P>Miss, you was with your friends then - but it made me SO happy!



<P>And I AM happy now, thank God! an' I take a pleasure, now, in doing



<P>little bits o' jobs for my neighbours - such as a poor old body



<P>'at's half blind can do; and they take it kindly of me, just as he



<P>said.&nbsp; You see, Miss, I'm knitting a pair o' stockings now; -



<P>they're for Thomas Jackson:&nbsp; he's a queerish old body, an' we've



<P>had many a bout at threaping, one anent t'other; an' at times we've



<P>differed sorely.&nbsp; So I thought I couldn't do better nor knit him

a



<P>pair o' warm stockings; an' I've felt to like him a deal better,



<P>poor old man, sin' I began.&nbsp; It's turned out just as Maister Weston



<P>said.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, I'm very glad to see you so happy, Nancy, and so wise:&nbsp;

but



<P>I must go now; I shall be wanted at the Hall,' said I; and bidding



<P>her good-bye, I departed, promising to come again when I had time,



<P>and feeling nearly as happy as herself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At another time I went to read to a poor labourer who was in the



<P>last stage of consumption.&nbsp; The young ladies had been to see him,



<P>and somehow a promise of reading had been extracted from them; but



<P>it was too much trouble, so they begged me to do it instead.&nbsp; I



<P>went, willingly enough; and there too I was gratified with the



<P>praises of Mr. Weston, both from the sick man and his wife.&nbsp; The



<P>former told me that he derived great comfort and benefit from the



<P>visits of the new parson, who frequently came to see him, and was



<P>'another guess sort of man' to Mr. Hatfield; who, before the



<P>other's arrival at Horton, had now and then paid him a visit; on



<P>which occasions he would always insist upon having the cottage-door



<P>kept open, to admit the fresh air for his own convenience, without



<P>considering how it might injure the sufferer; and having opened his



<P>prayer-book and hastily read over a part of the Service for the



<P>Sick, would hurry away again:&nbsp; if he did not stay to administer



<P>some harsh rebuke to the afflicted wife, or to make some



<P>thoughtless, not to say heartless, observation, rather calculated



<P>to increase than diminish the troubles of the suffering pair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Whereas,' said the man, 'Maister Weston 'ull pray with me quite in



<P>a different fashion, an' talk to me as kind as owt; an' oft read to



<P>me too, an' sit beside me just like a brother.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Just for all the world!' exclaimed his wife; 'an' about a three



<P>wik sin', when he seed how poor Jem shivered wi' cold, an' what



<P>pitiful fires we kept, he axed if wer stock of coals was nearly



<P>done.&nbsp; I telled him it was, an' we was ill set to get more:&nbsp;

but



<P>you know, mum, I didn't think o' him helping us; but, howsever, he



<P>sent us a sack o' coals next day; an' we've had good fires ever



<P>sin':&nbsp; and a great blessing it is, this winter time.&nbsp; But

that's



<P>his way, Miss Grey:&nbsp; when he comes into a poor body's house a-



<P>seein' sick folk, he like notices what they most stand i' need on;



<P>an' if he thinks they can't readily get it therseln, he never says



<P>nowt about it, but just gets it for 'em.&nbsp; An' it isn't everybody



<P>'at 'ud do that, 'at has as little as he has:&nbsp; for you know, mum,



<P>he's nowt at all to live on but what he gets fra' th' Rector, an'



<P>that's little enough they say.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I remembered then, with a species of exultation, that he had



<P>frequently been styled a vulgar brute by the amiable Miss Murray,



<P>because he wore a silver watch, and clothes not quite so bright and



<P>fresh as Mr. Hatfield's.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In returning to the Lodge I felt very happy, and thanked God that I



<P>had now something to think about; something to dwell on as a relief



<P>from the weary monotony, the lonely drudgery, of my present life:



<P>for I WAS lonely.&nbsp; Never, from month to month, from year to year,



<P>except during my brief intervals of rest at home, did I see one



<P>creature to whom I could open my heart, or freely speak my thoughts



<P>with any hope of sympathy, or even comprehension:&nbsp; never one,



<P>unless it were poor Nancy Brown, with whom I could enjoy a single



<P>moment of real social intercourse, or whose conversation was



<P>calculated to render me better, wiser, or happier than before; or



<P>who, as far as I could see, could be greatly benefited by mine.&nbsp;

My



<P>only companions had been unamiable children, and ignorant, wrong-



<P>headed girls; from whose fatiguing folly, unbroken solitude was



<P>often a relief most earnestly desired and dearly prized.&nbsp; But to

be



<P>restricted to such associates was a serious evil, both in its



<P>immediate effects and the consequences that were likely to ensue.



<P>Never a new idea or stirring thought came to me from without; and



<P>such as rose within me were, for the most part, miserably crushed



<P>at once, or doomed to sicken or fade away, because they could not



<P>see the light.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Habitual associates are known to exercise a great influence over



<P>each other's minds and manners.&nbsp; Those whose actions are for ever



<P>before our eyes, whose words are ever in our ears, will naturally



<P>lead us, albeit against our will, slowly, gradually, imperceptibly,



<P>perhaps, to act and speak as they do.&nbsp; I will not presume to say



<P>how far this irresistible power of assimilation extends; but if one



<P>civilised man were doomed to pass a dozen years amid a race of



<P>intractable savages, unless he had power to improve them, I greatly



<P>question whether, at the close of that period, he would not have



<P>become, at least, a barbarian himself.&nbsp; And I, as I could not make



<P>my young companions better, feared exceedingly that they would make



<P>me worse - would gradually bring my feelings, habits, capacities,



<P>to the level of their own; without, however, imparting to me their



<P>lightheartedness and cheerful vivacity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Already, I seemed to feel my intellect deteriorating, my heart



<P>petrifying, my soul contracting; and I trembled lest my very moral



<P>perceptions should become deadened, my distinctions of right and



<P>wrong confounded, and all my better faculties be sunk, at last,



<P>beneath the baneful influence of such a mode of life.&nbsp; The gross



<P>vapours of earth were gathering around me, and closing in upon my



<P>inward heaven; and thus it was that Mr. Weston rose at length upon



<P>me, appearing like the morning star in my horizon, to save me from



<P>the fear of utter darkness; and I rejoiced that I had now a subject



<P>for contemplation that was above me, not beneath.&nbsp; I was glad to



<P>see that all the world was not made up of Bloomfields, Murrays,



<P>Hatfields, Ashbys, &amp;c.; and that human excellence was not a mere



<P>dream of the imagination.&nbsp; When we hear a little good and no harm



<P>of a person, it is easy and pleasant to imagine more:&nbsp; in short,

it



<P>is needless to analyse all my thoughts; but Sunday was now become a



<P>day of peculiar delight to me (I was now almost broken-in to the



<P>back corner in the carriage), for I liked to hear him - and I liked



<P>to see him, too; though I knew he was not handsome, or even what is



<P>called agreeable, in outward aspect; but, certainly, he was not



<P>ugly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In stature he was a little, a very little, above the middle size;



<P>the outline of his face would be pronounced too square for beauty,



<P>but to me it announced decision of character; his dark brown hair



<P>was not carefully curled, like Mr. Hatfield's, but simply brushed



<P>aside over a broad white forehead; the eyebrows, I suppose, were



<P>too projecting, but from under those dark brows there gleamed an



<P>eye of singular power, brown in colour, not large, and somewhat



<P>deep-set, but strikingly brilliant, and full of expression; there



<P>was character, too, in the mouth, something that bespoke a man of



<P>firm purpose and an habitual thinker; and when he smiled - but I



<P>will not speak of that yet, for, at the time I mention, I had never



<P>seen him smile:&nbsp; and, indeed, his general appearance did not



<P>impress me with the idea of a man given to such a relaxation, nor



<P>of such an individual as the cottagers described him.&nbsp; I had early



<P>formed my opinion of him; and, in spite of Miss Murray's



<P>objurgations:&nbsp; was fully convinced that he was a man of strong



<P>sense, firm faith, and ardent piety, but thoughtful and stern:&nbsp;

and



<P>when I found that, to his other good qualities, was added that of



<P>true benevolence and gentle, considerate kindness, the discovery,



<P>perhaps, delighted me the more, as I had not been prepared to



<P>expect it.



<P>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">
