<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER V - THE UNCLE</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>BESIDES the old lady, there was another relative of the family,



<P>whose visits were a great annoyance to me - this was 'Uncle



<P>Robson,' Mrs. Bloomfield's brother; a tall, self-sufficient fellow,



<P>with dark hair and sallow complexion like his sister, a nose that



<P>seemed to disdain the earth, and little grey eyes, frequently half-



<P>closed, with a mixture of real stupidity and affected contempt of



<P>all surrounding objects.&nbsp; He was a thick-set, strongly-built man,



<P>but he had found some means of compressing his waist into a



<P>remarkably small compass; and that, together with the unnatural



<P>stillness of his form, showed that the lofty-minded, manly Mr.



<P>Robson, the scorner of the female sex, was not above the foppery of



<P>stays.&nbsp; He seldom deigned to notice me; and, when he did, it was



<P>with a certain supercilious insolence of tone and manner that



<P>convinced me he was no gentleman:&nbsp; though it was intended to have

a



<P>contrary effect.&nbsp; But it was not for that I disliked his coming,

so



<P>much as for the harm he did the children - encouraging all their



<P>evil propensities, and undoing in a few minutes the little good it



<P>had taken me months of labour to achieve.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Fanny and little Harriet he seldom condescended to notice; but Mary



<P>Ann was something of a favourite.&nbsp; He was continually encouraging



<P>her tendency to affectation (which I had done my utmost to crush),



<P>talking about her pretty face, and filling her head with all manner



<P>of conceited notions concerning her personal appearance (which I



<P>had instructed her to regard as dust in the balance compared with



<P>the cultivation of her mind and manners); and I never saw a child



<P>so susceptible of flattery as she was.&nbsp; Whatever was wrong, in



<P>either her or her brother, he would encourage by laughing at, if



<P>not by actually praising:&nbsp; people little know the injury they do

to



<P>children by laughing at their faults, and making a pleasant jest of



<P>what their true friends have endeavoured to teach them to hold in



<P>grave abhorrence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Though not a positive drunkard, Mr. Robson habitually swallowed



<P>great quantities of wine, and took with relish an occasional glass



<P>of brandy and water.&nbsp; He taught his nephew to imitate him in this



<P>to the utmost of his ability, and to believe that the more wine and



<P>spirits he could take, and the better he liked them, the more he



<P>manifested his bold, and manly spirit, and rose superior to his



<P>sisters.&nbsp; Mr. Bloomfield had not much to say against it, for his



<P>favourite beverage was gin and water; of which he took a



<P>considerable portion every day, by dint of constant sipping - and



<P>to that I chiefly attributed his dingy complexion and waspish



<P>temper.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr. Robson likewise encouraged Tom's propensity to persecute the



<P>lower creation, both by precept and example.&nbsp; As he frequently

came



<P>to course or shoot over his brother-in-law's grounds, he would



<P>bring his favourite dogs with him; and he treated them so brutally



<P>that, poor as I was, I would have given a sovereign any day to see



<P>one of them bite him, provided the animal could have done it with



<P>impunity.&nbsp; Sometimes, when in a very complacent mood, he would

go



<P>a-birds'-nesting with the children, a thing that irritated and



<P>annoyed me exceedingly; as, by frequent and persevering attempts, I



<P>flattered myself I had partly shown them the evil of this pastime,



<P>and hoped, in time, to bring them to some general sense of justice



<P>and humanity; but ten minutes' birds'-nesting with uncle Robson, or



<P>even a laugh from him at some relation of their former barbarities,



<P>was sufficient at once to destroy the effect of my whole elaborate



<P>course of reasoning and persuasion.&nbsp; Happily, however, during that



<P>spring, they never, but once, got anything but empty nests, or eggs



<P>- being too impatient to leave them till the birds were hatched;



<P>that once, Tom, who had been with his uncle into the neighbouring



<P>plantation, came running in high glee into the garden, with a brood



<P>of little callow nestlings in his hands.&nbsp; Mary Ann and Fanny, whom



<P>I was just bringing out, ran to admire his spoils, and to beg each



<P>a bird for themselves.&nbsp; 'No, not one!' cried Tom.&nbsp; 'They're

all



<P>mine; uncle Robson gave them to me - one, two, three, four, five -



<P>you shan't touch one of them! no, not one, for your lives!'



<P>continued he, exultingly; laying the nest on the ground, and



<P>standing over it with his legs wide apart, his hands thrust into



<P>his breeches-pockets, his body bent forward, and his face twisted



<P>into all manner of contortions in the ecstasy of his delight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But you shall see me fettle 'em off.&nbsp; My word, but I WILL wallop



<P>'em?&nbsp; See if I don't now.&nbsp; By gum! but there's rare sport

for me in



<P>that nest.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But, Tom,' said I, 'I shall not allow you to torture those birds.



<P>They must either be killed at once or carried back to the place you



<P>took them from, that the old birds may continue to feed them.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But you don't know where that is, Madam:&nbsp; it's only me and uncle



<P>Robson that knows that.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But if you don't tell me, I shall kill them myself - much as I



<P>hate it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You daren't.&nbsp; You daren't touch them for your life! because you



<P>know papa and mamma, and uncle Robson, would be angry.&nbsp; Ha, ha!



<P>I've caught you there, Miss!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I shall do what I think right in a case of this sort without



<P>consulting any one.&nbsp; If your papa and mamma don't happen to approve



<P>of it, I shall be sorry to offend them; but your uncle Robson's



<P>opinions, of course, are nothing to me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So saying - urged by a sense of duty - at the risk of both making



<P>myself sick and incurring the wrath of my employers - I got a large



<P>flat stone, that had been reared up for a mouse-trap by the



<P>gardener; then, having once more vainly endeavoured to persuade the



<P>little tyrant to let the birds be carried back, I asked what he



<P>intended to do with them.&nbsp; With fiendish glee he commenced a list



<P>of torments; and while he was busied in the relation, I dropped the



<P>stone upon his intended victims and crushed them flat beneath it.



<P>Loud were the outcries, terrible the execrations, consequent upon



<P>this daring outrage; uncle Robson had been coming up the walk with



<P>his gun, and was just then pausing to kick his dog.&nbsp; Tom flew



<P>towards him, vowing he would make him kick me instead of Juno.&nbsp;

Mr.



<P>Robson leant upon his gun, and laughed excessively at the violence



<P>of his nephew's passion, and the bitter maledictions and



<P>opprobrious epithets he heaped upon me.&nbsp; 'Well, you ARE a good



<P>'un!' exclaimed he, at length, taking up his weapon and proceeding



<P>towards the house.&nbsp; 'Damme, but the lad has some spunk in him,

too.



<P>Curse me, if ever I saw a nobler little scoundrel than that.&nbsp; He's



<P>beyond petticoat government already:&nbsp; by God! he defies mother,



<P>granny, governess, and all!&nbsp; Ha, ha, ha!&nbsp; Never mind, Tom,

I'll get



<P>you another brood to-morrow.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'If you do, Mr. Robson, I shall kill them too,' said I.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Humph!' replied he, and having honoured me with a broad stare -



<P>which, contrary to his expectations, I sustained without flinching



<P>- he turned away with an air of supreme contempt, and stalked into



<P>the house.&nbsp; Tom next went to tell his mamma.&nbsp; It was not her

way to



<P>say much on any subject; but, when she next saw me, her aspect and



<P>demeanour were doubly dark and chilled.&nbsp; After some casual remark



<P>about the weather, she observed - 'I am sorry, Miss Grey, you



<P>should think it necessary to interfere with Master Bloomfield's



<P>amusements; he was very much distressed about your destroying the



<P>birds.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'When Master Bloomfield's amusements consist in injuring sentient



<P>creatures,' I answered, 'I think it my duty to interfere.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You seemed to have forgotten,' said she, calmly, 'that the



<P>creatures were all created for our convenience.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I thought that doctrine admitted some doubt, but merely replied -



<P>'If they were, we have no right to torment them for our amusement.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I think,' said she, 'a child's amusement is scarcely to be weighed



<P>against the welfare of a soulless brute.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But, for the child's own sake, it ought not to be encouraged to



<P>have such amusements,' answered I, as meekly as I could, to make up



<P>for such unusual pertinacity.&nbsp; '"Blessed are the merciful, for

they



<P>shall obtain mercy."'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh! of course; but that refers to our conduct towards each other.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"The merciful man shows mercy to his beast,"' I ventured to add.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I think YOU have not shown much mercy,' replied she, with a short,



<P>bitter laugh; 'killing the poor birds by wholesale in that shocking



<P>manner, and putting the dear boy to such misery for a mere whim.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I judged it prudent to say no more.&nbsp; This was the nearest approach



<P>to a quarrel I ever had with Mrs. Bloomfield; as well as the



<P>greatest number of words I ever exchanged with her at one time,



<P>since the day of my first arrival.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But Mr. Robson and old Mrs. Bloomfield were not the only guests



<P>whose coming to Wellwood House annoyed me; every visitor disturbed



<P>me more or less; not so much because they neglected me (though I



<P>did feel their conduct strange and disagreeable in that respect),



<P>as because I found it impossible to keep my pupils away from them,



<P>as I was repeatedly desired to do:&nbsp; Tom must talk to them, and

Mary



<P>Ann must be noticed by them.&nbsp; Neither the one nor the other knew



<P>what it was to feel any degree of shamefacedness, or even common



<P>modesty.&nbsp; They would indecently and clamorously interrupt the



<P>conversation of their elders, tease them with the most impertinent



<P>questions, roughly collar the gentlemen, climb their knees



<P>uninvited, hang about their shoulders or rifle their pockets, pull



<P>the ladies' gowns, disorder their hair, tumble their collars, and



<P>importunately beg for their trinkets.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mrs. Bloomfield had the sense to be shocked and annoyed at all



<P>this, but she had not sense to prevent it:&nbsp; she expected me to



<P>prevent it.&nbsp; But how could I - when the guests, with their fine



<P>clothes and new faces, continually flattered and indulged them, out



<P>of complaisance to their parents - how could I, with my homely



<P>garments, every-day face, and honest words, draw them away?&nbsp; I



<P>strained every nerve to do so:&nbsp; by striving to amuse them, I



<P>endeavoured to attract them to my side; by the exertion of such



<P>authority as I possessed, and by such severity as I dared to use, I



<P>tried to deter them from tormenting the guests; and by reproaching



<P>their unmannerly conduct, to make them ashamed to repeat it.&nbsp; But



<P>they knew no shame; they scorned authority which had no terrors to



<P>back it; and as for kindness and affection, either they had no



<P>hearts, or such as they had were so strongly guarded, and so well



<P>concealed, that I, with all my efforts, had not yet discovered how



<P>to reach them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But soon my trials in this quarter came to a close - sooner than I



<P>either expected or desired; for one sweet evening towards the close



<P>of May, as I was rejoicing in the near approach of the holidays,



<P>and congratulating myself upon having made some progress with my



<P>pupils (as far as their learning went, at least, for I HAD



<P>instilled SOMETHING into their heads, and I had, at length, brought



<P>them to be a little - a very little - more rational about getting



<P>their lessons done in time to leave some space for recreation,



<P>instead of tormenting themselves and me all day long to no



<P>purpose), Mrs. Bloomfield sent for me, and calmly told me that



<P>after Midsummer my services would be no longer required.&nbsp; She



<P>assured me that my character and general conduct were



<P>unexceptionable; but the children had made so little improvement



<P>since my arrival that Mr. Bloomfield and she felt it their duty to



<P>seek some other mode of instruction.&nbsp; Though superior to most



<P>children of their years in abilities, they were decidedly behind



<P>them in attainments; their manners were uncultivated, and their



<P>tempers unruly.&nbsp; And this she attributed to a want of sufficient



<P>firmness, and diligent, persevering care on my part.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Unshaken firmness, devoted diligence, unwearied perseverance,



<P>unceasing care, were the very qualifications on which I had



<P>secretly prided myself; and by which I had hoped in time to



<P>overcome all difficulties, and obtain success at last.&nbsp; I wished

to



<P>say something in my own justification; but in attempting to speak,



<P>I felt my voice falter; and rather than testify any emotion, or



<P>suffer the tears to overflow that were already gathering in my



<P>eyes, I chose to keep silence, and bear all like a self-convicted



<P>culprit.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Thus was I dismissed, and thus I sought my home.&nbsp; Alas! what would



<P>they think of me? unable, after all my boasting, to keep my place,



<P>even for a single year, as governess to three small children, whose



<P>mother was asserted by my own aunt to be a 'very nice woman.'



<P>Having been thus weighed in the balance and found wanting, I need



<P>not hope they would be willing to try me again.&nbsp; And this was an



<P>unwelcome thought; for vexed, harassed, disappointed as I had been,



<P>and greatly as I had learned to love and value my home, I was not



<P>yet weary of adventure, nor willing to relax my efforts.&nbsp; I knew



<P>that all parents were not like Mr. and Mrs. Bloomfield, and I was



<P>certain all children were not like theirs.&nbsp; The next family must

be



<P>different, and any change must be for the better.&nbsp; I had been



<P>seasoned by adversity, and tutored by experience, and I longed to



<P>redeem my lost honour in the eyes of those whose opinion was more



<P>than that of all the world to me.



<P>

<UL>

<UL>

<UL><B><I><FONT SIZE=+3>Agnes Grey</FONT></I></B>



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>&nbsp;by Anne Bronte</FONT></B></UL>

</UL>

</UL>





<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER I - THE PARSONAGE</FONT></B>

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>ALL true histories contain instruction; though, in some, the



<P>treasure may be hard to find, and when found, so trivial in



<P>quantity, that the dry, shrivelled kernel scarcely compensates for



<P>the trouble of cracking the nut.&nbsp; Whether this be the case with

my



<P>history or not, I am hardly competent to judge.&nbsp; I sometimes think



<P>it might prove useful to some, and entertaining to others; but the



<P>world may judge for itself.&nbsp; Shielded by my own obscurity, and

by



<P>the lapse of years, and a few fictitious names, I do not fear to



<P>venture; and will candidly lay before the public what I would not



<P>disclose to the most intimate friend.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>My father was a clergyman of the north of England, who was



<P>deservedly respected by all who knew him; and, in his younger days,



<P>lived pretty comfortably on the joint income of a small incumbency



<P>and a snug little property of his own.&nbsp; My mother, who married

him



<P>against the wishes of her friends, was a squire's daughter, and a



<P>woman of spirit.&nbsp; In vain it was represented to her, that if she



<P>became the poor parson's wife, she must relinquish her carriage and



<P>her lady's-maid, and all the luxuries and elegancies of affluence;



<P>which to her were little less than the necessaries of life.&nbsp; A



<P>carriage and a lady's-maid were great conveniences; but, thank



<P>heaven, she had feet to carry her, and hands to minister to her own



<P>necessities.&nbsp; An elegant house and spacious grounds were not to

be



<P>despised; but she would rather live in a cottage with Richard Grey



<P>than in a palace with any other man in the world.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Finding arguments of no avail, her father, at length, told the



<P>lovers they might marry if they pleased; but, in so doing, his



<P>daughter would forfeit every fraction of her fortune.&nbsp; He expected



<P>this would cool the ardour of both; but he was mistaken.&nbsp; My father



<P>knew too well my mother's superior worth not to be sensible that



<P>she was a valuable fortune in herself:&nbsp; and if she would but



<P>consent to embellish his humble hearth he should be happy to take



<P>her on any terms; while she, on her part, would rather labour with



<P>her own hands than be divided from the man she loved, whose



<P>happiness it would be her joy to make, and who was already one with



<P>her in heart and soul.&nbsp; So her fortune went to swell the purse

of a



<P>wiser sister, who had married a rich nabob; and she, to the wonder



<P>and compassionate regret of all who knew her, went to bury herself



<P>in the homely village parsonage among the hills of -.&nbsp; And yet,

in



<P>spite of all this, and in spite of my mother's high spirit and my



<P>father's whims, I believe you might search all England through, and



<P>fail to find a happier couple.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Of six children, my sister Mary and myself were the only two that



<P>survived the perils of infancy and early childhood.&nbsp; I, being the



<P>younger by five or six years, was always regarded as THE child, and



<P>the pet of the family:&nbsp; father, mother, and sister, all combined

to



<P>spoil me - not by foolish indulgence, to render me fractious and



<P>ungovernable, but by ceaseless kindness, to make me too helpless



<P>and dependent - too unfit for buffeting with the cares and turmoils



<P>of life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mary and I were brought up in the strictest seclusion.&nbsp; My mother,



<P>being at once highly accomplished, well informed, and fond of



<P>employment, took the whole charge of our education on herself, with



<P>the exception of Latin - which my father undertook to teach us - so



<P>that we never even went to school; and, as there was no society in



<P>the neighbourhood, our only intercourse with the world consisted in



<P>a stately tea-party, now and then, with the principal farmers and



<P>tradespeople of the vicinity (just to avoid being stigmatized as



<P>too proud to consort with our neighbours), and an annual visit to



<P>our paternal grandfather's; where himself, our kind grandmamma, a



<P>maiden aunt, and two or three elderly ladies and gentlemen, were



<P>the only persons we ever saw.&nbsp; Sometimes our mother would amuse

us



<P>with stories and anecdotes of her younger days, which, while they



<P>entertained us amazingly, frequently awoke - in ME, at least - a



<P>secret wish to see a little more of the world.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I thought she must have been very happy:&nbsp; but she never seemed

to



<P>regret past times.&nbsp; My father, however, whose temper was neither



<P>tranquil nor cheerful by nature, often unduly vexed himself with



<P>thinking of the sacrifices his dear wife had made for him; and



<P>troubled his head with revolving endless schemes for the



<P>augmentation of his little fortune, for her sake and ours.&nbsp; In

vain



<P>my mother assured him she was quite satisfied; and if he would but



<P>lay by a little for the children, we should all have plenty, both



<P>for time present and to come:&nbsp; but saving was not my father's



<P>forte.&nbsp; He would not run in debt (at least, my mother took good



<P>care he should not), but while he had money he must spend it:&nbsp;

he



<P>liked to see his house comfortable, and his wife and daughters well



<P>clothed, and well attended; and besides, he was charitably



<P>disposed, and liked to give to the poor, according to his means:



<P>or, as some might think, beyond them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At length, however, a kind friend suggested to him a means of



<P>doubling his private property at one stroke; and further increasing



<P>it, hereafter, to an untold amount.&nbsp; This friend was a merchant,

a



<P>man of enterprising spirit and undoubted talent, who was somewhat



<P>straitened in his mercantile pursuits for want of capital; but



<P>generously proposed to give my father a fair share of his profits,



<P>if he would only entrust him with what he could spare; and he



<P>thought he might safely promise that whatever sum the latter chose



<P>to put into his hands, it should bring him in cent. per cent.&nbsp;

The



<P>small patrimony was speedily sold, and the whole of its price was



<P>deposited in the hands of the friendly merchant; who as promptly



<P>proceeded to ship his cargo, and prepare for his voyage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>My father was delighted, so were we all, with our brightening



<P>prospects.&nbsp; For the present, it is true, we were reduced to the



<P>narrow income of the curacy; but my father seemed to think there



<P>was no necessity for scrupulously restricting our expenditure to



<P>that; so, with a standing bill at Mr. Jackson's, another at



<P>Smith's, and a third at Hobson's, we got along even more



<P>comfortably than before:&nbsp; though my mother affirmed we had better



<P>keep within bounds, for our prospects of wealth were but



<P>precarious, after all; and if my father would only trust everything



<P>to her management, he should never feel himself stinted:&nbsp; but he,



<P>for once, was incorrigible.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>What happy hours Mary and I have passed while sitting at our work



<P>by the fire, or wandering on the heath-clad hills, or idling under



<P>the weeping birch (the only considerable tree in the garden),



<P>talking of future happiness to ourselves and our parents, of what



<P>we would do, and see, and possess; with no firmer foundation for



<P>our goodly superstructure than the riches that were expected to



<P>flow in upon us from the success of the worthy merchant's



<P>speculations.&nbsp; Our father was nearly as bad as ourselves; only

that



<P>he affected not to be so much in earnest:&nbsp; expressing his bright



<P>hopes and sanguine expectations in jests and playful sallies, that



<P>always struck me as being exceedingly witty and pleasant.&nbsp; Our



<P>mother laughed with delight to see him so hopeful and happy:&nbsp; but



<P>still she feared he was setting his heart too much upon the matter;



<P>and once I heard her whisper as she left the room, 'God grant he be



<P>not disappointed!&nbsp; I know not how he would bear it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Disappointed he was; and bitterly, too.&nbsp; It came like a thunder-



<P>clap on us all, that the vessel which contained our fortune had



<P>been wrecked, and gone to the bottom with all its stores, together



<P>with several of the crew, and the unfortunate merchant himself.&nbsp;

I



<P>was grieved for him; I was grieved for the overthrow of all our



<P>air-built castles:&nbsp; but, with the elasticity of youth, I soon



<P>recovered the shook.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Though riches had charms, poverty had no terrors for an



<P>inexperienced girl like me.&nbsp; Indeed, to say the truth, there was



<P>something exhilarating in the idea of being driven to straits, and



<P>thrown upon our own resources.&nbsp; I only wished papa, mamma, and

Mary



<P>were all of the same mind as myself; and then, instead of lamenting



<P>past calamities we might all cheerfully set to work to remedy them;



<P>and the greater the difficulties, the harder our present



<P>privations, the greater should be our cheerfulness to endure the



<P>latter, and our vigour to contend against the former.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mary did not lament, but she brooded continually over the



<P>misfortune, and sank into a state of dejection from which no effort



<P>of mine could rouse her.&nbsp; I could not possibly bring her to regard



<P>the matter on its bright side as I did:&nbsp; and indeed I was so



<P>fearful of being charged with childish frivolity, or stupid



<P>insensibility, that I carefully kept most of my bright ideas and



<P>cheering notions to myself; well knowing they could not be



<P>appreciated.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>My mother thought only of consoling my father, and paying our debts



<P>and retrenching our expenditure by every available means; but my



<P>father was completely overwhelmed by the calamity:&nbsp; health,



<P>strength, and spirits sank beneath the blow, and he never wholly



<P>recovered them.&nbsp; In vain my mother strove to cheer him, by



<P>appealing to his piety, to his courage, to his affection for



<P>herself and us.&nbsp; That very affection was his greatest torment:&nbsp;

it



<P>was for our sakes he had so ardently longed to increase his fortune



<P>- it was our interest that had lent such brightness to his hopes,



<P>and that imparted such bitterness to his present distress.&nbsp; He

now



<P>tormented himself with remorse at having neglected my mother's



<P>advice; which would at least have saved him from the additional



<P>burden of debt - he vainly reproached himself for having brought



<P>her from the dignity, the ease, the luxury of her former station to



<P>toil with him through the cares and toils of poverty.&nbsp; It was gall



<P>and wormwood to his soul to see that splendid, highly-accomplished



<P>woman, once so courted and admired, transformed into an active



<P>managing housewife, with hands and head continually occupied with



<P>household labours and household economy.&nbsp; The very willingness

with



<P>which she performed these duties, the cheerfulness with which she



<P>bore her reverses, and the kindness which withheld her from



<P>imputing the smallest blame to him, were all perverted by this



<P>ingenious self-tormentor into further aggravations of his



<P>sufferings.&nbsp; And thus the mind preyed upon the body, and disordered



<P>the system of the nerves, and they in turn increased the troubles



<P>of the mind, till by action and reaction his health was seriously



<P>impaired; and not one of us could convince him that the aspect of



<P>our affairs was not half so gloomy, so utterly hopeless, as his



<P>morbid imagination represented it to be.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The useful pony phaeton was sold, together with the stout, well-fed



<P>pony - the old favourite that we had fully determined should end



<P>its days in peace, and never pass from our hands; the little coach-



<P>house and stable were let; the servant boy, and the more efficient



<P>(being the more expensive) of the two maid-servants, were



<P>dismissed.&nbsp; Our clothes were mended, turned, and darned to the



<P>utmost verge of decency; our food, always plain, was now simplified



<P>to an unprecedented degree - except my father's favourite dishes;



<P>our coals and candles were painfully economized - the pair of



<P>candles reduced to one, and that most sparingly used; the coals



<P>carefully husbanded in the half-empty grate:&nbsp; especially when my



<P>father was out on his parish duties, or confined to bed through



<P>illness - then we sat with our feet on the fender, scraping the



<P>perishing embers together from time to time, and occasionally



<P>adding a slight scattering of the dust and fragments of coal, just



<P>to keep them alive.&nbsp; As for our carpets, they in time were worn



<P>threadbare, and patched and darned even to a greater extent than



<P>our garments.&nbsp; To save the expense of a gardener, Mary and I



<P>undertook to keep the garden in order; and all the cooking and



<P>household work that could not easily be managed by one servant-



<P>girl, was done by my mother and sister, with a little occasional



<P>help from me:&nbsp; only a little, because, though a woman in my own



<P>estimation, I was still a child in theirs; and my mother, like most



<P>active, managing women, was not gifted with very active daughters:



<P>for this reason - that being so clever and diligent herself, she



<P>was never tempted to trust her affairs to a deputy, but, on the



<P>contrary, was willing to act and think for others as well as for



<P>number one; and whatever was the business in hand, she was apt to



<P>think that no one could do it so well as herself:&nbsp; so that whenever



<P>I offered to assist her, I received such an answer as - 'No, love,



<P>you cannot indeed - there's nothing here you can do.&nbsp; Go and help



<P>your sister, or get her to take a walk with you - tell her she must



<P>not sit so much, and stay so constantly in the house as she does -



<P>she may well look thin and dejected.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Mary, mamma says I'm to help you; or get you to take a walk with



<P>me; she says you may well look thin and dejected, if you sit so



<P>constantly in the house.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Help me you cannot, Agnes; and I cannot go out with YOU - I have



<P>far too much to do.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Then let me help you.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You cannot, indeed, dear child.&nbsp; Go and practise your music, or



<P>play with the kitten.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was always plenty of sewing on hand; but I had not been



<P>taught to cut out a single garment, and except plain hemming and



<P>seaming, there was little I could do, even in that line; for they



<P>both asserted that it was far easier to do the work themselves than



<P>to prepare it for me:&nbsp; and besides, they liked better to see me



<P>prosecuting my studies, or amusing myself - it was time enough for



<P>me to sit bending over my work, like a grave matron, when my



<P>favourite little pussy was become a steady old cat.&nbsp; Under such



<P>circumstances, although I was not many degrees more useful than the



<P>kitten, my idleness was not entirely without excuse.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Through all our troubles, I never but once heard my mother complain



<P>of our want of money.&nbsp; As summer was coming on she observed to

Mary



<P>and me, 'What a desirable thing it would be for your papa to spend



<P>a few weeks at a watering-place.&nbsp; I am convinced the sea-air and



<P>the change of scene would be of incalculable service to him.&nbsp; But



<P>then, you see, there's no money,' she added, with a sigh.&nbsp; We both



<P>wished exceedingly that the thing might be done, and lamented



<P>greatly that it could not.&nbsp; 'Well, well!' said she, 'it's no use



<P>complaining.&nbsp; Possibly something might be done to further the



<P>project after all.&nbsp; Mary, you are a beautiful drawer.&nbsp; What

do you



<P>say to doing a few more pictures in your best style, and getting



<P>them framed, with the water-coloured drawings you have already



<P>done, and trying to dispose of them to some liberal picture-dealer,



<P>who has the sense to discern their merits?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Mamma, I should be delighted if you think they COULD be sold; and



<P>for anything worth while.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It's worth while trying, however, my dear:&nbsp; do you procure the



<P>drawings, and I'll endeavour to find a purchaser.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I wish I could do something,' said I.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You, Agnes! well, who knows?&nbsp; You draw pretty well, too:&nbsp;

if you



<P>choose some simple piece for your subject, I daresay you will be



<P>able to produce something we shall all be proud to exhibit.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But I have another scheme in my head, mamma, and have had long,



<P>only I did not like to mention it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Indeed! pray tell us what it is.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I should like to be a governess.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>My mother uttered an exclamation of surprise, and laughed.&nbsp; My



<P>sister dropped her work in astonishment, exclaiming, 'YOU a



<P>governess, Agnes!&nbsp; What can you be dreaming of?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well!&nbsp; I don't see anything so VERY extraordinary in it.&nbsp;

I do not



<P>pretend to be able to instruct great girls; but surely I could



<P>teach little ones:&nbsp; and I should like it so much:&nbsp; I am so

fond of



<P>children.&nbsp; Do let me, mamma!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But, my love, you have not learned to take care of YOURSELF yet:



<P>and young children require more judgment and experience to manage



<P>than elder ones.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But, mamma, I am above eighteen, and quite able to take care of



<P>myself, and others too.&nbsp; You do not know half the wisdom and



<P>prudence I possess, because I have never been tried.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Only think,' said Mary, 'what would you do in a house full of



<P>strangers, without me or mamma to speak and act for you - with a



<P>parcel of children, besides yourself, to attend to; and no one to



<P>look to for advice?&nbsp; You would not even know what clothes to put



<P>on.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You think, because I always do as you bid me, I have no judgment



<P>of my own:&nbsp; but only try me - that is all I ask - and you shall

see



<P>what I can do.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At that moment my father entered and the subject of our discussion



<P>was explained to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What, my little Agnes a governess!' cried he, and, in spite of his



<P>dejection, he laughed at the idea.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, papa, don't YOU say anything against it:&nbsp; I should like it

so



<P>much; and I am sure I could manage delightfully.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But, my darling, we could not spare you.'&nbsp; And a tear glistened

in



<P>his eye as he added - 'No, no! afflicted as we are, surely we are



<P>not brought to that pass yet.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, no!' said my mother.&nbsp; 'There is no necessity whatever for

such



<P>a step; it is merely a whim of her own.&nbsp; So you must hold your



<P>tongue, you naughty girl; for, though you are so ready to leave us,



<P>you know very well we cannot part with YOU.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I was silenced for that day, and for many succeeding ones; but



<P>still I did not wholly relinquish my darling scheme.&nbsp; Mary got

her



<P>drawing materials, and steadily set to work.&nbsp; I got mine too; but



<P>while I drew, I thought of other things.&nbsp; How delightful it would



<P>be to be a governess!&nbsp; To go out into the world; to enter upon

a



<P>new life; to act for myself; to exercise my unused faculties; to



<P>try my unknown powers; to earn my own maintenance, and something to



<P>comfort and help my father, mother, and sister, besides exonerating



<P>them from the provision of my food and clothing; to show papa what



<P>his little Agnes could do; to convince mamma and Mary that I was



<P>not quite the helpless, thoughtless being they supposed.&nbsp; And then,



<P>how charming to be entrusted with the care and education of



<P>children!&nbsp; Whatever others said, I felt I was fully competent to



<P>the task:&nbsp; the clear remembrance of my own thoughts in early



<P>childhood would be a surer guide than the instructions of the most



<P>mature adviser.&nbsp; I had but to turn from my little pupils to myself



<P>at their age, and I should know, at once, how to win their



<P>confidence and affections:&nbsp; how to waken the contrition of the



<P>erring; how to embolden the timid and console the afflicted; how to



<P>make Virtue practicable, Instruction desirable, and Religion lovely



<P>and comprehensible.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>- Delightful task!



<P>To teach the young idea how to shoot!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To train the tender plants, and watch their buds unfolding day by



<P>day!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Influenced by so many inducements, I determined still to persevere;



<P>though the fear of displeasing my mother, or distressing my



<P>father's feelings, prevented me from resuming the subject for



<P>several days.&nbsp; At length, again, I mentioned it to my mother in



<P>private; and, with some difficulty, got her to promise to assist me



<P>with her endeavours.&nbsp; My father's reluctant consent was next



<P>obtained, and then, though Mary still sighed her disapproval, my



<P>dear, kind mother began to look out for a situation for me.&nbsp; She



<P>wrote to my father's relations, and consulted the newspaper



<P>advertisements - her own relations she had long dropped all



<P>communication with:&nbsp; a formal interchange of occasional letters

was



<P>all she had ever had since her marriage, and she would not at any



<P>time have applied to them in a case of this nature.&nbsp; But so long



<P>and so entire had been my parents' seclusion from the world, that



<P>many weeks elapsed before a suitable situation could be procured.



<P>At last, to my great joy, it was decreed that I should take charge



<P>of the young family of a certain Mrs. Bloomfield; whom my kind,



<P>prim aunt Grey had known in her youth, and asserted to be a very



<P>nice woman.&nbsp; Her husband was a retired tradesman, who had realized



<P>a very comfortable fortune; but could not be prevailed upon to give



<P>a greater salary than twenty-five pounds to the instructress of his



<P>children.&nbsp; I, however, was glad to accept this, rather than refuse



<P>the situation - which my parents were inclined to think the better



<P>plan.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But some weeks more were yet to be devoted to preparation.&nbsp; How



<P>long, how tedious those weeks appeared to me!&nbsp; Yet they were happy



<P>ones in the main - full of bright hopes and ardent expectations.



<P>With what peculiar pleasure I assisted at the making of my new



<P>clothes, and, subsequently, the packing of my trunks!&nbsp; But there



<P>was a feeling of bitterness mingling with the latter occupation



<P>too; and when it was done - when all was ready for my departure on



<P>the morrow, and the last night at home approached - a sudden



<P>anguish seemed to swell my heart.&nbsp; My dear friends looked so sad,



<P>and spoke so very kindly, that I could scarcely keep my eyes from



<P>overflowing:&nbsp; but I still affected to be gay.&nbsp; I had taken

my last



<P>ramble with Mary on the moors, my last walk in the garden, and



<P>round the house; I had fed, with her, our pet pigeons for the last



<P>time - the pretty creatures that we had tamed to peck their food



<P>from our hands:&nbsp; I had given a farewell stroke to all their silky



<P>backs as they crowded in my lap.&nbsp; I had tenderly kissed my own



<P>peculiar favourites, the pair of snow-white fantails; I had played



<P>my last tune on the old familiar piano, and sung my last song to



<P>papa:&nbsp; not the last, I hoped, but the last for what appeared to

me



<P>a very long time.&nbsp; And, perhaps, when I did these things again

it



<P>would be with different feelings:&nbsp; circumstances might be changed,



<P>and this house might never be my settled home again.&nbsp; My dear



<P>little friend, the kitten, would certainly be changed:&nbsp; she was



<P>already growing a fine cat; and when I returned, even for a hasty



<P>visit at Christmas, would, most likely, have forgotten both her



<P>playmate and her merry pranks.&nbsp; I had romped with her for the last



<P>time; and when I stroked her soft bright fur, while she lay purring



<P>herself to sleep in my lap, it was with a feeling of sadness I



<P>could not easily disguise.&nbsp; Then at bed-time, when I retired with



<P>Mary to our quiet little chamber, where already my drawers were



<P>cleared out and my share of the bookcase was empty - and where,



<P>hereafter, she would have to sleep alone, in dreary solitude, as



<P>she expressed it - my heart sank more than ever:&nbsp; I felt as if

I



<P>had been selfish and wrong to persist in leaving her; and when I



<P>knelt once more beside our little bed, I prayed for a blessing on



<P>her and on my parents more fervently than ever I had done before.



<P>To conceal my emotion, I buried my face in my hands, and they were



<P>presently bathed in tears.&nbsp; I perceived, on rising, that she had



<P>been crying too:&nbsp; but neither of us spoke; and in silence we betook



<P>ourselves to our repose, creeping more closely together from the



<P>consciousness that we were to part so soon.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the morning brought a renewal of hope and spirits.&nbsp; I was to



<P>depart early; that the conveyance which took me (a gig, hired from



<P>Mr. Smith, the draper, grocer, and tea-dealer of the village) might



<P>return the same day.&nbsp; I rose, washed, dressed, swallowed a hasty



<P>breakfast, received the fond embraces of my father, mother, and



<P>sister, kissed the cat - to the great scandal of Sally, the maid -



<P>shook hands with her, mounted the gig, drew my veil over my face,



<P>and then, but not till then, burst into a flood of tears.&nbsp; The

gig



<P>rolled on; I looked back; my dear mother and sister were still



<P>standing at the door, looking after me, and waving their adieux.&nbsp;

I



<P>returned their salute, and prayed God to bless them from my heart:



<P>we descended the hill, and I could see them no more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It's a coldish mornin' for you, Miss Agnes,' observed Smith; 'and



<P>a darksome 'un too; but we's happen get to yon spot afore there



<P>come much rain to signify.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, I hope so,' replied I, as calmly as I could.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It's comed a good sup last night too.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But this cold wind will happen keep it off.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Perhaps it will.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here ended our colloquy.&nbsp; We crossed the valley, and began to



<P>ascend the opposite hill.&nbsp; As we were toiling up, I looked back



<P>again; there was the village spire, and the old grey parsonage



<P>beyond it, basking in a slanting beam of sunshine - it was but a



<P>sickly ray, but the village and surrounding hills were all in



<P>sombre shade, and I hailed the wandering beam as a propitious omen



<P>to my home.&nbsp; With clasped hands I fervently implored a blessing

on



<P>its inhabitants, and hastily turned away; for I saw the sunshine



<P>was departing; and I carefully avoided another glance, lest I



<P>should see it in gloomy shadow, like the rest of the landscape.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XV - THE WALK</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>'OH, dear!&nbsp; I wish Hatfield had not been so precipitate!' said



<P>Rosalie next day at four P.M., as, with a portentous yawn, she laid



<P>down her worsted-work and looked listlessly towards the window.



<P>'There's no inducement to go out now; and nothing to look forward



<P>to.&nbsp; The days will be so long and dull when there are no parties

to



<P>enliven them; and there are none this week, or next either, that I



<P>know of.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Pity you were so cross to him,' observed Matilda, to whom this



<P>lamentation was addressed.&nbsp; 'He'll never come again:&nbsp; and

I suspect



<P>you liked him after all.&nbsp; I hoped you would have taken him for

your



<P>beau, and left dear Harry to me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Humph! my beau must be an Adonis indeed, Matilda, the admired of



<P>all beholders, if I am to be contented with him alone.&nbsp; I'm sorry



<P>to lose Hatfield, I confess; but the first decent man, or number of



<P>men, that come to supply his place, will be more than welcome.



<P>It's Sunday to-morrow - I do wonder how he'll look, and whether



<P>he'll be able to go through the service.&nbsp; Most likely he'll pretend



<P>he's got a cold, and make Mr. Weston do it all.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Not he!' exclaimed Matilda, somewhat contemptuously.&nbsp; 'Fool as

he



<P>is, he's not so soft as that comes to.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Her sister was slightly offended; but the event proved Matilda was



<P>right:&nbsp; the disappointed lover performed his pastoral duties as



<P>usual.&nbsp; Rosalie, indeed, affirmed he looked very pale and dejected:



<P>he might be a little paler; but the difference, if any, was



<P>scarcely perceptible.&nbsp; As for his dejection, I certainly did not



<P>hear his laugh ringing from the vestry as usual, nor his voice loud



<P>in hilarious discourse; though I did hear it uplifted in rating the



<P>sexton in a manner that made the congregation stare; and, in his



<P>transits to and from the pulpit and the communion-table, there was



<P>more of solemn pomp, and less of that irreverent, self-confident,



<P>or rather self-delighted imperiousness with which he usually swept



<P>along - that air that seemed to say, 'You all reverence and adore



<P>me, I know; but if anyone does not, I defy him to the teeth!'&nbsp;

But



<P>the most remarkable change was, that he never once suffered his



<P>eyes to wander in the direction of Mr. Murray's pew, and did not



<P>leave the church till we were gone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mr. Hatfield had doubtless received a very severe blow; but his



<P>pride impelled him to use every effort to conceal the effects of



<P>it.&nbsp; He had been disappointed in his certain hope of obtaining

not



<P>only a beautiful, and, to him, highly attractive wife, but one



<P>whose rank and fortune might give brilliance to far inferior



<P>charms:&nbsp; he was likewise, no doubt, intensely mortified by his



<P>repulse, and deeply offended at the conduct of Miss Murray



<P>throughout.&nbsp; It would have given him no little consolation to have



<P>known how disappointed she was to find him apparently so little



<P>moved, and to see that he was able to refrain from casting a single



<P>glance at her throughout both services; though, she declared, it



<P>showed he was thinking of her all the time, or his eyes would have



<P>fallen upon her, if it were only by chance:&nbsp; but if they had so



<P>chanced to fall, she would have affirmed it was because they could



<P>not resist the attraction.&nbsp; It might have pleased him, too, in

some



<P>degree, to have seen how dull and dissatisfied she was throughout



<P>that week (the greater part of it, at least), for lack of her usual



<P>source of excitement; and how often she regretted having 'used him



<P>up so soon,' like a child that, having devoured its plumcake too



<P>hastily, sits sucking its fingers, and vainly lamenting its



<P>greediness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At length I was called upon, one fine morning, to accompany her in



<P>a walk to the village.&nbsp; Ostensibly she went to get some shades

of



<P>Berlin wool, at a tolerably respectable shop that was chiefly



<P>supported by the ladies of the vicinity:&nbsp; really - I trust there

is



<P>no breach of charity in supposing that she went with the idea of



<P>meeting either with the Rector himself, or some other admirer by



<P>the way; for as we went along, she kept wondering 'what Hatfield



<P>would do or say, if we met him,' &amp;c. &amp;c.; as we passed Mr. Green's



<P>park-gates, she 'wondered whether he was at home - great stupid



<P>blockhead'; as Lady Meltham's carriage passed us, she 'wondered



<P>what Mr. Harry was doing this fine day'; and then began to abuse



<P>his elder brother for being 'such a fool as to get married and go



<P>and live in London.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why,' said I, 'I thought you wanted to live in London yourself.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes, because it's so dull here:&nbsp; but then he makes it still duller



<P>by taking himself off:&nbsp; and if he were not married I might have

him



<P>instead of that odious Sir Thomas.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then, observing the prints of a horse's feet on the somewhat miry



<P>road, she 'wondered whether it was a gentleman's horse,' and



<P>finally concluded it was, for the impressions were too small to



<P>have been made by a 'great clumsy cart-horse'; and then she



<P>'wondered who the rider could be,' and whether we should meet him



<P>coming back, for she was sure he had only passed that morning; and



<P>lastly, when we entered the village and saw only a few of its



<P>humble inhabitants moving about, she 'wondered why the stupid



<P>people couldn't keep in their houses; she was sure she didn't want



<P>to see their ugly faces, and dirty, vulgar clothes - it wasn't for



<P>that she came to Horton!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Amid all this, I confess, I wondered, too, in secret, whether we



<P>should meet, or catch a glimpse of somebody else; and as we passed



<P>his lodgings, I even went so far as to wonder whether he was at the



<P>window.&nbsp; On entering the shop, Miss Murray desired me to stand

in



<P>the doorway while she transacted her business, and tell her if



<P>anyone passed.&nbsp; But alas! there was no one visible besides the



<P>villagers, except Jane and Susan Green coming down the single



<P>street, apparently returning from a walk.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Stupid things!' muttered she, as she came out after having



<P>concluded her bargain.&nbsp; 'Why couldn't they have their dolt of a



<P>brother with them? even he would be better than nothing.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She greeted them, however, with a cheerful smile, and protestations



<P>of pleasure at the happy meeting equal to their own.&nbsp; They placed



<P>themselves one on each side of her, and all three walked away



<P>chatting and laughing as young ladies do when they get together, if



<P>they be but on tolerably intimate terms.&nbsp; But I, feeling myself

to



<P>be one too many, left them to their merriment and lagged behind, as



<P>usual on such occasions:&nbsp; I had no relish for walking beside Miss



<P>Green or Miss Susan like one deaf and dumb, who could neither speak



<P>nor be spoken to.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But this time I was not long alone.&nbsp; It struck me, first, as very



<P>odd, that just as I was thinking about Mr. Weston he should come up



<P>and accost me; but afterwards, on due reflection, I thought there



<P>was nothing odd about it, unless it were the fact of his speaking



<P>to me; for on such a morning and so near his own abode, it was



<P>natural enough that he should be about; and as for my thinking of



<P>him, I had been doing that, with little intermission, ever since we



<P>set out on our journey; so there was nothing remarkable in that.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You are alone again, Miss Grey,' said he.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What kind of people are those ladies - the Misses Green?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I really don't know.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'That's strange - when you live so near and see them so often!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, I suppose they are lively, good-tempered girls; but I



<P>imagine you must know them better than I do, yourself, for I never



<P>exchanged a word with either of them.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Indeed?&nbsp; They don't strike me as being particularly reserved.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Very likely they are not so to people of their own class; but they



<P>consider themselves as moving in quite a different sphere from me!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He made no reply to this:&nbsp; but after a short pause, he said, -

'I



<P>suppose it's these things, Miss Grey, that make you think you could



<P>not live without a home?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Not exactly.&nbsp; The fact is I am too socially disposed to be able

to



<P>live contentedly without a friend; and as the only friends I have,



<P>or am likely to have, are at home, if it - or rather, if they were



<P>gone - I will not say I could not live - but I would rather not



<P>live in such a desolate world.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But why do you say the only friends you are likely to have?&nbsp; Are



<P>you so unsociable that you cannot make friends?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, but I never made one yet; and in my present position there is



<P>no possibility of doing so, or even of forming a common



<P>acquaintance.&nbsp; The fault may be partly in myself, but I hope not



<P>altogether.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'The fault is partly in society, and partly, I should think, in



<P>your immediate neighbours:&nbsp; and partly, too, in yourself; for many



<P>ladies, in your position, would make themselves be noticed and



<P>accounted of.&nbsp; But your pupils should be companions for you in

some



<P>degree; they cannot be many years younger than yourself.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, yes, they are good company sometimes; but I cannot call them



<P>friends, nor would they think of bestowing such a name on me - they



<P>have other companions better suited to their tastes.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Perhaps you are too wise for them.&nbsp; How do you amuse yourself

when



<P>alone - do you read much?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Reading is my favourite occupation, when I have leisure for it and



<P>books to read.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From speaking of books in general, he passed to different books in



<P>particular, and proceeded by rapid transitions from topic to topic,



<P>till several matters, both of taste and opinion, had been discussed



<P>considerably within the space of half an hour, but without the



<P>embellishment of many observations from himself; he being evidently



<P>less bent upon communicating his own thoughts and predilections,



<P>than on discovering mine.&nbsp; He had not the tact, or the art, to



<P>effect such a purpose by skilfully drawing out my sentiments or



<P>ideas through the real or apparent statement of his own, or leading



<P>the conversation by imperceptible gradations to such topics as he



<P>wished to advert to:&nbsp; but such gentle abruptness, and such single-



<P>minded straightforwardness, could not possibly offend me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And why should he interest himself at all in my moral and



<P>intellectual capacities:&nbsp; what is it to him what I think or feel?'



<P>I asked myself.&nbsp; And my heart throbbed in answer to the question.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But Jane and Susan Green soon reached their home.&nbsp; As they stood



<P>parleying at the park-gates, attempting to persuade Miss Murray to



<P>come in, I wished Mr. Weston would go, that she might not see him



<P>with me when she turned round; but, unfortunately, his business,



<P>which was to pay one more visit to poor Mark Wood, led him to



<P>pursue the same path as we did, till nearly the close of our



<P>journey.&nbsp; When, however, he saw that Rosalie had taken leave of

her



<P>friends and I was about to join her, he would have left me and



<P>passed on at a quicker pace; but, as he civilly lifted his hat in



<P>passing her, to my surprise, instead of returning the salute with a



<P>stiff, ungracious bow, she accosted him with one of her sweetest



<P>smiles, and, walking by his side, began to talk to him with all



<P>imaginable cheerfulness and affability; and so we proceeded all



<P>three together.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a short pause in the conversation, Mr. Weston made some



<P>remark addressed particularly to me, as referring to something we



<P>had been talking of before; but before I could answer, Miss Murray



<P>replied to the observation and enlarged upon it:&nbsp; he rejoined;

and,



<P>from thence to the close of the interview, she engrossed him



<P>entirely to herself.&nbsp; It might be partly owing to my own stupidity,



<P>my want of tact and assurance:&nbsp; but I felt myself wronged:&nbsp;

I



<P>trembled with apprehension; and I listened with envy to her easy,



<P>rapid flow of utterance, and saw with anxiety the bright smile with



<P>which she looked into his face from time to time:&nbsp; for she was



<P>walking a little in advance, for the purpose (as I judged) of being



<P>seen as well as heard.&nbsp; If her conversation was light and trivial,



<P>it was amusing, and she was never at a loss for something to say,



<P>or for suitable words to express it in.&nbsp; There was nothing pert

or



<P>flippant in her manner now, as when she walked with Mr. Hatfield,



<P>there was only a gentle, playful kind of vivacity, which I thought



<P>must be peculiarly pleasing to a man of Mr. Weston's disposition



<P>and temperament.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When he was gone she began to laugh, and muttered to herself, 'I



<P>thought I could do it!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Do what?' I asked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Fix that man.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What in the world do you mean?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I mean that he will go home and dream of me.&nbsp; I have shot him



<P>through the heart!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'How do you know?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'By many infallible proofs:&nbsp; more especially the look he gave me



<P>when he went away.&nbsp; It was not an impudent look - I exonerate him



<P>from that - it was a look of reverential, tender adoration.&nbsp; Ha,



<P>ha! he's not quite such a stupid blockhead as I thought him!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I made no answer, for my heart was in my throat, or something like



<P>it, and I could not trust myself to speak.&nbsp; 'O God, avert it!'

I



<P>cried, internally - 'for his sake, not for mine!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Miss Murray made several trivial observations as we passed up the



<P>park, to which (in spite of my reluctance to let one glimpse of my



<P>feelings appear) I could only answer by monosyllables.&nbsp; Whether

she



<P>intended to torment me, or merely to amuse herself, I could not



<P>tell - and did not much care; but I thought of the poor man and his



<P>one lamb, and the rich man with his thousand flocks; and I dreaded



<P>I knew not what for Mr. Weston, independently of my own blighted



<P>hopes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Right glad was I to get into the house, and find myself alone once



<P>more in my own room.&nbsp; My first impulse was to sink into the chair



<P>beside the bed; and laying my head on the pillow, to seek relief in



<P>a passionate burst of tears:&nbsp; there was an imperative craving for



<P>such an indulgence; but, alas! I must restrain and swallow back my



<P>feelings still:&nbsp; there was the bell - the odious bell for the



<P>schoolroom dinner; and I must go down with a calm face, and smile,



<P>and laugh, and talk nonsense - yes, and eat, too, if possible, as



<P>if all was right, and I was just returned from a pleasant walk.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">
