<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XIV - THE RECTOR</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE following day was as fine as the preceding one.&nbsp; Soon after



<P>breakfast Miss Matilda, having galloped and blundered through a few



<P>unprofitable lessons, and vengeably thumped the piano for an hour,



<P>in a terrible humour with both me and it, because her mamma would



<P>not give her a holiday, had betaken herself to her favourite places



<P>of resort, the yards, the stables, and the dog-kennels; and Miss



<P>Murray was gone forth to enjoy a quiet ramble with a new



<P>fashionable novel for her companion, leaving me in the schoolroom



<P>hard at work upon a water-colour drawing which I had promised to do



<P>for her, and which she insisted upon my finishing that day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At my feet lay a little rough terrier.&nbsp; It was the property of

Miss



<P>Matilda; but she hated the animal, and intended to sell it,



<P>alleging that it was quite spoiled.&nbsp; It was really an excellent

dog



<P>of its kind; but she affirmed it was fit for nothing, and had not



<P>even the sense to know its own mistress.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The fact was she had purchased it when but a small puppy, insisting



<P>at first that no one should touch it but herself; but soon becoming



<P>tired of so helpless and troublesome a nursling, she had gladly



<P>yielded to my entreaties to be allowed to take charge of it; and I,



<P>by carefully nursing the little creature from infancy to



<P>adolescence, of course, had obtained its affections:&nbsp; a reward

I



<P>should have greatly valued, and looked upon as far outweighing all



<P>the trouble I had had with it, had not poor Snap's grateful



<P>feelings exposed him to many a harsh word and many a spiteful kick



<P>and pinch from his owner, and were he not now in danger of being



<P>'put away' in consequence, or transferred to some rough, stony-



<P>hearted master.&nbsp; But how could I help it?&nbsp; I could not make

the dog



<P>hate me by cruel treatment, and she would not propitiate him by



<P>kindness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>However, while I thus sat, working away with my pencil, Mrs. Murray



<P>came, half-sailing, half-bustling, into the room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Miss Grey,' she began, - 'dear! how can you sit at your drawing



<P>such a day as this?'&nbsp; (She thought I was doing it for my own



<P>pleasure.)&nbsp; 'I WONDER you don't put on your bonnet and go out with



<P>the young ladies.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I think, ma'am, Miss Murray is reading; and Miss Matilda is



<P>amusing herself with her dogs.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'If you would try to amuse Miss Matilda yourself a little more, I



<P>think she would not be driven to seek amusement in the



<P>companionship of dogs and horses and grooms, so much as she is; and



<P>if you would be a little more cheerful and conversable with Miss



<P>Murray, she would not so often go wandering in the fields with a



<P>book in her hand.&nbsp; However, I don't want to vex you,' added she,



<P>seeing, I suppose, that my cheeks burned and my hand trembled with



<P>some unamiable emotion.&nbsp; 'Do, pray, try not to be so touchy -



<P>there's no speaking to you else.&nbsp; And tell me if you know where



<P>Rosalie is gone:&nbsp; and why she likes to be so much alone?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'She says she likes to be alone when she has a new book to read.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But why can't she read it in the park or the garden? - why should



<P>she go into the fields and lanes?&nbsp; And how is it that that Mr.



<P>Hatfield so often finds her out?&nbsp; She told me last week he'd walked



<P>his horse by her side all up Moss Lane; and now I'm sure it was he



<P>I saw, from my dressing-room window, walking so briskly past the



<P>park-gates, and on towards the field where she so frequently goes.



<P>I wish you would go and see if she is there; and just gently remind



<P>her that it is not proper for a young lady of her rank and



<P>prospects to be wandering about by herself in that manner, exposed



<P>to the attentions of anyone that presumes to address her; like some



<P>poor neglected girl that has no park to walk in, and no friends to



<P>take care of her:&nbsp; and tell her that her papa would be extremely



<P>angry if he knew of her treating Mr. Hatfield in the familiar



<P>manner that I fear she does; and - oh! if you - if ANY governess



<P>had but half a mother's watchfulness - half a mother's anxious



<P>care, I should be saved this trouble; and you would see at once the



<P>necessity of keeping your eye upon her, and making your company



<P>agreeable to -&nbsp; Well, go - go; there's no time to be lost,' cried



<P>she, seeing that I had put away my drawing materials, and was



<P>waiting in the doorway for the conclusion of her address.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>According to her prognostications, I found Miss Murray in her



<P>favourite field just without the park; and, unfortunately, not



<P>alone; for the tall, stately figure of Mr. Hatfield was slowly



<P>sauntering by her side.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here was a poser for me.&nbsp; It was my duty to interrupt the TETE-A-



<P>TETE:&nbsp; but how was it to be done?&nbsp; Mr. Hatfield could not

to be



<P>driven away by so insignificant person as I; and to go and place



<P>myself on the other side of Miss Murray, and intrude my unwelcome



<P>presence upon her without noticing her companion, was a piece of



<P>rudeness I could not be guilty of:&nbsp; neither had I the courage to



<P>cry aloud from the top of the field that she was wanted elsewhere.



<P>So I took the intermediate course of walking slowly but steadily



<P>towards them; resolving, if my approach failed to scare away the



<P>beau, to pass by and tell Miss Murray her mamma wanted her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She certainly looked very charming as she strolled, lingering along



<P>under the budding horse-chestnut trees that stretched their long



<P>arms over the park-palings; with her closed book in one hand, and



<P>in the other a graceful sprig of myrtle, which served her as a very



<P>pretty plaything; her bright ringlets escaping profusely from her



<P>little bonnet, and gently stirred by the breeze, her fair cheek



<P>flushed with gratified vanity, her smiling blue eyes, now slyly



<P>glancing towards her admirer, now gazing downward at her myrtle



<P>sprig.&nbsp; But Snap, running before me, interrupted her in the midst



<P>of some half-pert, half-playful repartee, by catching hold of her



<P>dress and vehemently tugging thereat; till Mr. Hatfield, with his



<P>cane, administered a resounding thwack upon the animal's skull, and



<P>sent it yelping back to me with a clamorous outcry that afforded



<P>the reverend gentleman great amusement:&nbsp; but seeing me so near,

he



<P>thought, I suppose, he might as well be taking his departure; and,



<P>as I stooped to caress the dog, with ostentatious pity to show my



<P>disapproval of his severity, I heard him say:&nbsp; 'When shall I see



<P>you again, Miss Murray?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'At church, I suppose,' replied she, 'unless your business chances



<P>to bring you here again at the precise moment when I happen to be



<P>walking by.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I could always manage to have business here, if I knew precisely



<P>when and where to find you.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But if I would, I could not inform you, for I am so immethodical,



<P>I never can tell to-day what I shall do tomorrow.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Then give me that, meantime, to comfort me,' said he, half



<P>jestingly and half in earnest, extending his hand for the sprig of



<P>myrtle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, indeed, I shan't.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Do! PRAY do!&nbsp; I shall be the most miserable of men if you don't.



<P>You cannot be so cruel as to deny me a favour so easily granted and



<P>yet so highly prized!' pleaded he as ardently as if his life



<P>depended on it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>By this time I stood within a very few yards of them, impatiently



<P>waiting his departure.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'There then! take it and go,' said Rosalie.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He joyfully received the gift, murmured something that made her



<P>blush and toss her head, but with a little laugh that showed her



<P>displeasure was entirely affected; and then with a courteous



<P>salutation withdrew.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Did you ever see such a man, Miss Grey?' said she, turning to me;



<P>'I'm so GLAD you came!&nbsp; I thought I never SHOULD, get rid of him;



<P>and I was so terribly afraid of papa seeing him.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Has he been with you long?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, not long, but he's so extremely impertinent:&nbsp; and he's always



<P>hanging about, pretending his business or his clerical duties



<P>require his attendance in these parts, and really watching for poor



<P>me, and pouncing upon me wherever he sees me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, your mamma thinks you ought not to go beyond the park or



<P>garden without some discreet, matronly person like me to accompany



<P>you, and keep off all intruders.&nbsp; She descried Mr. Hatfield



<P>hurrying past the park-gates, and forthwith despatched me with



<P>instructions to seek you up and to take care of you, and likewise



<P>to warn - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, mamma's so tiresome!&nbsp; As if I couldn't take care of myself.



<P>She bothered me before about Mr. Hatfield; and I told her she might



<P>trust me:&nbsp; I never should forget my rank and station for the most



<P>delightful man that ever breathed.&nbsp; I wish he would go down on

his



<P>knees to-morrow, and implore me to be his wife, that I might just



<P>show her how mistaken she is in supposing that I could ever - Oh,



<P>it provokes me so!&nbsp; To think that I could be such a fool as to

fall



<P>in LOVE!&nbsp; It is quite beneath the dignity of a woman to do such

a



<P>thing.&nbsp; Love!&nbsp; I detest the word!&nbsp; As applied to one

of our sex, I



<P>think it a perfect insult.&nbsp; A preference I MIGHT acknowledge; but



<P>never for one like poor Mr. Hatfield, who has not seven hundred a



<P>year to bless himself with.&nbsp; I like to talk to him, because he's

so



<P>clever and amusing - I wish Sir Thomas Ashby were half as nice;



<P>besides, I must have SOMEBODY to flirt with, and no one else has



<P>the sense to come here; and when we go out, mamma won't let me



<P>flirt with anybody but Sir Thomas - if he's there; and if he's NOT



<P>there, I'm bound hand and foot, for fear somebody should go and



<P>make up some exaggerated story, and put it into his head that I'm



<P>engaged, or likely to be engaged, to somebody else; or, what is



<P>more probable, for fear his nasty old mother should see or hear of



<P>my ongoings, and conclude that I'm not a fit wife for her excellent



<P>son:&nbsp; as if the said son were not the greatest scamp in



<P>Christendom; and as if any woman of common decency were not a world



<P>too good for him.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Is it really so, Miss Murray? and does your mamma know it, and yet



<P>wish you to marry him?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'To be sure, she does!&nbsp; She knows more against him than I do, I



<P>believe:&nbsp; she keeps it from me lest I should be discouraged; not



<P>knowing how little I care about such things.&nbsp; For it's no great



<P>matter, really:&nbsp; he'll be all right when he's married, as mamma



<P>says; and reformed rakes make the best husbands, EVERYBODY knows.



<P>I only wish he were not so ugly - THAT'S all I think about:&nbsp; but



<P>then there's no choice here in the country; and papa WILL NOT let



<P>us go to London - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But I should think Mr. Hatfield would be far better.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And so he would, if he were lord of Ashby Park - there's not a



<P>doubt of it:&nbsp; but the fact is, I MUST have Ashby Park, whoever



<P>shares it with me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But Mr. Hatfield thinks you like him all this time; you don't



<P>consider how bitterly he will be disappointed when he finds himself



<P>mistaken.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'NO, indeed!&nbsp; It will be a proper punishment for his presumption

-



<P>for ever DARING to think I could like him.&nbsp; I should enjoy nothing



<P>so much as lifting the veil from his eyes.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'The sooner you do it the better then.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No; I tell you, I like to amuse myself with him.&nbsp; Besides, he



<P>doesn't really think I like him.&nbsp; I take good care of that:&nbsp;

you



<P>don't know how cleverly I manage.&nbsp; He may presume to think he can



<P>induce me to like him; for which I shall punish him as he



<P>deserves.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, mind you don't give too much reason for such presumption -



<P>that's all,' replied I.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But all my exhortations were in vain:&nbsp; they only made her somewhat



<P>more solicitous to disguise her wishes and her thoughts from me.



<P>She talked no more to me about the Rector; but I could see that her



<P>mind, if not her heart, was fixed upon him still, and that she was



<P>intent upon obtaining another interview:&nbsp; for though, in compliance



<P>with her mother's request, I was now constituted the companion of



<P>her rambles for a time, she still persisted in wandering in the



<P>fields and lanes that lay in the nearest proximity to the road;



<P>and, whether she talked to me or read the book she carried in her



<P>hand, she kept continually pausing to look round her, or gaze up



<P>the road to see if anyone was coming; and if a horseman trotted by,



<P>I could tell by her unqualified abuse of the poor equestrian,



<P>whoever he might be, that she hated him BECAUSE he was not Mr.



<P>Hatfield.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Surely,' thought I, 'she is not so indifferent to him as she



<P>believes herself to be, or would have others to believe her; and



<P>her mother's anxiety is not so wholly causeless as she affirms.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Three days passed away, and he did not make his appearance.&nbsp; On

the



<P>afternoon of the fourth, as we were walking beside the park-palings



<P>in the memorable field, each furnished with a book (for I always



<P>took care to provide myself with something to be doing when she did



<P>not require me to talk), she suddenly interrupted my studies by



<P>exclaiming -

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, Miss Grey! do be so kind as to go and see Mark Wood, and take



<P>his wife half-a-crown from me - I should have given or sent it a



<P>week ago, but quite forgot.&nbsp; There!' said she, throwing me her



<P>purse, and speaking very fast - 'Never mind getting it out now, but



<P>take the purse and give them what you like; I would go with you,



<P>but I want to finish this volume.&nbsp; I'll come and meet you when

I've



<P>done it.&nbsp; Be quick, will you - and - oh, wait; hadn't you better



<P>read to him a bit?&nbsp; Run to the house and get some sort of a good



<P>book.&nbsp; Anything will do.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I did as I was desired; but, suspecting something from her hurried



<P>manner and the suddenness of the request, I just glanced back



<P>before I quitted the field, and there was Mr. Hatfield about to



<P>enter at the gate below.&nbsp; By sending me to the house for a book,



<P>she had just prevented my meeting him on the road.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Never mind!' thought I, 'there'll be no great harm done.&nbsp; Poor



<P>Mark will be glad of the half-crown, and perhaps of the good book



<P>too; and if the Rector does steal Miss Rosalie's heart, it will



<P>only humble her pride a little; and if they do get married at last,



<P>it will only save her from a worse fate; and she will be quite a



<P>good enough partner for him, and he for her.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Mark Wood was the consumptive labourer whom I mentioned before.&nbsp;

He



<P>was now rapidly wearing away.&nbsp; Miss Murray, by her liberality,



<P>obtained literally the blessing of him that was ready to perish;



<P>for though the half-crown could be of very little service to him,



<P>he was glad of it for the sake of his wife and children, so soon to



<P>be widowed and fatherless.&nbsp; After I had sat a few minutes, and

read



<P>a little for the comfort and edification of himself and his



<P>afflicted wife, I left them; but I had not proceeded fifty yards



<P>before I encountered Mr. Weston, apparently on his way to the same



<P>abode.&nbsp; He greeted me in his usual quiet, unaffected way, stopped



<P>to inquire about the condition of the sick man and his family, and



<P>with a sort of unconscious, brotherly disregard to ceremony took



<P>from my hand the book out of which I had been reading, turned over



<P>its pages, made a few brief but very sensible remarks, and restored



<P>it; then told me about some poor sufferer he had just been



<P>visiting, talked a little about Nancy Brown, made a few



<P>observations upon my little rough friend the terrier, that was



<P>frisking at his feet, and finally upon the beauty of the weather,



<P>and departed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I have omitted to give a detail of his words, from a notion that



<P>they would not interest the reader as they did me, and not because



<P>I have forgotten them.&nbsp; No; I remember them well; for I thought



<P>them over and over again in the course of that day and many



<P>succeeding ones, I know not how often; and recalled every



<P>intonation of his deep, clear voice, every flash of his quick,



<P>brown eye, and every gleam of his pleasant, but too transient



<P>smile.&nbsp; Such a confession will look very absurd, I fear:&nbsp;

but no



<P>matter:&nbsp; I have written it:&nbsp; and they that read it will not

know



<P>the writer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>While I was walking along, happy within, and pleased with all



<P>around, Miss Murray came hastening to meet me; her buoyant step,



<P>flushed cheek, and radiant smiles showing that she, too, was happy,



<P>in her own way.&nbsp; Running up to me, she put her arm through mine,



<P>and without waiting to recover breath, began - 'Now, Miss Grey,



<P>think yourself highly honoured, for I'm come to tell you my news



<P>before I've breathed a word of it to anyone else.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, what is it?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, SUCH news!&nbsp; In the first place, you must know that Mr.



<P>Hatfield came upon me just after you were gone.&nbsp; I was in such

a



<P>way for fear papa or mamma should see him; but you know I couldn't



<P>call you back again, and so! - oh, dear!&nbsp; I can't tell you all



<P>about it now, for there's Matilda, I see, in the park, and I must



<P>go and open my budget to her.&nbsp; But, however, Hatfield was most



<P>uncommonly audacious, unspeakably complimentary, and



<P>unprecedentedly tender - tried to be so, at least - he didn't



<P>succeed very well in THAT, because it's not his vein.&nbsp; I'll tell



<P>you all he said another time.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But what did YOU say - I'm more interested in that?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I'll tell you that, too, at some future period.&nbsp; I happened to

be



<P>in a very good humour just then; but, though I was complaisant and



<P>gracious enough, I took care not to compromise myself in any



<P>possible way.&nbsp; But, however, the conceited wretch chose to



<P>interpret my amiability of temper his own way, and at length



<P>presumed upon my indulgence so far - what do you think? - he



<P>actually made me an offer!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And you - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I proudly drew myself up, and with the greatest coolness expressed



<P>my astonishment at such an occurrence, and hoped he had seen



<P>nothing in my conduct to justify his expectations.&nbsp; You should

have



<P>SEEN how his countenance fell!&nbsp; He went perfectly white in the



<P>face.&nbsp; I assured him that I esteemed him and all that, but could



<P>not possibly accede to his proposals; and if I did, papa and mamma



<P>could never be brought to give their consent.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"But if they could," said he, "would yours be wanting?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Certainly, Mr. Hatfield," I replied, with a cool decision which



<P>quelled all hope at once.&nbsp; Oh, if you had seen how dreadfully



<P>mortified he was - how crushed to the earth by his disappointment!



<P>really, I almost pitied him myself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'One more desperate attempt, however, he made.&nbsp; After a silence

of



<P>considerable duration, during which he struggled to be calm, and I



<P>to be grave - for I felt a strong propensity to laugh - which would



<P>have ruined all - he said, with the ghost of a smile - "But tell me



<P>plainly, Miss Murray, if I had the wealth of Sir Hugh Meltham, or



<P>the prospects of his eldest son, would you still refuse me?&nbsp; Answer



<P>me truly, upon your honour."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Certainly," said I.&nbsp; "That would make no difference whatever."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It was a great lie, but he looked so confident in his own



<P>attractions still, that I determined not to leave him one stone



<P>upon another.&nbsp; He looked me full in the face; but I kept my



<P>countenance so well that he could not imagine I was saying anything



<P>more than the actual truth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Then it's all over, I suppose," he said, looking as if he could



<P>have died on the spot with vexation and the intensity of his



<P>despair.&nbsp; But he was angry as well as disappointed.&nbsp; There

was he,



<P>suffering so unspeakably, and there was I, the pitiless cause of it



<P>all, so utterly impenetrable to all the artillery of his looks and



<P>words, so calmly cold and proud, he could not but feel some



<P>resentment; and with singular bitterness he began - "I certainly



<P>did not expect this, Miss Murray.&nbsp; I might say something about

your



<P>past conduct, and the hopes you have led me to foster, but I



<P>forbear, on condition - "

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"No conditions, Mr. Hatfield!" said I, now truly indignant at his



<P>insolence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Then let me beg it as a favour," he replied, lowering his voice



<P>at once, and taking a humbler tone:&nbsp; "let me entreat that you will



<P>not mention this affair to anyone whatever.&nbsp; If you will keep



<P>silence about it, there need be no unpleasantness on either side -



<P>nothing, I mean, beyond what is quite unavoidable:&nbsp; for my own



<P>feelings I will endeavour to keep to myself, if I cannot annihilate



<P>them - I will try to forgive, if I cannot forget the cause of my



<P>sufferings.&nbsp; I will not suppose, Miss Murray, that you know how



<P>deeply you have injured me.&nbsp; I would not have you aware of it;

but



<P>if, in addition to the injury you have already done me - pardon me,



<P>but, whether innocently or not, you HAVE done it - and if you add



<P>to it by giving publicity to this unfortunate affair, or naming it



<P>AT ALL, you will find that I too can speak, and though you scorned



<P>my love, you will hardly scorn my - "

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'He stopped, but he bit his bloodless lip, and looked so terribly



<P>fierce that I was quite frightened.&nbsp; However, my pride upheld me



<P>still, and I answered disdainfully; "I do not know what motive you



<P>suppose I could have for naming it to anyone, Mr. Hatfield; but if



<P>I were disposed to do so, you would not deter me by threats; and it



<P>is scarcely the part of a gentleman to attempt it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Pardon me, Miss Murray," said he, "I have loved you so intensely



<P>- I do still adore you so deeply, that I would not willingly offend



<P>you; but though I never have loved, and never CAN love any woman as



<P>I have loved you, it is equally certain that I never was so ill-



<P>treated by any.&nbsp; On the contrary, I have always found your sex

the



<P>kindest and most tender and obliging of God's creation, till now."



<P>(Think of the conceited fellow saying that!)&nbsp; "And the novelty

and



<P>harshness of the lesson you have taught me to-day, and the



<P>bitterness of being disappointed in the only quarter on which the



<P>happiness of my life depended, must excuse any appearance of



<P>asperity.&nbsp; If my presence is disagreeable to you, Miss Murray,"

he



<P>said (for I was looking about me to show how little I cared for



<P>him, so he thought I was tired of him, I suppose) - "if my presence



<P>is disagreeable to you, Miss Murray, you have only to promise me



<P>the favour I named, and I will relieve you at once.&nbsp; There are

many



<P>ladies - some even in this parish - who would be delighted to



<P>accept what you have so scornfully trampled under your feet.&nbsp; They



<P>would be naturally inclined to hate one whose surpassing loveliness



<P>has so completely estranged my heart from them and blinded me to



<P>their attractions; and a single hint of the truth from me to one of



<P>these would be sufficient to raise such a talk against you as would



<P>seriously injure your prospects, and diminish your chance of



<P>success with any other gentleman you or your mamma might design to



<P>entangle."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"What do your mean, sir?" said I, ready to stamp with passion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"I mean that this affair from beginning to end appears to me like



<P>a case of arrant flirtation, to say the least of it - such a case



<P>as you would find it rather inconvenient to have blazoned through



<P>the world:&nbsp; especially with the additions and exaggerations of

your



<P>female rivals, who would be too glad to publish the matter, if I



<P>only gave them a handle to it.&nbsp; But I promise you, on the faith

of



<P>a gentleman, that no word or syllable that could tend to your



<P>prejudice shall ever escape my lips, provided you will - "

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Well, well, I won't mention it," said I.&nbsp; "You may rely upon

my



<P>silence, if that can afford you any consolation."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"You promise it?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Yes," I answered; for I wanted to get rid of him now.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'"Farewell, then!" said he, in a most doleful, heart-sick tone; and



<P>with a look where pride vainly struggled against despair, he turned



<P>and went away:&nbsp; longing, no doubt, to get home, that he might shut



<P>himself up in his study and cry - if he doesn't burst into tears



<P>before he gets there.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But you have broken your promise already,' said I, truly horrified



<P>at her perfidy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh! it's only to you; I know you won't repeat it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Certainly, I shall not:&nbsp; but you say you are going to tell your



<P>sister; and she will tell your brothers when they come home, and



<P>Brown immediately, if you do not tell her yourself; and Brown will



<P>blazon it, or be the means of blazoning it, throughout the



<P>country.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, indeed, she won't.&nbsp; We shall not tell her at all, unless it

be



<P>under the promise of the strictest secrecy.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But how can you expect her to keep her promises better than her



<P>more enlightened mistress?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, well, she shan't hear it then,' said Miss Murray, somewhat



<P>snappishly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But you will tell your mamma, of course,' pursued I; 'and she will



<P>tell your papa.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Of course I shall tell mamma - that is the very thing that pleases



<P>me so much.&nbsp; I shall now be able to convince her how mistaken she



<P>was in her fears about me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, THAT'S it, is it?&nbsp; I was wondering what it was that delighted



<P>you so much.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes; and another thing is, that I've humbled Mr. Hatfield so



<P>charmingly; and another - why, you must allow me some share of



<P>female vanity:&nbsp; I don't pretend to be without that most essential



<P>attribute of our sex - and if you had seen poor Hatfield's intense



<P>eagerness in making his ardent declaration and his flattering



<P>proposal, and his agony of mind, that no effort of pride could



<P>conceal, on being refused, you would have allowed I had some cause



<P>to be gratified.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'The greater his agony, I should think, the less your cause for



<P>gratification.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, nonsense!' cried the young lady, shaking herself with



<P>vexation.&nbsp; 'You either can't understand me, or you won't.&nbsp;

If I had



<P>not confidence in your magnanimity, I should think you envied me.



<P>But you will, perhaps, comprehend this cause of pleasure - which is



<P>as great as any - namely, that I am delighted with myself for my



<P>prudence, my self-command, my heartlessness, if you please.&nbsp; I

was



<P>not a bit taken by surprise, not a bit confused, or awkward, or



<P>foolish; I just acted and spoke as I ought to have done, and was



<P>completely my own mistress throughout.&nbsp; And here was a man,



<P>decidedly good-looking - Jane and Susan Green call him bewitchingly



<P>handsome I suppose they're two of the ladies he pretends would be



<P>so glad to have him; but, however, he was certainly a very clever,



<P>witty, agreeable companion - not what you call clever, but just



<P>enough to make him entertaining; and a man one needn't be ashamed



<P>of anywhere, and would not soon grow tired of; and to confess the



<P>truth, I rather liked him - better even, of late, than Harry



<P>Meltham - and he evidently idolised me; and yet, though he came



<P>upon me all alone and unprepared, I had the wisdom, and the pride,



<P>and the strength to refuse him - and so scornfully and coolly as I



<P>did:&nbsp; I have good reason to be proud of that.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And are you equally proud of having told him that his having the



<P>wealth of Sir Hugh Meltham would make no difference to you, when



<P>that was not the case; and of having promised to tell no one of his



<P>misadventure, apparently without the slightest intention of keeping



<P>your promise?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Of course! what else could I do?&nbsp; You would not have had me -

but



<P>I see, Miss Grey, you're not in a good temper.&nbsp; Here's Matilda;



<P>I'll see what she and mamma have to say about it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She left me, offended at my want of sympathy, and thinking, no



<P>doubt, that I envied her.&nbsp; I did not - at least, I firmly believed



<P>I did not.&nbsp; I was sorry for her; I was amazed, disgusted at her



<P>heartless vanity; I wondered why so much beauty should be given to



<P>those who made so bad a use of it, and denied to some who would



<P>make it a benefit to both themselves and others.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But, God knows best, I concluded.&nbsp; There are, I suppose, some men



<P>as vain, as selfish, and as heartless as she is, and, perhaps, such



<P>women may be useful to punish them.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XVI - THE SUBSTITUTION</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>NEXT Sunday was one of the gloomiest of April days - a day of



<P>thick, dark clouds, and heavy showers.&nbsp; None of the Murrays were



<P>disposed to attend church in the afternoon, excepting Rosalie:&nbsp;

she



<P>was bent upon going as usual; so she ordered the carriage, and I



<P>went with her:&nbsp; nothing loth, of course, for at church I might

look



<P>without fear of scorn or censure upon a form and face more pleasing



<P>to me than the most beautiful of God's creations; I might listen



<P>without disturbance to a voice more charming than the sweetest



<P>music to my ears; I might seem to hold communion with that soul in



<P>which I felt so deeply interested, and imbibe its purest thoughts



<P>and holiest aspirations, with no alloy to such felicity except the



<P>secret reproaches of my conscience, which would too often whisper



<P>that I was deceiving my own self, and mocking God with the service



<P>of a heart more bent upon the creature than the Creator.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sometimes, such thoughts would give me trouble enough; but



<P>sometimes I could quiet them with thinking - it is not the man, it



<P>is his goodness that I love.&nbsp; 'Whatsoever things are pure,



<P>whatsoever things are lovely, whatsoever things are honest and of



<P>good report, think on these things.'&nbsp; We do well to worship God

in



<P>His works; and I know none of them in which so many of His



<P>attributes - so much of His own spirit shines, as in this His



<P>faithful servant; whom to know and not to appreciate, were obtuse



<P>insensibility in me, who have so little else to occupy my heart.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Almost immediately after the conclusion of the service, Miss Murray



<P>left the church.&nbsp; We had to stand in the porch, for it was raining,



<P>and the carriage was not yet come.&nbsp; I wondered at her coming forth



<P>so hastily, for neither young Meltham nor Squire Green was there;



<P>but I soon found it was to secure an interview with Mr. Weston as



<P>he came out, which he presently did.&nbsp; Having saluted us both, he



<P>would have passed on, but she detained him; first with observations



<P>upon the disagreeable weather, and then with asking if he would be



<P>so kind as to come some time to-morrow to see the granddaughter of



<P>the old woman who kept the porter's lodge, for the girl was ill of



<P>a fever, and wished to see him.&nbsp; He promised to do so.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And at what time will you be most likely to come, Mr. Weston?&nbsp;

The



<P>old woman will like to know when to expect you - you know such



<P>people think more about having their cottages in order when decent



<P>people come to see them than we are apt to suppose.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Here was a wonderful instance of consideration from the thoughtless



<P>Miss Murray.&nbsp; Mr. Weston named an hour in the morning at which

he



<P>would endeavour, to be there.&nbsp; By this time the carriage was ready,



<P>and the footman was waiting, with an open umbrella, to escort Miss



<P>Murray through the churchyard.&nbsp; I was about to follow; but Mr.



<P>Weston had an umbrella too, and offered me the benefit of its



<P>shelter, for it was raining heavily.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, thank you, I don't mind the rain,' I said.&nbsp; I always lacked



<P>common sense when taken by surprise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But you don't LIKE it, I suppose? - an umbrella will do you no



<P>harm at any rate,' he replied, with a smile that showed he was not



<P>offended; as a man of worse temper or less penetration would have



<P>been at such a refusal of his aid.&nbsp; I could not deny the truth

of



<P>his assertion, and so went with him to the carriage; he even



<P>offered me his hand on getting in:&nbsp; an unnecessary piece of



<P>civility, but I accepted that too, for fear of giving offence.&nbsp;

One



<P>glance he gave, one little smile at parting - it was but for a



<P>moment; but therein I read, or thought I read, a meaning that



<P>kindled in my heart a brighter flame of hope than had ever yet



<P>arisen.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I would have sent the footman back for you, Miss Grey, if you'd



<P>waited a moment - you needn't have taken Mr. Weston's umbrella,'



<P>observed Rosalie, with a very unamiable cloud upon her pretty face.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I would have come without an umbrella, but Mr. Weston offered me



<P>the benefit of his, and I could not have refused it more than I did



<P>without offending him,' replied I, smiling placidly; for my inward



<P>happiness made that amusing, which would have wounded me at another



<P>time.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The carriage was now in motion.&nbsp; Miss Murray bent forwards, and



<P>looked out of the window as we were passing Mr. Weston.&nbsp; He was



<P>pacing homewards along the causeway, and did not turn his head.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Stupid ass!' cried she, throwing herself back again in the seat.



<P>'You don't know what you've lost by not looking this way!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What has he lost?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'A bow from me, that would have raised him to the seventh heaven!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I made no answer.&nbsp; I saw she was out of humour, and I derived a



<P>secret gratification from the fact, not that she was vexed, but



<P>that she thought she had reason to be so.&nbsp; It made me think my



<P>hopes were not entirely the offspring of my wishes and imagination.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I mean to take up Mr. Weston instead of Mr. Hatfield,' said my



<P>companion, after a short pause, resuming something of her usual



<P>cheerfulness.&nbsp; 'The ball at Ashby Park takes place on Tuesday,

you



<P>know; and mamma thinks it very likely that Sir Thomas will propose



<P>to me then:&nbsp; such things are often done in the privacy of the ball-



<P>room, when gentlemen are most easily ensnared, and ladies most



<P>enchanting.&nbsp; But if I am to be married so soon, I must make the



<P>best of the present time:&nbsp; I am determined Hatfield shall not be



<P>the only man who shall lay his heart at my feet, and implore me to



<P>accept the worthless gift in vain.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'If you mean Mr. Weston to be one of your victims,' said I, with



<P>affected indifference, 'you will have to make such overtures



<P>yourself that you will find it difficult to draw back when he asks



<P>you to fulfil the expectations you have raised.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I don't suppose he will ask me to marry him, nor should I desire



<P>it:&nbsp; that would be rather too much presumption! but I intend him

to



<P>feel my power.&nbsp; He has felt it already, indeed:&nbsp; but he shall



<P>ACKNOWLEDGE it too; and what visionary hopes he may have, he must



<P>keep to himself, and only amuse me with the result of them - for a



<P>time.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh! that some kind spirit would whisper those words in his ear,' I



<P>inwardly exclaimed.&nbsp; I was far too indignant to hazard a reply

to



<P>her observation aloud; and nothing more was said about Mr. Weston



<P>that day, by me or in my hearing.&nbsp; But next morning, soon after



<P>breakfast, Miss Murray came into the schoolroom, where her sister



<P>was employed at her studies, or rather her lessons, for studies



<P>they were not, and said, 'Matilda, I want you to take a walk with



<P>me about eleven o'clock.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, I can't, Rosalie!&nbsp; I have to give orders about my new bridle



<P>and saddle-cloth, and speak to the rat-catcher about his dogs:



<P>Miss Grey must go with you.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, I want you,' said Rosalie; and calling her sister to the



<P>window, she whispered an explanation in her ear; upon which the



<P>latter consented to go.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I remembered that eleven was the hour at which Mr. Weston proposed



<P>to come to the porter's lodge; and remembering that, I beheld the



<P>whole contrivance.&nbsp; Accordingly, at dinner, I was entertained with



<P>a long account of how Mr. Weston had overtaken them as they were



<P>walking along the road; and how they had had a long walk and talk



<P>with him, and really found him quite an agreeable companion; and



<P>how he must have been, and evidently was, delighted with them and



<P>their amazing condescension, &amp;c. &amp;c.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter02</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER II - FIRST LESSONS IN THE ART OF INSTRUCTION</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>AS we drove along, my spirits revived again, and I turned, with



<P>pleasure, to the contemplation of the new life upon which I was



<P>entering.&nbsp; But though it was not far past the middle of September,



<P>the heavy clouds and strong north-easterly wind combined to render



<P>the day extremely cold and dreary; and the journey seemed a very



<P>long one, for, as Smith observed, the roads were 'very heavy'; and



<P>certainly, his horse was very heavy too:&nbsp; it crawled up the hills,



<P>and crept down them, and only condescended to shake its sides in a



<P>trot where the road was at a dead level or a very gentle slope,



<P>which was rarely the case in those rugged regions; so that it was



<P>nearly one o'clock before we reached the place of our destination.



<P>Yet, after all, when we entered the lofty iron gateway, when we



<P>drove softly up the smooth, well-rolled carriage-road, with the



<P>green lawn on each side, studded with young trees, and approached



<P>the new but stately mansion of Wellwood, rising above its mushroom



<P>poplar-groves, my heart failed me, and I wished it were a mile or



<P>two farther off.&nbsp; For the first time in my life I must stand alone:



<P>there was no retreating now.&nbsp; I must enter that house, and



<P>introduce myself among its strange inhabitants.&nbsp; But how was it

to



<P>be done?&nbsp; True, I was near nineteen; but, thanks to my retired

life



<P>and the protecting care of my mother and sister, I well knew that



<P>many a girl of fifteen, or under, was gifted with a more womanly



<P>address, and greater ease and self-possession, than I was.&nbsp; Yet,

if



<P>Mrs. Bloomfield were a kind, motherly woman, I might do very well,



<P>after all; and the children, of course, I should soon be at ease



<P>with them - and Mr. Bloomfield, I hoped, I should have but little



<P>to do with.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Be calm, be calm, whatever happens,' I said within myself; and



<P>truly I kept this resolution so well, and was so fully occupied in



<P>steadying my nerves and stifling the rebellious flutter of my



<P>heart, that when I was admitted into the hall and ushered into the



<P>presence of Mrs. Bloomfield, I almost forgot to answer her polite



<P>salutation; and it afterwards struck me, that the little I did say



<P>was spoken in the tone of one half-dead or half-asleep.&nbsp; The lady,



<P>too, was somewhat chilly in her manner, as I discovered when I had



<P>time to reflect.&nbsp; She was a tall, spare, stately woman, with thick



<P>black hair, cold grey eyes, and extremely sallow complexion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>With due politeness, however, she showed me my bedroom, and left me



<P>there to take a little refreshment.&nbsp; I was somewhat dismayed at

my



<P>appearance on looking in the glass:&nbsp; the cold wind had swelled

and



<P>reddened my hands, uncurled and entangled my hair, and dyed my face



<P>of a pale purple; add to this my collar was horridly crumpled, my



<P>frock splashed with mud, my feet clad in stout new boots, and as



<P>the trunks were not brought up, there was no remedy; so having



<P>smoothed my hair as well as I could, and repeatedly twitched my



<P>obdurate collar, I proceeded to clomp down the two flights of



<P>stairs, philosophizing as I went; and with some difficulty found my



<P>way into the room where Mrs. Bloomfield awaited me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She led me into the dining-room, where the family luncheon had been



<P>laid out.&nbsp; Some beefsteaks and half-cold potatoes were set before



<P>me; and while I dined upon these, she sat opposite, watching me (as



<P>I thought) and endeavouring to sustain something like a



<P>conversation - consisting chiefly of a succession of commonplace



<P>remarks, expressed with frigid formality:&nbsp; but this might be more



<P>my fault than hers, for I really could NOT converse.&nbsp; In fact,

my



<P>attention was almost wholly absorbed in my dinner:&nbsp; not from



<P>ravenous appetite, but from distress at the toughness of the



<P>beefsteaks, and the numbness of my hands, almost palsied by their



<P>five-hours' exposure to the bitter wind.&nbsp; I would gladly have eaten



<P>the potatoes and let the meat alone, but having got a large piece



<P>of the latter on to my plate, I could not be so impolite as to



<P>leave it; so, after many awkward and unsuccessful attempts to cut



<P>it with the knife, or tear it with the fork, or pull it asunder



<P>between them, sensible that the awful lady was a spectator to the



<P>whole transaction, I at last desperately grasped the knife and fork



<P>in my fists, like a child of two years old, and fell to work with



<P>all the little strength I possessed.&nbsp; But this needed some apology



<P>- with a feeble attempt at a laugh, I said, 'My hands are so



<P>benumbed with the cold that I can scarcely handle my knife and



<P>fork.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I daresay you would find it cold,' replied she with a cool,



<P>immutable gravity that did not serve to re-assure me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When the ceremony was concluded, she led me into the sitting-room



<P>again, where she rang and sent for the children.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You will find them not very far advanced in their attainments,'



<P>said she, 'for I have had so little time to attend to their



<P>education myself, and we have thought them too young for a



<P>governess till now; but I think they are clever children, and very



<P>apt to learn, especially the little boy; he is, I think, the flower



<P>of the flock - a generous, noble-spirited boy, one to be led, but



<P>not driven, and remarkable for always speaking the truth.&nbsp; He seems



<P>to scorn deception' (this was good news).&nbsp; 'His sister Mary Ann



<P>will require watching,' continued she, 'but she is a very good girl



<P>upon the whole; though I wish her to be kept out of the nursery as



<P>much as possible, as she is now almost six years old, and might



<P>acquire bad habits from the nurses.&nbsp; I have ordered her crib to

be



<P>placed in your room, and if you will be so kind as to overlook her



<P>washing and dressing, and take charge of her clothes, she need have



<P>nothing further to do with the nursery maid.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I replied I was quite willing to do so; and at that moment my young



<P>pupils entered the apartment, with their two younger sisters.



<P>Master Tom Bloomfield was a well-grown boy of seven, with a



<P>somewhat wiry frame, flaxen hair, blue eyes, small turned-up nose,



<P>and fair complexion.&nbsp; Mary Ann was a tall girl too, somewhat dark



<P>like her mother, but with a round full face and a high colour in



<P>her cheeks.&nbsp; The second sister was Fanny, a very pretty little



<P>girl; Mrs. Bloomfield assured me she was a remarkably gentle child,



<P>and required encouragement:&nbsp; she had not learned anything yet;

but



<P>in a few days, she would be four years old, and then she might take



<P>her first lesson in the alphabet, and be promoted to the



<P>schoolroom.&nbsp; The remaining one was Harriet, a little broad, fat,



<P>merry, playful thing of scarcely two, that I coveted more than all



<P>the rest - but with her I had nothing to do.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I talked to my little pupils as well as I could, and tried to



<P>render myself agreeable; but with little success I fear, for their



<P>mother's presence kept me under an unpleasant restraint.&nbsp; They,



<P>however, were remarkably free from shyness.&nbsp; They seemed bold,



<P>lively children, and I hoped I should soon be on friendly terms



<P>with them - the little boy especially, of whom I had heard such a



<P>favourable character from his mamma.&nbsp; In Mary Ann there was a



<P>certain affected simper, and a craving for notice, that I was sorry



<P>to observe.&nbsp; But her brother claimed all my attention to himself;



<P>he stood bolt upright between me and the fire, with his hands



<P>behind his back, talking away like an orator, occasionally



<P>interrupting his discourse with a sharp reproof to his sisters when



<P>they made too much noise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, Tom, what a darling you are!' exclaimed his mother.&nbsp; 'Come

and



<P>kiss dear mamma; and then won't you show Miss Grey your schoolroom,



<P>and your nice new books?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I won't kiss YOU, mamma; but I WILL show Miss Grey my schoolroom,



<P>and my new books.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And MY schoolroom, and MY new books, Tom,' said Mary Ann.



<P>'They're mine too.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'They're MINE,' replied he decisively.&nbsp; 'Come along, Miss Grey

-



<P>I'll escort you.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When the room and books had been shown, with some bickerings



<P>between the brother and sister that I did my utmost to appease or



<P>mitigate, Mary Ann brought me her doll, and began to be very



<P>loquacious on the subject of its fine clothes, its bed, its chest



<P>of drawers, and other appurtenances; but Tom told her to hold her



<P>clamour, that Miss Grey might see his rocking-horse, which, with a



<P>most important bustle, he dragged forth from its corner into the



<P>middle of the room, loudly calling on me to attend to it.&nbsp; Then,



<P>ordering his sister to hold the reins, he mounted, and made me



<P>stand for ten minutes, watching how manfully he used his whip and



<P>spurs.&nbsp; Meantime, however, I admired Mary Ann's pretty doll, and



<P>all its possessions; and then told Master Tom he was a capital



<P>rider, but I hoped he would not use his whip and spurs so much when



<P>he rode a real pony.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, yes, I will!' said he, laying on with redoubled ardour.&nbsp; 'I'll



<P>cut into him like smoke!&nbsp; Eeh! my word! but he shall sweat for

it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This was very shocking; but I hoped in time to be able to work a



<P>reformation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Now you must put on your bonnet and shawl,' said the little hero,



<P>'and I'll show you my garden.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And MINE,' said Mary Ann.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Tom lifted his fist with a menacing gesture; she uttered a loud,



<P>shrill scream, ran to the other side of me, and made a face at him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Surely, Tom, you would not strike your sister!&nbsp; I hope I shall



<P>NEVER see you do that.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You will sometimes:&nbsp; I'm obliged to do it now and then to keep

her



<P>in order.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But it is not your business to keep her in order, you know - that



<P>is for - '

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, now go and put on your bonnet.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I don't know - it is so very cloudy and cold, it seems likely to



<P>rain; - and you know I have had a long drive.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No matter - you MUST come; I shall allow of no excuses,' replied



<P>the consequential little gentleman.&nbsp; And, as it was the first day



<P>of our acquaintance, I thought I might as well indulge him.&nbsp; It

was



<P>too cold for Mary Ann to venture, so she stayed with her mamma, to



<P>the great relief of her brother, who liked to have me all to



<P>himself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The garden was a large one, and tastefully laid out; besides



<P>several splendid dahlias, there were some other fine flowers still



<P>in bloom:&nbsp; but my companion would not give me time to examine them:



<P>I must go with him, across the wet grass, to a remote sequestered



<P>corner, the most important place in the grounds, because it



<P>contained HIS garden.&nbsp; There were two round beds, stocked with

a



<P>variety of plants.&nbsp; In one there was a pretty little rose-tree.&nbsp;

I



<P>paused to admire its lovely blossoms.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, never mind that!' said he, contemptuously.&nbsp; 'That's only MARY



<P>ANN'S garden; look, THIS is mine.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After I had observed every flower, and listened to a disquisition



<P>on every plant, I was permitted to depart; but first, with great



<P>pomp, he plucked a polyanthus and presented it to me, as one



<P>conferring a prodigious favour.&nbsp; I observed, on the grass about

his



<P>garden, certain apparatus of sticks and corn, and asked what they



<P>were.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Traps for birds.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why do you catch them?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Papa says they do harm.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And what do you do with them when you catch them?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Different things.&nbsp; Sometimes I give them to the cat; sometimes

I



<P>cut them in pieces with my penknife; but the next, I mean to roast



<P>alive.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And why do you mean to do such a horrible thing?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'For two reasons:&nbsp; first, to see how long it will live - and then,



<P>to see what it will taste like.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But don't you know it is extremely wicked to do such things?



<P>Remember, the birds can feel as well as you; and think, how would



<P>you like it yourself?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, that's nothing!&nbsp; I'm not a bird, and I can't feel what I do

to



<P>them.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But you will have to feel it some time, Tom:&nbsp; you have heard where



<P>wicked people go to when they die; and if you don't leave off



<P>torturing innocent birds, remember, you will have to go there, and



<P>suffer just what you have made them suffer.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, pooh!&nbsp; I shan't.&nbsp; Papa knows how I treat them, and he

never



<P>blames me for it:&nbsp; he says it is just what HE used to do when HE



<P>was a boy.&nbsp; Last summer, he gave me a nest full of young sparrows,



<P>and he saw me pulling off their legs and wings, and heads, and



<P>never said anything; except that they were nasty things, and I must



<P>not let them soil my trousers:&nbsp; end Uncle Robson was there too,

and



<P>he laughed, and said I was a fine boy.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But what would your mamma say?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, she doesn't care! she says it's a pity to kill the pretty



<P>singing birds, but the naughty sparrows, and mice, and rats, I may



<P>do what I like with.&nbsp; So now, Miss Grey, you see it is NOT wicked.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I still think it is, Tom; and perhaps your papa and mamma would



<P>think so too, if they thought much about it.&nbsp; However,' I



<P>internally added, 'they may say what they please, but I am



<P>determined you shall do nothing of the kind, as long as I have



<P>power to prevent it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He next took me across the lawn to see his mole-traps, and then



<P>into the stack-yard to see his weasel-traps:&nbsp; one of which, to

his



<P>great joy, contained a dead weasel; and then into the stable to



<P>see, not the fine carriage-horses, but a little rough colt, which



<P>he informed me had been bred on purpose for him, and he was to ride



<P>it as soon as it was properly trained.&nbsp; I tried to amuse the little



<P>fellow, and listened to all his chatter as complacently as I could;



<P>for I thought if he had any affections at all, I would endeavour to



<P>win them; and then, in time, I might be able to show him the error



<P>of his ways:&nbsp; but I looked in vain for that generous, noble spirit



<P>his mother talked of; though I could see he was not without a



<P>certain degree of quickness and penetration, when he chose to exert



<P>it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When we re-entered the house it was nearly tea-time.&nbsp; Master Tom



<P>told me that, as papa was from home, he and I and Mary Ann were to



<P>have tea with mamma, for a treat; for, on such occasions, she



<P>always dined at luncheon-time with them, instead of at six o'clock.



<P>Soon after tea, Mary Ann went to bed, but Tom favoured us with his



<P>company and conversation till eight.&nbsp; After he was gone, Mrs.



<P>Bloomfield further enlightened me on the subject of her children's



<P>dispositions and acquirements, and on what they were to learn, and



<P>how they were to be managed, and cautioned me to mention their



<P>defects to no one but herself.&nbsp; My mother had warned me before

to



<P>mention them as little as possible to HER, for people did not like



<P>to be told of their children's faults, and so I concluded I was to



<P>keep silence on them altogether.&nbsp; About half-past nine, Mrs.



<P>Bloomfield invited me to partake of a frugal supper of cold meat



<P>and bread.&nbsp; I was glad when that was over, and she took her bedroom



<P>candlestick and retired to rest; for though I wished to be pleased



<P>with her, her company was extremely irksome to me; and I could not



<P>help feeling that she was cold, grave, and forbidding - the very



<P>opposite of the kind, warm-hearted matron my hopes had depicted her



<P>to be.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">
