</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER III - A FEW MORE LESSONS</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>I ROSE next morning with a feeling of hopeful exhilaration, in



<P>spite of the disappointments already experienced; but I found the



<P>dressing of Mary Ann was no light matter, as her abundant hair was



<P>to be smeared with pomade, plaited in three long tails, and tied



<P>with bows of ribbon:&nbsp; a task my unaccustomed fingers found great



<P>difficulty in performing.&nbsp; She told me her nurse could do it in



<P>half the time, and, by keeping up a constant fidget of impatience,



<P>contrived to render me still longer.&nbsp; When all was done, we went



<P>into the schoolroom, where I met my other pupil, and chatted with



<P>the two till it was time to go down to breakfast.&nbsp; That meal being



<P>concluded, and a few civil words having been exchanged with Mrs.



<P>Bloomfield, we repaired to the schoolroom again, and commenced the



<P>business of the day.&nbsp; I found my pupils very backward, indeed;

but



<P>Tom, though averse to every species of mental exertion, was not



<P>without abilities.&nbsp; Mary Ann could scarcely read a word, and was

so



<P>careless and inattentive that I could hardly get on with her at



<P>all.&nbsp; However, by dint of great labour and patience, I managed

to



<P>get something done in the course of the morning, and then



<P>accompanied my young charge out into the garden and adjacent



<P>grounds, for a little recreation before dinner.&nbsp; There we got along



<P>tolerably together, except that I found they had no notion of going



<P>with me:&nbsp; I must go with them, wherever they chose to lead me.&nbsp;

I



<P>must run, walk, or stand, exactly as it suited their fancy.&nbsp; This,



<P>I thought, was reversing the order of things; and I found it doubly



<P>disagreeable, as on this as well as subsequent occasions, they



<P>seemed to prefer the dirtiest places and the most dismal



<P>occupations.&nbsp; But there was no remedy; either I must follow them,



<P>or keep entirely apart from them, and thus appear neglectful of my



<P>charge.&nbsp; To-day, they manifested a particular attachment to a well



<P>at the bottom of the lawn, where they persisted in dabbling with



<P>sticks and pebbles for above half an hour.&nbsp; I was in constant fear



<P>that their mother would see them from the window, and blame me for



<P>allowing them thus to draggle their clothes and wet their feet and



<P>hands, instead of taking exercise; but no arguments, commands, or



<P>entreaties could draw them away.&nbsp; If SHE did not see them, some

one



<P>else did - a gentleman on horseback had entered the gate and was



<P>proceeding up the road; at the distance of a few paces from us he



<P>paused, and calling to the children in a waspish penetrating tone,



<P>bade them 'keep out of that water.'&nbsp; 'Miss Grey,' said he, '(I



<P>suppose it IS Miss Grey), I am surprised that you should allow them



<P>to dirty their clothes in that manner!&nbsp; Don't you see how Miss



<P>Bloomfield has soiled her frock? and that Master Bloomfield's socks



<P>are quite wet? and both of them without gloves?&nbsp; Dear, dear!&nbsp;

Let



<P>me REQUEST that in future you will keep them DECENT at least!' so



<P>saying, he turned away, and continued his ride up to the house.



<P>This was Mr. Bloomfield.&nbsp; I was surprised that he should nominate



<P>his children Master and Miss Bloomfield; and still more so, that he



<P>should speak so uncivilly to me, their governess, and a perfect



<P>stranger to himself.&nbsp; Presently the bell rang to summon us in.&nbsp;

I



<P>dined with the children at one, while he and his lady took their



<P>luncheon at the same table.&nbsp; His conduct there did not greatly



<P>raise him in my estimation.&nbsp; He was a man of ordinary stature -



<P>rather below than above - and rather thin than stout, apparently



<P>between thirty and forty years of age:&nbsp; he had a large mouth, pale,



<P>dingy complexion, milky blue eyes, and hair the colour of a hempen



<P>cord.&nbsp; There was a roast leg of mutton before him:&nbsp; he helped

Mrs.



<P>Bloomfield, the children, and me, desiring me to cut up the



<P>children's meat; then, after twisting about the mutton in various



<P>directions, and eyeing it from different points, he pronounced it



<P>not fit to be eaten, and called for the cold beef.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What is the matter with the mutton, my dear?' asked his mate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'It is quite overdone.&nbsp; Don't you taste, Mrs. Bloomfield, that

all



<P>the goodness is roasted out of it?&nbsp; And can't you see that all

that



<P>nice, red gravy is completely dried away?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, I think the BEEF will suit you.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The beef was set before him, and he began to carve, but with the



<P>most rueful expressions of discontent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What is the matter with the BEEF, Mr. Bloomfield?&nbsp; I'm sure I



<P>thought it was very nice.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And so it WAS very nice.&nbsp; A nicer joint could not be; but it is



<P>QUITE spoiled,' replied he, dolefully.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'How so?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'How so!&nbsp; Why, don't you see how it is cut?&nbsp; Dear - dear!

it is



<P>quite shocking!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'They must have cut it wrong in the kitchen, then, for I'm sure I



<P>carved it quite properly here, yesterday.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No DOUBT they cut it wrong in the kitchen - the savages!&nbsp; Dear

-



<P>dear!&nbsp; Did ever any one see such a fine piece of beef so completely



<P>ruined?&nbsp; But remember that, in future, when a decent dish leaves



<P>this table, they shall not TOUCH it in the kitchen.&nbsp; Remember THAT,



<P>Mrs. Bloomfield!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Notwithstanding the ruinous state of the beef, the gentleman



<P>managed to out himself some delicate slices, part of which he ate



<P>in silence.&nbsp; When he next spoke, it was, in a less querulous tone,



<P>to ask what there was for dinner.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Turkey and grouse,' was the concise reply.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And what besides?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Fish.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What kind of fish?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I don't know.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'YOU DON'T KNOW?' cried he, looking solemnly up from his plate, and



<P>suspending his knife and fork in astonishment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No.&nbsp; I told the cook to get some fish - I did not particularize



<P>what.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, that beats everything!&nbsp; A lady professes to keep house,

and



<P>doesn't even know what fish is for dinner! professes to order fish,



<P>and doesn't specify what!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Perhaps, Mr. Bloomfield, you will order dinner yourself in



<P>future.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nothing more was said; and I was very glad to get out of the room



<P>with my pupils; for I never felt so ashamed and uncomfortable in my



<P>life for anything that was not my own fault.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the afternoon we applied to lessons again:&nbsp; then went out again;



<P>then had tea in the schoolroom; then I dressed Mary Ann for



<P>dessert; and when she and her brother had gone down to the dining-



<P>room, I took the opportunity of beginning a letter to my dear



<P>friends at home:&nbsp; but the children came up before I had half



<P>completed it.&nbsp; At seven I had to put Mary Ann to bed; then I played



<P>with Tom till eight, when he, too, went; and I finished my letter



<P>and unpacked my clothes, which I had hitherto found no opportunity



<P>for doing, and, finally, went to bed myself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But this is a very favourable specimen of a day's proceedings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>My task of instruction and surveillance, instead of becoming easier



<P>as my charges and I got better accustomed to each other, became



<P>more arduous as their characters unfolded.&nbsp; The name of governess,



<P>I soon found, was a mere mockery as applied to me:&nbsp; my pupils had



<P>no more notion of obedience than a wild, unbroken colt.&nbsp; The



<P>habitual fear of their father's peevish temper, and the dread of



<P>the punishments he was wont to inflict when irritated, kept them



<P>generally within bounds in his immediate presence.&nbsp; The girls,

too,



<P>had some fear of their mother's anger; and the boy might



<P>occasionally be bribed to do as she bid him by the hope of reward;



<P>but I had no rewards to offer; and as for punishments, I was given



<P>to understand, the parents reserved that privilege to themselves;



<P>and yet they expected me to keep my pupils in order.&nbsp; Other



<P>children might be guided by the fear of anger and the desire of



<P>approbation; but neither the one nor the other had any effect upon



<P>these.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Master Tom, not content with refusing to be ruled, must needs set



<P>up as a ruler, and manifested a determination to keep, not only his



<P>sisters, but his governess in order, by violent manual and pedal



<P>applications; and, as he was a tall, strong boy of his years, this



<P>occasioned no trifling inconvenience.&nbsp; A few sound boxes on the



<P>ear, on such occasions, might have settled the matter easily



<P>enough:&nbsp; but as, in that case, he might make up some story to his



<P>mother which she would be sure to believe, as she had such unshaken



<P>faith in his veracity - though I had already discovered it to be by



<P>no means unimpeachable - I determined to refrain from striking him,



<P>even in self-defence; and, in his most violent moods, my only



<P>resource was to throw him on his back and hold his hands and feet



<P>till the frenzy was somewhat abated.&nbsp; To the difficulty of



<P>preventing him from doing what he ought not, was added that of



<P>forcing him to do what he ought.&nbsp; Often he would positively refuse



<P>to learn, or to repeat his lessons, or even to look at his book.



<P>Here, again, a good birch rod might have been serviceable; but, as



<P>my powers were so limited, I must make the best use of what I had.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As there were no settled hours for study and play, I resolved to



<P>give my pupils a certain task, which, with moderate attention, they



<P>could perform in a short time; and till this was done, however



<P>weary I was, or however perverse they might be, nothing short of



<P>parental interference should induce me to suffer them to leave the



<P>schoolroom, even if I should sit with my chair against the door to



<P>keep them in.&nbsp; Patience, Firmness, and Perseverance were my only



<P>weapons; and these I resolved to use to the utmost.&nbsp; I determined



<P>always strictly to fulfil the threats and promises I made; and, to



<P>that end, I must be cautious to threaten and promise nothing that I



<P>could not perform.&nbsp; Then, I would carefully refrain from all



<P>useless irritability and indulgence of my own ill-temper:&nbsp; when



<P>they behaved tolerably, I would be as kind and obliging as it was



<P>in my power to be, in order to make the widest possible distinction



<P>between good and bad conduct; I would reason with them, too, in the



<P>simplest and most effective manner.&nbsp; When I reproved them, or



<P>refused to gratify their wishes, after a glaring fault, it should



<P>be more in sorrow than in anger:&nbsp; their little hymns and prayers

I



<P>would make plain and clear to their understanding; when they said



<P>their prayers at night and asked pardon for their offences, I would



<P>remind them of the sins of the past day, solemnly, but in perfect



<P>kindness, to avoid raising a spirit of opposition; penitential



<P>hymns should be said by the naughty, cheerful ones by the



<P>comparatively good; and every kind of instruction I would convey to



<P>them, as much as possible, by entertaining discourse - apparently



<P>with no other object than their present amusement in view.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>By these means I hoped in time both to benefit the children and to



<P>gain the approbation of their parents; and also to convince my



<P>friends at home that I was not so wanting in skill and prudence as



<P>they supposed.&nbsp; I knew the difficulties I had to contend with were



<P>great; but I knew (at least I believed) unremitting patience and



<P>perseverance could overcome them; and night and morning I implored



<P>Divine assistance to this end.&nbsp; But either the children were so



<P>incorrigible, the parents so unreasonable, or myself so mistaken in



<P>my views, or so unable to carry them out, that my best intentions



<P>and most strenuous efforts seemed productive of no better result



<P>than sport to the children, dissatisfaction to their parents, and



<P>torment to myself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The task of instruction was as arduous for the body as the mind.&nbsp;

I



<P>had to run after my pupils to catch them, to carry or drag them to



<P>the table, and often forcibly to hold them there till the lesson



<P>was done.&nbsp; Tom I frequently put into a corner, seating myself



<P>before him in a chair, with a book which contained the little task



<P>that must be said or read, before he was released, in my hand.&nbsp;

He



<P>was not strong enough to push both me and the chair away, so he



<P>would stand twisting his body and face into the most grotesque and



<P>singular contortions - laughable, no doubt, to an unconcerned



<P>spectator, but not to me - and uttering loud yells and doleful



<P>outcries, intended to represent weeping but wholly without the



<P>accompaniment of tears.&nbsp; I knew this was done solely for the



<P>purpose of annoying me; and, therefore, however I might inwardly



<P>tremble with impatience and irritation, I manfully strove to



<P>suppress all visible signs of molestation, and affected to sit with



<P>calm indifference, waiting till it should please him to cease this



<P>pastime, and prepare for a run in the garden, by casting his eye on



<P>the book and reading or repeating the few words he was required to



<P>say.&nbsp; Sometimes he was determined to do his writing badly; and

I



<P>had to hold his hand to prevent him from purposely blotting or



<P>disfiguring the paper.&nbsp; Frequently I threatened that, if he did

not



<P>do better, he should have another line:&nbsp; then he would stubbornly



<P>refuse to write this line; and I, to save my word, had finally to



<P>resort to the expedient of holding his fingers upon the pen, and



<P>forcibly drawing his hand up and down, till, in spite of his



<P>resistance, the line was in some sort completed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Yet Tom was by no means the most unmanageable of my pupils:



<P>sometimes, to my great joy, he would have the sense to see that his



<P>wisest policy was to finish his tasks, and go out and amuse himself



<P>till I and his sisters came to join him; which frequently was not



<P>at all, for Mary Ann seldom followed his example in this



<P>particular:&nbsp; she apparently preferred rolling on the floor to any



<P>other amusement:&nbsp; down she would drop like a leaden weight; and



<P>when I, with great difficulty, had succeeded in rooting her thence,



<P>I had still to hold her up with one arm, while with the other I



<P>held the book from which she was to read or spell her lesson.&nbsp;

As



<P>the dead weight of the big girl of six became too heavy for one arm



<P>to bear, I transferred it to the other; or, if both were weary of



<P>the burden, I carried her into a corner, and told her she might



<P>come out when she should find the use of her feet, and stand up:



<P>but she generally preferred lying there like a log till dinner or



<P>teatime, when, as I could not deprive her of her meals, she must be



<P>liberated, and would come crawling out with a grin of triumph on



<P>her round, red face.&nbsp; Often she would stubbornly refuse to



<P>pronounce some particular word in her lesson; and now I regret the



<P>lost labour I have had in striving to conquer her obstinacy.&nbsp; If

I



<P>had passed it over as a matter of no consequence, it would have



<P>been better for both parties, than vainly striving to overcome it



<P>as I did; but I thought it my absolute duty to crush this vicious



<P>tendency in the bud:&nbsp; and so it was, if I could have done it; and



<P>had my powers been less limited, I might have enforced obedience;



<P>but, as it was, it was a trial of strength between her and me, in



<P>which she generally came off victorious; and every victory served



<P>to encourage and strengthen her for a future contest.&nbsp; In vain

I



<P>argued, coaxed, entreated, threatened, scolded; in vain I kept her



<P>in from play, or, if obliged to take her out, refused to play with



<P>her, or to speak kindly or have anything to do with her; in vain I



<P>tried to set before her the advantages of doing as she was bid, and



<P>being loved, and kindly treated in consequence, and the



<P>disadvantages of persisting in her absurd perversity.&nbsp; Sometimes,



<P>when she would ask me to do something for her, I would answer, -



<P>'Yes, I will, Mary Ann, if you will only say that word.&nbsp; Come!



<P>you'd better say it at once, and have no more trouble about it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Then, of course, I can do nothing for you.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>With me, at her age, or under, neglect and disgrace were the most



<P>dreadful of punishments; but on her they made no impression.



<P>Sometimes, exasperated to the utmost pitch, I would shake her



<P>violently by the shoulder, or pull her long hair, or put her in the



<P>corner; for which she punished me with loud, shrill, piercing



<P>screams, that went through my head like a knife.&nbsp; She knew I hated



<P>this, and when she had shrieked her utmost, would look into my face



<P>with an air of vindictive satisfaction, exclaiming, - 'NOW, then!



<P>THAT'S for you!' and then shriek again and again, till I was forced



<P>to stop my ears.&nbsp; Often these dreadful cries would bring Mrs.



<P>Bloomfield up to inquire what was the matter?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Mary Ann is a naughty girl, ma'am.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But what are these shocking screams?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'She is screaming in a passion.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I never heard such a dreadful noise!&nbsp; You might be killing her.



<P>Why is she not out with her brother?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I cannot get her to finish her lessons.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'But Mary Ann must be a GOOD girl, and finish her lessons.'&nbsp; This



<P>was blandly spoken to the child.&nbsp; 'And I hope I shall NEVER hear



<P>such terrible cries again!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And fixing her cold, stony eyes upon me with a look that could not



<P>be mistaken, she would shut the door, and walk away.&nbsp; Sometimes

I



<P>would try to take the little obstinate creature by surprise, and



<P>casually ask her the word while she was thinking of something else;



<P>frequently she would begin to say it, and then suddenly cheek



<P>herself, with a provoking look that seemed to say, 'Ah! I'm too



<P>sharp for you; you shan't trick it out of me, either.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On another occasion, I pretended to forget the whole affair; and



<P>talked and played with her as usual, till night, when I put her to



<P>bed; then bending over her, while she lay all smiles and good



<P>humour, just before departing, I said, as cheerfully and kindly as



<P>before - 'Now, Mary Ann, just tell me that word before I kiss you



<P>good-night.&nbsp; You are a good girl now, and, of course, you will

say



<P>it.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'No, I won't.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Then I can't kiss you.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Well, I don't care.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In vain I expressed my sorrow; in vain I lingered for some symptom



<P>of contrition; she really 'didn't care,' and I left her alone, and



<P>in darkness, wondering most of all at this last proof of insensate



<P>stubbornness.&nbsp; In MY childhood I could not imagine a more



<P>afflictive punishment than for my mother to refuse to kiss me at



<P>night:&nbsp; the very idea was terrible.&nbsp; More than the idea I

never



<P>felt, for, happily, I never committed a fault that was deemed



<P>worthy of such penalty; but once I remember, for some transgression



<P>of my sister's, our mother thought proper to inflict it upon her:



<P>what SHE felt, I cannot tell; but my sympathetic tears and



<P>suffering for her sake I shall not soon forget.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Another troublesome trait in Mary Ann was her incorrigible



<P>propensity to keep running into the nursery, to play with her



<P>little sisters and the nurse.&nbsp; This was natural enough, but, as

it



<P>was against her mother's express desire, I, of course, forbade her



<P>to do so, and did my utmost to keep her with me; but that only



<P>increased her relish for the nursery, and the more I strove to keep



<P>her out of it, the oftener she went, and the longer she stayed, to



<P>the great dissatisfaction of Mrs. Bloomfield, who, I well knew,



<P>would impute all the blame of the matter to me.&nbsp; Another of my



<P>trials was the dressing in the morning:&nbsp; at one time she would

not



<P>be washed; at another she would not be dressed, unless she might



<P>wear some particular frock, that I knew her mother would not like



<P>her to have; at another she would scream and run away if I



<P>attempted to touch her hair.&nbsp; So that, frequently, when, after

much



<P>trouble and toil, I had, at length, succeeded in bringing her down,



<P>the breakfast was nearly half over; and black looks from 'mamma,'



<P>and testy observations from 'papa,' spoken at me, if not to me,



<P>were sure to be my meed:&nbsp; for few things irritated the latter so



<P>much as want of punctuality at meal times.&nbsp; Then, among the minor



<P>annoyances, was my inability to satisfy Mrs. Bloomfield with her



<P>daughter's dress; and the child's hair 'was never fit to be seen.'



<P>Sometimes, as a powerful reproach to me, she would perform the



<P>office of tire woman herself, and then complain bitterly of the



<P>trouble it gave her.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When little Fanny came into the schoolroom, I hoped she would be



<P>mild and inoffensive, at least; but a few days, if not a few hours,



<P>sufficed to destroy the illusion:&nbsp; I found her a mischievous,



<P>intractable little creature, given up to falsehood and deception,



<P>young as she was, and alarmingly fond of exercising her two



<P>favourite weapons of offence and defence:&nbsp; that of spitting in

the



<P>faces of those who incurred her displeasure, and bellowing like a



<P>bull when her unreasonable desires were not gratified.&nbsp; As she,



<P>generally, was pretty quiet in her parents' presence, and they were



<P>impressed with the notion of her being a remarkably gentle child,



<P>her falsehoods were readily believed, and her loud uproars led them



<P>to suspect harsh and injudicious treatment on my part; and when, at



<P>length, her bad disposition became manifest even to their



<P>prejudiced eyes, I felt that the whole was attributed to me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What a naughty girl Fanny is getting!' Mrs. Bloomfield would say



<P>to her spouse.&nbsp; 'Don't you observe, my dear, how she is altered



<P>since she entered the schoolroom?&nbsp; She will soon be as bad as the



<P>other two; and, I am sorry to say, they have quite deteriorated of



<P>late.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'You may say that,' was the answer.&nbsp; 'I've been thinking that same



<P>myself.&nbsp; I thought when we got them a governess they'd improve;



<P>but, instead of that, they get worse and worse:&nbsp; I don't know how



<P>it is with their learning, but their habits, I know, make no sort



<P>of improvement; they get rougher, and dirtier, and more unseemly



<P>every day.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I knew this was all pointed at me; and these, and all similar



<P>innuendoes, affected me far more deeply than any open accusations



<P>would have done; for against the latter I should have been roused



<P>to speak in my own defence:&nbsp; now I judged it my wisest plan to



<P>subdue every resentful impulse, suppress every sensitive shrinking,



<P>and go on perseveringly, doing my best; for, irksome as my



<P>situation was, I earnestly wished to retain it.&nbsp; I thought, if

I



<P>could struggle on with unremitting firmness and integrity, the



<P>children would in time become more humanized:&nbsp; every month would



<P>contribute to make them some little wiser, and, consequently, more



<P>manageable; for a child of nine or ten as frantic and ungovernable



<P>as these at six and seven would be a maniac.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I flattered myself I was benefiting my parents and sister by my



<P>continuance here; for small as the salary was, I still was earning



<P>something, and with strict economy I could easily manage to have



<P>something to spare for them, if they would favour me by taking it.



<P>Then it was by my own will that I had got the place:&nbsp; I had brought



<P>all this tribulation on myself, and I was determined to bear it;



<P>nay, more than that, I did not even regret the step I had taken.&nbsp;

I



<P>longed to show my friends that, even now, I was competent to



<P>undertake the charge, and able to acquit myself honourably to the



<P>end; and if ever I felt it degrading to submit so quietly, or



<P>intolerable to toil so constantly, I would turn towards my home,



<P>and say within myself -

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They may crush, but they shall not subdue me!



<P>'Tis of thee that I think, not of them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>About Christmas I was allowed to visit home; but my holiday was



<P>only of a fortnight's duration:&nbsp; 'For,' said Mrs. Bloomfield, 'I



<P>thought, as you had seen your friends so lately, you would not care



<P>for a longer stay.'&nbsp; I left her to think so still:&nbsp; but she

little



<P>knew how long, how wearisome those fourteen weeks of absence had



<P>been to me; how intensely I had longed for my holidays, how greatly



<P>I was disappointed at their curtailment.&nbsp; Yet she was not to blame



<P>in this.&nbsp; I had never told her my feelings, and she could not be



<P>expected to divine them; I had not been with her a full term, and



<P>she was justified in not allowing me a full vacation.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter17</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XVII - CONFESSIONS</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>AS I am in the way of confessions I may as well acknowledge that,



<P>about this time, I paid more attention to dress than ever I had



<P>done before.&nbsp; This is not saying much - for hitherto I had been

a



<P>little neglectful in that particular; but now, also, it was no



<P>uncommon thing to spend as much as two minutes in the contemplation



<P>of my own image in the glass; though I never could derive any



<P>consolation from such a study.&nbsp; I could discover no beauty in those



<P>marked features, that pale hollow cheek, and ordinary dark brown



<P>hair; there might be intellect in the forehead, there might be



<P>expression in the dark grey eyes, but what of that? - a low Grecian



<P>brow, and large black eyes devoid of sentiment would be esteemed



<P>far preferable.&nbsp; It is foolish to wish for beauty.&nbsp; Sensible

people



<P>never either desire it for themselves or care about it in others.



<P>If the mind be but well cultivated, and the heart well disposed, no



<P>one ever cares for the exterior.&nbsp; So said the teachers of our



<P>childhood; and so say we to the children of the present day.&nbsp; All



<P>very judicious and proper, no doubt; but are such assertions



<P>supported by actual experience?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>We are naturally disposed to love what gives us pleasure, and what



<P>more pleasing than a beautiful face - when we know no harm of the



<P>possessor at least?&nbsp; A little girl loves her bird - Why?&nbsp;

Because



<P>it lives and feels; because it is helpless and harmless?&nbsp; A toad,



<P>likewise, lives and feels, and is equally helpless and harmless;



<P>but though she would not hurt a toad, she cannot love it like the



<P>bird, with its graceful form, soft feathers, and bright, speaking



<P>eyes.&nbsp; If a woman is fair and amiable, she is praised for both



<P>qualities, but especially the former, by the bulk of mankind:&nbsp;

if,



<P>on the other hand, she is disagreeable in person and character, her



<P>plainness is commonly inveighed against as her greatest crime,



<P>because, to common observers, it gives the greatest offence; while,



<P>if she is plain and good, provided she is a person of retired



<P>manners and secluded life, no one ever knows of her goodness,



<P>except her immediate connections.&nbsp; Others, on the contrary, are



<P>disposed to form unfavourable opinions of her mind, and



<P>disposition, if it be but to excuse themselves for their



<P>instinctive dislike of one so unfavoured by nature; and VISA VERSA



<P>with her whose angel form conceals a vicious heart, or sheds a



<P>false, deceitful charm over defects and foibles that would not be



<P>tolerated in another.&nbsp; They that have beauty, let them be thankful



<P>for it, and make a good use of it, like any other talent; they that



<P>have it not, let them console themselves, and do the best they can



<P>without it:&nbsp; certainly, though liable to be over-estimated, it

is a



<P>gift of God, and not to be despised.&nbsp; Many will feel this who have



<P>felt that they could love, and whose hearts tell them that they are



<P>worthy to be loved again; while yet they are debarred, by the lack



<P>of this or some such seeming trifle, from giving and receiving that



<P>happiness they seem almost made to feel and to impart.&nbsp; As well



<P>might the humble glowworm despise that power of giving light



<P>without which the roving fly might pass her and repass her a



<P>thousand times, and never rest beside her:&nbsp; she might hear her



<P>winged darling buzzing over and around her; he vainly seeking her,



<P>she longing to be found, but with no power to make her presence



<P>known, no voice to call him, no wings to follow his flight; - the



<P>fly must seek another mate, the worm must live and die alone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Such were some of my reflections about this period.&nbsp; I might go

on



<P>prosing more and more, I might dive much deeper, and disclose other



<P>thoughts, propose questions the reader might be puzzled to answer,



<P>and deduce arguments that might startle his prejudices, or,



<P>perhaps, provoke his ridicule, because he could not comprehend



<P>them; but I forbear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Now, therefore, let us return to Miss Murray.&nbsp; She accompanied

her



<P>mamma to the ball on Tuesday; of course splendidly attired, and



<P>delighted with her prospects and her charms.&nbsp; As Ashby Park was



<P>nearly ten miles distant from Horton Lodge, they had to set out



<P>pretty early, and I intended to have spent the evening with Nancy



<P>Brown, whom I had not seen for a long time; but my kind pupil took



<P>care I should spend it neither there nor anywhere else beyond the



<P>limits of the schoolroom, by giving me a piece of music to copy,



<P>which kept me closely occupied till bed-time.&nbsp; About eleven next



<P>morning, as soon as she had left her room, she came to tell me her



<P>news.&nbsp; Sir Thomas had indeed proposed to her at the ball; an event



<P>which reflected great credit on her mamma's sagacity, if not upon



<P>her skill in contrivance.&nbsp; I rather incline to the belief that

she



<P>had first laid her plans, and then predicted their success.&nbsp; The



<P>offer had been accepted, of course, and the bridegroom elect was



<P>coming that day to settle matters with Mr. Murray.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Rosalie was pleased with the thoughts of becoming mistress of Ashby



<P>Park; she was elated with the prospect of the bridal ceremony and



<P>its attendant splendour and eclat, the honeymoon spent abroad, and



<P>the subsequent gaieties she expected to enjoy in London and



<P>elsewhere; she appeared pretty well pleased too, for the time



<P>being, with Sir Thomas himself, because she had so lately seen him,



<P>danced with him, and been flattered by him; but, after all, she



<P>seemed to shrink from the idea of being so soon united:&nbsp; she wished



<P>the ceremony to be delayed some months, at least; and I wished it



<P>too.&nbsp; It seemed a horrible thing to hurry on the inauspicious



<P>match, and not to give the poor creature time to think and reason



<P>on the irrevocable step she was about to take.&nbsp; I made no



<P>pretension to 'a mother's watchful, anxious care,' but I was amazed



<P>and horrified at Mrs. Murray's heartlessness, or want of thought



<P>for the real good of her child; and by my unheeded warnings and



<P>exhortations, I vainly strove to remedy the evil.&nbsp; Miss Murray

only



<P>laughed at what I said; and I soon found that her reluctance to an



<P>immediate union arose chiefly from a desire to do what execution



<P>she could among the young gentlemen of her acquaintance, before she



<P>was incapacitated from further mischief of the kind.&nbsp; It was for



<P>this cause that, before confiding to me the secret of her



<P>engagement, she had extracted a promise that I would not mention a



<P>word on the subject to any one.&nbsp; And when I saw this, and when

I



<P>beheld her plunge more recklessly than ever into the depths of



<P>heartless coquetry, I had no more pity for her.&nbsp; 'Come what will,'



<P>I thought, 'she deserves it.&nbsp; Sir Thomas cannot be too bad for

her;



<P>and the sooner she is incapacitated from deceiving and injuring



<P>others the better.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The wedding was fixed for the first of June.&nbsp; Between that and

the



<P>critical ball was little more than six weeks; but, with Rosalie's



<P>accomplished skill and resolute exertion, much might be done, even



<P>within that period; especially as Sir Thomas spent most of the



<P>interim in London; whither he went up, it was said, to settle



<P>affairs with his lawyer, and make other preparations for the



<P>approaching nuptials.&nbsp; He endeavoured to supply the want of his



<P>presence by a pretty constant fire of billets-doux; but these did



<P>not attract the neighbours' attention, and open their eyes, as



<P>personal visits would have done; and old Lady Ashby's haughty, sour



<P>spirit of reserve withheld her from spreading the news, while her



<P>indifferent health prevented her coming to visit her future



<P>daughter-in-law; so that, altogether, this affair was kept far



<P>closer than such things usually are.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Rosalie would sometimes show her lover's epistles to me, to



<P>convince me what a kind, devoted husband he would make.&nbsp; She showed



<P>me the letters of another individual, too, the unfortunate Mr.



<P>Green, who had not the courage, or, as she expressed it, the



<P>'spunk,' to plead his cause in person, but whom one denial would



<P>not satisfy:&nbsp; he must write again and again.&nbsp; He would not

have



<P>done so if he could have seen the grimaces his fair idol made over



<P>his moving appeals to her feelings, and heard her scornful



<P>laughter, and the opprobrious epithets she heaped upon him for his



<P>perseverance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Why don't you tell him, at once, that you are engaged?' I asked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, I don't want him to know that,' replied she.&nbsp; 'If he knew

it,



<P>his sisters and everybody would know it, and then there would be an



<P>end of my - ahem!&nbsp; And, besides, if I told him that, he would think



<P>my engagement was the only obstacle, and that I would have him if I



<P>were free; which I could not bear that any man should think, and



<P>he, of all others, at least.&nbsp; Besides, I don't care for his



<P>letters,' she added, contemptuously; 'he may write as often as he



<P>pleases, and look as great a calf as he likes when I meet him; it



<P>only amuses me.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Meantime, young Meltham was pretty frequent in his visits to the



<P>house or transits past it; and, judging by Matilda's execrations



<P>and reproaches, her sister paid more attention to him than civility



<P>required; in other words, she carried on as animated a flirtation



<P>as the presence of her parents would admit.&nbsp; She made some attempts



<P>to bring Mr. Hatfield once more to her feet; but finding them



<P>unsuccessful, she repaid his haughty indifference with still



<P>loftier scorn, and spoke of him with as much disdain and



<P>detestation as she had formerly done of his curate.&nbsp; But, amid

all



<P>this, she never for a moment lost sight of Mr. Weston.&nbsp; She



<P>embraced every opportunity of meeting him, tried every art to



<P>fascinate him, and pursued him with as much perseverance as if she



<P>really loved him and no other, and the happiness of her life



<P>depended upon eliciting a return of affection.&nbsp; Such conduct was



<P>completely beyond my comprehension.&nbsp; Had I seen it depicted in

a



<P>novel, I should have thought it unnatural; had I heard it described



<P>by others, I should have deemed it a mistake or an exaggeration;



<P>but when I saw it with my own eyes, and suffered from it too, I



<P>could only conclude that excessive vanity, like drunkenness,



<P>hardens the heart, enslaves the faculties, and perverts the



<P>feelings; and that dogs are not the only creatures which, when



<P>gorged to the throat, will yet gloat over what they cannot devour,



<P>and grudge the smallest morsel to a starving brother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>She now became extremely beneficent to the poor cottagers.&nbsp; Her



<P>acquaintance among them was more widely extended, her visits to



<P>their humble dwellings were more frequent and excursive than they



<P>had ever been before.&nbsp; Hereby, she earned among them the reputation



<P>of a condescending and very charitable young lady; and their



<P>encomiums were sure to be repeated to Mr. Weston:&nbsp; whom also she



<P>had thus a daily chance of meeting in one or other of their abodes,



<P>or in her transits to and fro; and often, likewise, she could



<P>gather, through their gossip, to what places he was likely to go at



<P>such and such a time, whether to baptize a child, or to visit the



<P>aged, the sick, the sad, or the dying; and most skilfully she laid



<P>her plans accordingly.&nbsp; In these excursions she would sometimes

go



<P>with her sister - whom, by some means, she had persuaded or bribed



<P>to enter into her schemes - sometimes alone, never, now, with me;



<P>so that I was debarred the pleasure of seeing Mr. Weston, or



<P>hearing his voice even in conversation with another:&nbsp; which would



<P>certainly have been a very great pleasure, however hurtful or



<P>however fraught with pain.&nbsp; I could not even see him at church:



<P>for Miss Murray, under some trivial pretext, chose to take



<P>possession of that corner in the family pew which had been mine



<P>ever since I came; and, unless I had the presumption to station



<P>myself between Mr. and Mrs. Murray, I must sit with my back to the



<P>pulpit, which I accordingly did.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Now, also, I never walked home with my pupils:&nbsp; they said their



<P>mamma thought it did not look well to see three people out of the



<P>family walking, and only two going in the carriage; and, as they



<P>greatly preferred walking in fine weather, I should be honoured by



<P>going with the seniors.&nbsp; 'And besides,' said they, 'you can't walk



<P>as fast as we do; you know you're always lagging behind.'&nbsp; I knew



<P>these were false excuses, but I made no objections, and never



<P>contradicted such assertions, well knowing the motives which



<P>dictated them.&nbsp; And in the afternoons, during those six memorable



<P>weeks, I never went to church at all.&nbsp; If I had a cold, or any



<P>slight indisposition, they took advantage of that to make me stay



<P>at home; and often they would tell me they were not going again



<P>that day, themselves, and then pretend to change their minds, and



<P>set off without telling me:&nbsp; so managing their departure that I



<P>never discovered the change of purpose till too late.&nbsp; Upon their



<P>return home, on one of these occasions, they entertained me with an



<P>animated account of a conversation they had had with Mr. Weston as



<P>they came along.&nbsp; 'And he asked if you were ill, Miss Grey,' said



<P>Matilda; 'but we told him you were quite well, only you didn't want



<P>to come to church - so he'll think you're turned wicked.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>All chance meetings on week-days were likewise carefully prevented;



<P>for, lest I should go to see poor Nancy Brown or any other person,



<P>Miss Murray took good care to provide sufficient employment for all



<P>my leisure hours.&nbsp; There was always some drawing to finish, some



<P>music to copy, or some work to do, sufficient to incapacitate me



<P>from indulging in anything beyond a short walk about the grounds,



<P>however she or her sister might be occupied.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One morning, having sought and waylaid Mr. Weston, they returned in



<P>high glee to give me an account of their interview.&nbsp; 'And he asked



<P>after you again,' said Matilda, in spite of her sister's silent but



<P>imperative intimation that she should hold her tongue.&nbsp; 'He



<P>wondered why you were never with us, and thought you must have



<P>delicate health, as you came out so seldom.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'He didn't Matilda - what nonsense you're talking!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Oh, Rosalie, what a lie!&nbsp; He did, you know; and you said - Don't,



<P>Rosalie - hang it! - I won't be pinched so!&nbsp; And, Miss Grey,



<P>Rosalie told him you were quite well, but you were always so buried



<P>in your books that you had no pleasure in anything else.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'What an idea he must have of me!' I thought.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'And,' I asked, 'does old Nancy ever inquire about me?'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'Yes; and we tell her you are so fond of reading and drawing that



<P>you can do nothing else.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'That is not the case though; if you had told her I was so busy I



<P>could not come to see her, it would have been nearer the truth.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>'I don't think it would,' replied Miss Murray, suddenly kindling



<P>up; 'I'm sure you have plenty of time to yourself now, when you



<P>have so little teaching to do.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was no use beginning to dispute with such indulged, unreasoning



<P>creatures:&nbsp; so I held my peace.&nbsp; I was accustomed, now, to

keeping



<P>silence when things distasteful to my ear were uttered; and now,



<P>too, I was used to wearing a placid smiling countenance when my



<P>heart was bitter within me.&nbsp; Only those who have felt the like

can



<P>imagine my feelings, as I sat with an assumption of smiling



<P>indifference, listening to the accounts of those meetings and



<P>interviews with Mr. Weston, which they seemed to find such pleasure



<P>in describing to me; and hearing things asserted of him which, from



<P>the character of the man, I knew to be exaggerations and



<P>perversions of the truth, if not entirely false - things derogatory



<P>to him, and flattering to them - especially to Miss Murray - which



<P>I burned to contradict, or, at least, to show my doubts about, but



<P>dared not; lest, in expressing my disbelief, I should display my



<P>interest too.&nbsp; Other things I heard, which I felt or feared were



<P>indeed too true:&nbsp; but I must still conceal my anxiety respecting



<P>him, my indignation against them, beneath a careless aspect;



<P>others, again, mere hints of something said or done, which I longed



<P>to hear more of, but could not venture to inquire.&nbsp; So passed the



<P>weary time.&nbsp; I could not even comfort myself with saying, 'She

will



<P>soon be married; and then there may be hope.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Soon after her marriage the holidays would come; and when I



<P>returned from home, most likely, Mr. Weston would be gone, for I



<P>was told that he and the Rector could not agree (the Rector's



<P>fault, of course), and he was about to remove to another place.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>No - besides my hope in God, my only consolation was in thinking



<P>that, though he know it not, I was more worthy of his love than



<P>Rosalie Murray, charming and engaging as she was; for I could



<P>appreciate his excellence, which she could not:&nbsp; I would devote

my



<P>life to the promotion of his happiness; she would destroy his



<P>happiness for the momentary gratification of her own vanity.&nbsp; 'Oh,



<P>if he could but know the difference!'&nbsp; I would earnestly exclaim.



<P>'But no!&nbsp; I would not have him see my heart:&nbsp; yet, if he could

but



<P>know her hollowness, her worthless, heartless frivolity, he would



<P>then be safe, and I should be - ALMOST happy, though I might never



<P>see him more!'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I fear, by this time, the reader is well nigh disgusted with the



<P>folly and weakness I have so freely laid before him.&nbsp; I never



<P>disclosed it then, and would not have done so had my own sister or



<P>my mother been with me in the house.&nbsp; I was a close and resolute



<P>dissembler - in this one case at least.&nbsp; My prayers, my tears,

my



<P>wishes, fears, and lamentations, were witnessed by myself and



<P>heaven alone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When we are harassed by sorrows or anxieties, or long oppressed by



<P>any powerful feelings which we must keep to ourselves, for which we



<P>can obtain and seek no sympathy from any living creature, and which



<P>yet we cannot, or will not wholly crush, we often naturally seek



<P>relief in poetry - and often find it, too - whether in the



<P>effusions of others, which seem to harmonize with our existing



<P>case, or in our own attempts to give utterance to those thoughts



<P>and feelings in strains less musical, perchance, but more



<P>appropriate, and therefore more penetrating and sympathetic, and,



<P>for the time, more soothing, or more powerful to rouse and to



<P>unburden the oppressed and swollen heart.&nbsp; Before this time, at



<P>Wellwood House and here, when suffering from home-sick melancholy,



<P>I had sought relief twice or thrice at this secret source of



<P>consolation; and now I flew to it again, with greater avidity than



<P>ever, because I seemed to need it more.&nbsp; I still preserve those



<P>relics of past sufferings and experience, like pillars of witness



<P>set up in travelling through the vale of life, to mark particular



<P>occurrences.&nbsp; The footsteps are obliterated now; the face of the



<P>country may be changed; but the pillar is still there, to remind me



<P>how all things were when it was reared.&nbsp; Lest the reader should

be



<P>curious to see any of these effusions, I will favour him with one



<P>short specimen:&nbsp; cold and languid as the lines may seem, it was



<P>almost a passion of grief to which they owed their being:-

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Oh, they have robbed me of the hope



<P>My spirit held so dear;



<P>They will not let me hear that voice



<P>My soul delights to hear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They will not let me see that face



<P>I so delight to see;



<P>And they have taken all thy smiles,



<P>And all thy love from me.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Well, let them seize on all they can; -



<P>One treasure still is mine, -



<P>A heart that loves to think on thee,



<P>And feels the worth of thine.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Yes, at least, they could not deprive me of that:&nbsp; I could think

of



<P>him day and night; and I could feel that he was worthy to be



<P>thought of.&nbsp; Nobody knew him as I did; nobody could appreciate

him



<P>as I did; nobody could love him as I - could, if I might:&nbsp; but



<P>there was the evil.&nbsp; What business had I to think so much of one



<P>that never thought of me?&nbsp; Was it not foolish? was it not wrong?



<P>Yet, if I found such deep delight in thinking of him, and if I kept



<P>those thoughts to myself, and troubled no one else with them, where



<P>was the harm of it? I would ask myself.&nbsp; And such reasoning



<P>prevented me from making any sufficient effort to shake off my



<P>fetters.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But, if those thoughts brought delight, it was a painful, troubled



<P>pleasure, too near akin to anguish; and one that did me more injury



<P>than I was aware of.&nbsp; It was an indulgence that a person of more



<P>wisdom or more experience would doubtless have denied herself.&nbsp;

And



<P>yet, how dreary to turn my eyes from the contemplation of that



<P>bright object and force them to dwell on the dull, grey, desolate



<P>prospect around:&nbsp; the joyless, hopeless, solitary path that lay



<P>before me.&nbsp; It was wrong to be so joyless, so desponding; I should



<P>have made God my friend, and to do His will the pleasure and the



<P>business of my life; but faith was weak, and passion was too



<P>strong.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In this time of trouble I had two other causes of affliction.&nbsp;

The



<P>first may seem a trifle, but it cost me many a tear:&nbsp; Snap, my



<P>little dumb, rough-visaged, but bright-eyed, warm-hearted



<P>companion, the only thing I had to love me, was taken away, and



<P>delivered over to the tender mercies of the village rat-catcher, a



<P>man notorious for his brutal treatment of his canine slaves.&nbsp; The



<P>other was serious enough; my letters from home gave intimation that



<P>my father's health was worse.&nbsp; No boding fears were expressed,

but



<P>I was grown timid and despondent, and could not help fearing that



<P>some dreadful calamity awaited us there.&nbsp; I seemed to see the black



<P>clouds gathering round my native hills, and to hear the angry



<P>muttering of a storm that was about to burst, and desolate our



<P>hearth.



<P>
